The Red Page
Bruges, in Flanders
Old Memlinc —Hans, not Jan —paintec
some miniature pictures 500 years ago
which hang in a room behind St. John’
Hospital in Bruges. I say “hang”; bu
Bruges is now the German depot and base
for transferring prisoners and wounded, so
I don’t suppose much is left. It is probably
safe to say that those five Memlincs have
gone; they are easily worth half a millioi
dollars in New York. Good pictures : film
and deft and strong. Flemish kitchens
with very high lights and very low lights
babies in cradles before huge open fires
busy housewives, and quiet, dull men sit
ting at their tables in the eventide.
The run from Ostend to Bruges takes 15
minutes, in silly little coupes holding six
yet very comfortable. We pottered liwaj
from the garish twentieth-century resort,
-gambling clubs, pubs, notorious gorgeous
hotels; all built yesterday and full to-day
and to-night and to-morrow, and anon empty.
I saw old King Leopold on the Digue There
more than once, with a characteristic body-
guard of a lady-in-waiting on either .side,
the gentlemen in attendance some distance
in the rear. A bad old man, but clever and
strong: a good builder of cities and a good
director of a nation.
The train fumbles slowly into Bruges,
mediaeval, reticent and muffled, ancient and
quiet; dignified, secretive, mellow. The
cab rolls over tortuous, cobbled streets and
lanes and alleyways. The very echoes it
wakes are dim ami shady and old, rising to
join the whispered colloquies of the gabled,
red-roofed, Gothic houses —tliousand-years-
whispered colloquies. The place has fallen
in years, is imprisoned in old age.
As I look back at the city from the height
of our. bright youth and ■ strength, a deep
pity mingles with my affection. Not pity
for the fighting days she endures now. Such
days have been her portion all her life. The
tramp of hostile feet on her cobblestones
must beat film her own men’s feet, so usual
is the sound in her ears. But pity rather
for the stagnant age. Time was when she
-was rich and powerful. But silt packed
itself athwart the river’s mouth and the tall
ships and the business went to Antwerp.
And Brugge slipt into a backwater, and
became a haunt of antiquarians and artists
and writers and other half-witted folk.
In the rich years, seditions, Low Country
wars, Norman invasions, innumerable ex-
ternal and internal troubles—these and the
like kept her busy. This showman’s job
that lasted 500 years must have bored her
stiff: she must have looked back often and
wistfully to the days of blood and murder
ami sudden death. And now— Germans, of
all people!
Velasquez had a Crucifixion in the Cathe-
dral which would break your heart—the
soul of pain itself. And old Memlinc had
those fiery visions in the hospital. Even
Michael Angelo had a marble Madonna,
floating mistily in a dim aisle of Notre
Dame, an icy -guardian angel of the ungodly
and the godly. The Gruuthuuse kitchen was
furnished _ with mediaeval chairs and tables
and utensils, all authentic and some of silver
and gold. In dim Gothic rooms overhanging
the canals were gleaming Missals, decorated
and illuminated in incredible colors, the
long labor of the friars. There was lace,
miles of gossamer and cobwebs ; an effigy of
pink alabaster on a black tomb ; a Moorish
Catholic Church My memory fails me.
A Ministry of Arts kept a watchful eye.
Bruges lived by its peering tourists and visi-
tors ; would a man build a shed within the
city limits, the Ministry of Arts leaned over
his shoulder, lest the shed be not a truly
Gothic shed.
The old man with the patriarchal beard
and the shocking reputation seldom troubled
Bruges. He went through from Ostend to
his beloved Brussels, which he had disposed
in imitation of Paris. The place suited him
very well. His forcing of the vivacious
Parisian spirit on the Dutch-extracted stolid-
ity was a difficult job and noble. That
Flemish phlegm would have been retrograde
—or at best stationary—alone. I think his-
tory will forgive him the Congo, if we can’t.
HILARY LOFTING.
In the Name of the Prophet
A little book, without which no home is
complete, is Prophecy Concerning the Rosh
Keldldh , a product of local industry, now in
its third edition and with abundant hopes
of reaching its thirtieth. It is a work of
mysticism and conglomeration, which throws
new twilight on the Scripture. Inside its
62 pages are many things that are wonder-
ful, many that are beautiful but unconvinc-
ing, many that don’t seem worth while, and
a lot that fathom the depths of the soul but
are not quoted on the Stock Exchange. The
author has a streak of poetry in him. He has
devoted much study to matters of ancient learn-
ing -which would have a better show if the
ancient learning wasn’t so much eclipsed by
the high price of modern provisions; and the
work suggests that he suffers from compres-
sion, possibly caused by the increased cost
of paper. His book is a pearl of a sort cast
before swine, just when the ruling rates in
the pork market have caused swine to be
scarce and supercilious.
In the compass of 62 pages the volume
explains the disappearance of the tribe of
Dan from Holy Writ. Its case was so bad
that it wasn’t even cursed, but was obliter-
ated with silent contempt. Prophets, priests
and monarchs are glanced at, with their
causes, reasons, vocabularies, aims, clothes
and results. Brief explanations are fur-
nished of the “beasts” in Revelation, also the
“seals,” the “cherubim,” the “waters,” and
the etceteras; and the whole system of gov-
ernment is thrown in like 2h per cent, dis-
count qr as one might throw r a brown paper
parcel through the carriage window when

Made in the United States and Canada. Worn
with health and comfort by women the world over
OFFERED this season with a front so perfect
that it rivals the beauty of the world-famed

This cleverly designed front absolutely eliminates
all appearance of fat or thickness at the front and
emphasizes the delicate curve of the waistline by
accenting the curve under the bust. Only in a
Gossard can this perfect front be attained, together
with the small hips and flat back demanded by the
present mode. Gossards are the only front-lacing
corsets that completely conform to Fashion’s lines.
Be sure you buy a genuine Gossard. The name
Gossard on the inside of the corset is your guar-
antee of the original. Look for the name
Qossard
and demand the genuine.
All front-lacing corsets are not Gossards. A
Gossard is a front-lacing corset made by Gossard.
A genuine Gossard means more than a front-
lacing corset; it means emphatically a perfect front,
a smooth back, your all-day comfort, your safe-
guarded health —it means unusual wearing service
alone worth the price you may pay.
Priced at 12/6 and up
Let your refusal of a front-lacing corset bearing
other than the Gossard name be just as pronounced
as would be your refusal of counterfeit money.
It is true economy to have at least two Gossard
Corsets in your wardrobe—-you will be more com-
fortably dressed andthey will give you longer service.
That the front lacing is the superior method of
lacing is conceded by the best dressed women the
world over. Gossard Corsets are the original front-
lacing corsets and the perfect expression of the
front-lacing principles. In your search for the per-
fect corset you will finally wear a Gossard, so an-
ticipate the inevitable—buy and enjoy one now.
Some stores do not sell the genuine Gossard Cor-
sets; some palm off imitations. There is a store—
sometimes more—in every city selling the genuine
Gossard Corsets. You will know them by their
Gossard signs displayed in their window and by
their advertising Gossard Corsets.

Your Obedient Servants
That’s the relation of
Big Ben and Baby Ben
to thousands of men and
women who believe in
the economy of living
on time.
They were born to serve —to
take orders and obey— to run
and ring on time. And they do
their tasks cheerfully—do them
well.
Big Ben and Baby Ben head a family
of successful clocks—the Westclox
family from over the sea. The Westclox
mark of merit on the dial denotes a
better construction inside. Needle-fine
pivots of polished steel greatly reduce
friction in this patented method of clock
making. Westclox success is bred
in the bone.
These are clocks you’ll respect; for
they record time carefully like the
precious thing it is.
Big Ben is seven inches tall; Baby
Ben is 3£ inches. And either, or both,
will find a favored spot in your home.
Your clock dealer has them; price
each, 15/-.
Western Clock Co. —makers of Westclox
La Salle, Illinois, U.S.A.

The man who has learned how and
when to take risks, banks on his
superior nerve to pull him through.
And if by daily habit and by simple
precautions he has kept his body fit
and vigorous always, you may be sure
his nerve will not fail him in any crisis.
Such men succeed where others dare
not even try to succeed.
Hr HE habit of taking
* a half teaspoon"
ful of Kruschen Salts
in a tumbler of hoc
water every morning
before rising clears the
head, invigorates and
cleanses the system
and promotes daily good health. You sleep
better —and rise refreshed ; you eat better —and
assimilate all the nourishment that food can give;
you work better —and enjoy your work. You
can tax your energies to the uttermost and take
pleasure in the effort if only you keep your body fit
while your brain works. The Kruschen habit makes you fit and
keeps you fit—good health becomes an unconscious habit of life.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
East and West.
Red gold is the setting- sun,
Red gold is the rising moon,
And in their glow and gleam
We two must end our dream
Too soon, and all too soon!
Red gold is the setting sun,
Red gold is the rising moon!
If I go east to the moon,
And you go west to the sun,
We sure shall meet anon,
When many nights have gone
And many days are done;
If I go east to the moon,
And you go west to the sun.
The West shall call the Bast,
And the East shall call the West:
And worlds are- not too wide
When Love, with giant-stride,
Puts forth upon his quest;
The West shall call the East,
And the East shall call the West!
But why go east to the moon,
And why go west to the sun,
Since here and now we meet,
Since love and life are sweet,
Since heart and heart are one?
Then why go east to the moon.
And why go west to the sun?
Our parting bliss forbids;
It mav not be, Dear Heart!
Por what is all the world
And all its flowers, dew-pearled,
When lovers sigh and part?
Our parting Love forbids,
It cannot be, Dear Heart!
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
At Dawn.
Oh, the joy of the clattering hoofs
And the wind rushing by;
The dark ribboned road beneath,
O’erhead a grey sky,
And the green to right and the green to left
And the sheen -where the rivulets lie!
o’er a thundering bridge,
And a river dawn-kissed,
A song to the heart’s quickened beat
Comes out of the mist,
And the chinking soft and the chinking high
Of the bit to the turning wrist.
Oh. the joy of the deep thudding hoofs
O’er a dew-starred plain,
And the leap o’er a clinking creek
To the Load again,
And the plunge to right and the plunge to left
To the curb of the tightening rein!
Speed borne on the breath of the wind
That is scenting the morn,
Unfurling a flag of gold
As the sun is born,
So, light to the East and dusk to the West,
The day comes astride of the dawn.
WEST RALIA.
ANTONIUS H.
Operation.
Under the kindly hypodermic’s reign
The corridors swung on a sea of sleep,
While from the corners hot-eyed ghosts of
pain
Strove at the leash, or broke away to creep
Gibbering towards a door. All starry-eyed
The morning peeped in at the casement wide.
Down a small flight of steps, across a walk
Where breezes stirred, sweet as the breath
of grace,
And a red rose a-tiptoe on its stalk
Thrust through a window a still dewy face—
Then on a wave of air fraught with the
scent
Of ether through the high arched door I
went.
Down long dim terraces of sleep I slipped,
Sighing with peace to rest my fill at last:
Honey of ease cloyed all the air and dripped
Balm upon limbs that knew their tiredness
past,
While from the shadows Death drew greyly
by
For the last whisper of the last faint sigh.
Then through the room came the quick
tramp of feet,
And straightway to the table closely pressed
A form insistent, eager, bent to greet,
A rose gleamed red against his tunic breast.
“I die that she may live,” to Death he said.
And the rose gaped apart and thickly bled.
Sweetly, insistently, upon the high grey
pane,
A red rose tiptoe heat a petalled rain.
The doctor lifted up his tired head,
“Death beaten by a bare half-length.” he
said.
N.S.W.
NORA McAULIFFE.
Our Home.
I dreaded lest no house a home could seem
Where you had never been and ne’er could
be ;
So when I left our home all silently
(Because I dared not speak) there seemed
no gleam
Of hope for me; the past was like a dream
Merging into the land of shadows; we
Must henceforth live apart eternally,
I working here, you somewhere in God’s
scheme.
But now I find my home is yours; your
chair,
The books you loved, your pipe, the shelves
you made
All seem a part of you, and bring you there,
So that my mem’ry of you cannot fade.
Your very voice seems ringing in the air;
I almost see you in the twilight shade.
VICTORIA.
A. B. C.

THURSDAY, JUNE 20, 1918.
IS AUSTRALIA ON ANOTHER
PLANET?
Sydney Worker, assuring Australia that it
has nothing to be afraid of, commits itself to
these theories and doctrines: —
(1) Australia is practically invulnerable.
(2) No “elaborate system” of defence is necessary.
(8) Wars are nowadays waged only for the pos-
session or exploitation of fertile and undeveloped
territories inhabited by inferior races.
(4) There is nothing to fear from the wanton
aggression of other countries.
(5) A citizen army is not required, it having
been estimated by Kitchener that 80,000 men would
be ample to defend the Commonwealth from invasion.
(6) There is no instance on record of a country
like Australia, isolated from its nearest neighbors by
wide expanses of water, held by a civilised and high-
spirited people, being forcibly seized by • another
people. It simply cannot be done. It is both physi-
cally and morally impossible.
No attempt is made by the Worker to show
why this collection of pragmatic assertions
should be swallowed at one gulp. The state-
ments are just thrown down with a splendid
gesture of finality and an intimation that
unless the Australian toiler refuses to con-
sume them he will be made the victim of
Capitalism and Militarism. The gist of it all
is that nobody can lay violent hands on Aus-
tralia because of some inherent local invul-
nerability. What this invulnerability con-
sists of is left to the imagination. The
Worker may know what it is. The Bul-
letin does not. and pending information on
the point believes it to be a mere rumor.
Assuming that a fleet of warships arrived off
the Australian coast, and there were no war-
ships to meet them, and, further, that they
were accompanied by transports carrying a
quarter of a million men, will the Worker
explain what invulnerable circumstance
would defeat the rest of the strangers’ pro-
gramme? It is no answer to say that the
warships and transports Cannot arrive.
Australia has transported 350,000 men over-
sea- Maorilanders and Afrikanders and
Canadians have been carried in large num-
bers. Uncle: Sam is ferrying his sons
across the Atlantic at the rate of about
50.000 a week. If men and warships
and transports can, under the protec-
tion of naval guns strong enough to
keep the track open, go one way over the
ocean they can go another. If instead of the
British navy being top-dog another navy
could keep the track clear, then men, muni-
tions and transports could arrive in Aus-
tralia as easily as they can now reach
France —and perhaps a great deal more
easily. There is no escape from this. Nor
is there any from the plain fact that Kitch-
ener’s 80,000 men for the defence of Aus-
tralia from invasion would, if the invader
held the sea, be a subject of jest in the comic
papers. Kitchener w-as not thinking of the
conditions Australia would have to face if
the invader had command of the sea. But
the Worker is. Therefore The Bulletin
urges it, in order to clear up the mist about
this invulnerability, to define in plain terms
what it considers Germany would see invul-
nerable about Australia if the trip to Aus-
tralia was as safe as the trip at present is
from Australia, and if the defences of the
Commonwealth were not “elaborate” or had
been wholly neglected.
Anyone who says that wars are only now
waged for possession of undeveloped terri-
tories inhabited by “inferior races” ought at
least to back up the remark with a certain
amount of evidence. The facts of the moment
seem to furnish such an overwhelming con-
tradiction that the observation looks very
like perjury. Servia was not an undeveloped
country nor were the people “inferior.” Yet
Servia has been blotted out of existence. To
speak of Poland, Lithuania and the Ukraine
as the abodes of savages would be ludicrous.
Alsace and Lorraine were not torn in ’7.1
from a France populated by cannibals. The
vast, mineral deposits of Longwy, for which
Germany is fighting- in this war as much as
for anything else, are not owned by nomads.
A Germany that was consolidated with blood
and iron is struggling for more consolidation
with the same materials, trampling over
civilised people and developed countries. It
would be a gross perversion of historical
truth to allege that wars have generally been
by “superior” people upon “inferior” races.
These have been only side-shows. The most
devastating wars the world has known have
been those carried on by peoples who each
believed they were more civilised than the
other, and the tragedy of the world is that
this still holds true. Why Australia should
have nothing to fear from wanton aggression,
when nearly all of Europe is cowering in
fear before an aggressor and the rest is
bleeding in its defence, might be understood
if the invulnerability of the Commonwealth
was madd clear. But an argument for
pacifism based on the theory that the day of
wanton _ aggression is past only sounds like
a poor joke. Those days never have passed.
The record of humanity is red with the proof.
And they never can pass while armies and
navies are maintained to stoush some
wretched victim unable to hit back effec-
tively. There is everything to fear from
wanton aggression and always has been. Can
they who promulgate with one breath the
doctrine that Australia has nothing to fear
because it is “invulnerable,” and with the
next qualify this by a proclamation that
there is no need to fear attack because wan-
ton aggression is not a possible contingency,
point to any imnortant example of a country
which, being unable to defend itself or get
protection from others, was not promptly
pounceu upon by some brutal outsider?
* * * *
It is true that no country “like Australia,”
surrounded by water, has in modern times
been “forcibly seized” en masse. It is true
because Australia is the only country like
itself. Otherwise the allegation has no mean-
ing. Water has never been a final protection
to any people or country if the other party
could float with safety. There was water
between Rome and Carthage; and a micro-
scope is wanted to find Carthage. There was
a vast expanse of water between where
Cortes came from and Mexico. There was
even more between England and Portugal
and France on the one side and India on the
other. And for purposes of forcible seizure
the ocean mattered mighty little when the
Boer Republics had to be invaded. If the
idea of the Worker is that no matter what
happens the sea is an impenetrable armor for
Australia, The Bulletin takes leave to
suggest that its cocksure friend is browsing
on delusions. Past experience, when men
.set forth to war in coracles and barques and
all sorts of tubs, and present occurrences
when floating fortresses, submarines and
high-powered steam transports are in the
picture, throw _ ridicule on the supposition.
So long as a friendly navy is on top to scare
anyone off, then Australia is not in danger of
a great invasion. The moment that navy is
not on top and another navy is available to
aid and abet an invader, Australia is open
to trouble. The opposite opinion can only be
held by refusing to look at unpleasant facts.
Invasion is a horrible thing to think of. It
is natural enough for people living in a part
of the world distant from the cult of destruc-
tion to find it difficult to imagine schemes
being hatched to blow them up. But that
does not prevent the schemers getting to
work. Nor will it, until mankind arrives at
a new stage of thought and purges the world
of military ambitions and economic jealousies.
Human society is a long way from that stage
yet, and men must deal with things as
they are instead of fumbling round in a world
of dreams. It would be a relief almost
beyond expression in words if war could be
banished from the earth. Dread of the
hideous thing has blighted the human race
since the dawn of time. But it has always
been dread of a reality. If the German wins
this war, then as sure as day will dawn
to-morrow Australia within the lifetime of
men now living will be put to the test. Force
will stand enthroned as the sole measure of
Right and Justice in the World’s affairs. And
it is the bitter truth that before the world-
mastery of Germany the vision of Australian
unity and independence would crumple like
an egg-shell under a boot-heel. The Bul-
letin wishes it could be as certain that the
people who talk about the inherent invulner-
ability of the Commonwealth and about
modern Militarism’s guiltlessness of wanton
aggression believe a word of their poisonous
doctrine.
THE TWO DROMIOS WHO DON’T
RESEMBLE MUCH.
Australia and Canada have certain points
of resemblance. They are somewhere near the
same size; they are parts of the same
empire; and they are the two biggest isolated
fragments of empire. In the last respect
there has never been any fragment exactly
like them since Brazil cut the painter and set
up for itself. Also there is a certain simi-
larity of language. But about there the
differences begin, and within the last two or
three years they have been accentuated. Long
before the war opened the Canadian was sus-
pected of materialism. He was more addicted
to dollars than to theories. His prompt adop-
tion of compulsory service was part of his
materialism, and showed how impervious he
was to the finer shades of the doctrine of
liberty. He reasoned coarsely that men must
go to the war, and that there was nothing in
the voluntary principle, under which good
men went to the cannon’s mouth while Hun
agents, spies and supporters, anarchists,
I.W.W. men, crazy pacifists, cowards, time-
servers, shirkers, chronic office-seekers, pro-
fiteers and the like stayed behind and
snatched at the control of affairs. His policy
would have sent Judd and politician Bren-
nan and preacher Roseby to the trenches
just as cheerfully as it sent Carmichael,
supposing the last-named required sending.
But the Canadian went further. He has
long been troubled with the idea that the first
business of an artisan of any kind is to do
more work and better work than the
fellow in another country ; to do it with less
effort by reason of better machinery and more
intelligenceand to be adequately paid for it,
He knew that war was a time of high prices
and great scarcity of goods, and that the
world would, as in previous big wars, be
divided into two classes—the cormorant who
collected the high prices and the worm who
paid them. He resolved unanimously to be
the cormorant. The last few years are com-
monly described as the time of “The Cana-
dian Revolution,” and during that time the
Dominion’s production of goods increased so
fast that its exports boomed in this fashion:
Canadian Exports.
BEFORE THE WAR. I IN WAR YEARS.
1912 .. £70,105,500 1915 ..£125,895,500
1913 .. 89,424,000 1916 .. 223,800,000
1914 .. 77,754,000 | 1917 .. 317,223,000
In 1915 the country sold abroad so much
more goods than it bought abroad that it had
£33,304,500 to collect from its outside cus-
tomers. In 1916 it had £66,622,000, and in 1917
£111,170,000 to collect. Blushless and quite
unashamed, it made money out of the war
by sheer energy and industry and a prompt
snatch at the forelock of opportunity. It
sold £80,000,000 worth of cartridges, explo-
sives, iron and steel manufactures, vessels of
one sort and another and paper to the Allies
in a single year. Wages went up automati-
cally, and went far higher than ever before.
With so much money coming in as payment
for high-priced goods there was no need to
have Hughes and Cook and Holman and
six Agents-General and one High Commis-
sioner all begging simultaneously for loans
in London. Having learned self-reliance
under the policy, of heavy Protection, it put
up a record in its haste to make even war
pay.
* * ■ * *
There is a Scriptural saying to the general
effect that two women shall be grinding at a
mill, and one shall be taken with a bright
idea while the other shall be left in the
lurch. Australia, as the war progressed,
developed along different lines from the
Canadian. An early effort of its comic-
opera Government was a Law for the
Prevention of New Industries. It was
enacted that all new profits earned by
an industry in excess of the average profits of
the years immediately before the war should
be suspected of being war profits, and be tax-
able at 50 per cent, to begin with, and more
later on. Of course in a perfectly new indus-
try all the profits would be new profits, and
therefore war profits if the Act was con-
strued that way, so that the song-and-dance
Government could take most of the earnings
in case of success and leave the manufacturer
all the losses in case of failure. And it was
clearly explained that though, under the
miserable tariff devised largely in the impor-
ters’ interest, most new industries Were only
possible while the war restricted foreign com-
petition, yet the musical-comedy Ministry
specifically refused to alter the tariff and
assure that the bright fresh industry wouldn’t
be snuffed out by foreign competition when-
ever the war was over. In effect, the manu-
facturer asked ,a pantomime or harlequinade
Cabinet if he was expected to be simply a
warming-pan for the Hun and other outside
people, and the clown said that it might be
so. or it might not, while the pantaloon re-
plied that the matter was one for further con-
sideration. The columbine said nothing.
Naturally the number of people employed in
Australian industries diminished rather than
increased, which, by the way, was also the
case with the people employed in agriculture.
Instead of having plenty of manufactured
goods to meet all demands and a huge surplus
to sell for cash abroad, the depressed land
which runs its affairs with a Punch Govern-
ment and a Judy Opposition, is even more
dependent than before on imports, just at a
time when imports in many lines are unpro-
curable and in all lines are at famine prices.
. So Australia has drifted into a condition
in which it can hardly even build a house to
put itself in, owing to high cost and unpro-
curableness of imported materials, and in a
less degree to. high wages, multiplicity of
strikes and holidays and general break-down.
In the biggest city the amount spent on new
buildings in 1917 was less than half the
figure for the year before the war, and
owing to increased cost the amount of new
accommodation provided w r as probably less
than a quarter. In many country towns
houses are almost unprocurable. The rail-
way service, has been grievously curtailed
because rolling stock can’t be imported, and
the materials for building it here can’t be
imported either. The shifting of the wheat
surplus is more than a year in arrear for
lack of the ships we don’t build, and the job
of railroading the wheat to the ships grows
more difficult. Public works are slowing
down for want of imported loans and im-
ported rails, and, anyhow, new railways are
of little use in the absence of imported
engines to drag imported trains along them.
The Sydney tram-cars that run a shrunken
service are being painted a new color because
the old brown paint can’t be imported.
There is a difficulty about knitting socks
for’ soldiers because this great sheep country
is short of imported wool and the special
tax is lying in wait for any new man who
starts making it. The question of finding
gold to pay the foreign interest bill is be-
coming acute as anybody who remembers
when he last saw a sovereign in common
use may imagine, for this country didn’t
happen to earn 111,000,000 surplus sover-
eigns last year as Canada did by selling
munitions and other things to the Allies.
In a thousand ways the machine of our
civilisation is slowing down and tending
towards a stoppage. When the country com-
mitted itself to the policy of low tariff,
foreign borrowing, reliance on imported
goods and other people’s ships, and as few
local manufactures as possible, it decided
that when the time came to choose between
being the boss or the slave, the eater or the
eaten, the ’bus or the panting runner who
didn’t overtake the ’bus, the maker and
seller at bloated prices or the buyer and bor-
rower at bloated prices, the cormorant or
the worm, it would, in humble obedience to
the command of the foreign importers and
their papers and their politicians, assume
the wormy or ’bus-missing part. And its
absurd and acrobatic Government, instead
of realising the situation in the first, months
of the war and reversing the old policy even
at the eleventh hour, continued worming.
It worms even to this day.
* * * *
Still one branch of industry has pros-
pered. The Administration which runs
things on the lines of the two kings in “The
Gondoliers” naturally decided not to use its
Parliamentary majority to enforce the com-
pulsory service it believes in. Avoiding, as
usual, the plain and obvious front door, and
approaching its subject in disguise by the
back yard and the roof, after the manner of
the boy detective, it at last fixed up an
arrangement by which men who wish well
to Australia go to the war, and Hun sympa-
thisers and cranks of all sorts remain be-
hind to supervise public affairs and talk
insurrection with impunity. Perhaps it was
partly by reason of this fact that strikes in
Australia, measured by the number of work-
ing days lost, increased 400 per cent, be-
tween 1914 and 1917. Canada’s exports of
goods—the things it sold in return for the
othet fellow’s cash —increased 400 per cent,
in the same time. So in this percentage
there is one more point of resemblance
between the two Dromios who, in most
things, are so very unlike.
PEACE AND FREEDOM IN THE
PACIFIC.
In the national sense Australia is not really
interested at all in the islands of the Pacific.
But Australia has always been profoundly
interested in anyone else who may covet those
localities, and is so still. It is impossible
not to be interested in him. Hence the Com-
monwealth is compelled to take quite a lot of
notice of places which, except for this indivi-
dual’s yearnings, Australians would have no
concern about.
* * * *
“Hands off the Pacific!” is all very well
as a cry, but there is a practical side to the
business. If the phrase is interpreted
to mean that while other hands are to be
kept away Australian hands are to be thrust
out, and that the Commonwealth is anxious
to convert the Pacific into an Australian
lake, the subsequent trouble will certainly be
greater than The Bulletin cares to think
about. But if the idea is that restraints
upon buccaneering among the palms shall
apply with equal force to this and every
other country—that the islands shall not
become the medium through which policies
provocative of resentment and quarrels may
be followed —then there is a good case to put
forward for “Hands off!” That islands
should be mopped up by truculent Govern-
ments at a distance, and, under cover of per-
juries about their anxiety to “spread civilisa-
tion” and so on, converted into naval bases,
is something that Australia is obviously not
anxious to hear about. Such policies have
one object only, and that is to place someone
in a position of being able to use the afore-
said bases against Australian ports and ship-
ping. This is what Germany had in view
in the occupancy of Rabaul and Samoa. One
of the things to be determined by the war
now raging is whether the German is to re-
turn to those places. Unless he is beaten
he will arrive again, hungrier and stronger
in purpose than before. That is one reason
why Australia is deeply concerned about his
defeat, for to the Commonwealth a victorious
Germany with a navy cruising around Torres
Strait and the Tasman Sea would be a night-
mare of unimaginable horror. The “Hands
off!” doctrine would then be enunciated in
another language. But if by the grace of
Providence and the use of bombs the Ger-
man, lusting for territorial expansion and
fight, does not arrive in the Pacific, there
will still remain strong reasons why
“Hands off!” should get a hearing. Aus-
tralia is not the only country interested in
the Pacific being a peaceful sea. The United
States, Japan and China use the ocean, and
though most of the islands of the Pacific are
close to Australia on the chart, they are not
far from San Francisco and Yokohama.
* * * *
The interests of Australia, of the native
population of the islands and of the world
would be best served, then, by a League of
Nations guaranteeing the Pacific freedom
from filibusters and territorial monopolists.
This implies leaving the island peoples alone
to choose their own way of life and, inci-
dentally, of trade and all the rest of it.
Australia, for her part, has no more moral
right to go seizing, say, Fiji, or Maoriland to
appropriate Samoa, than any other country
has. To do so is at best merely an incite-
ment to someone else to grab another place
and the preliminary step to getting tangled
up in disputation with other parties who feel
they ought to be in the grab as well. At
worst it means Australia officially tolerating
the exploitation of brown brother and his


land and his labor, and neglecting the immense
job the Commonwealth has on its own pre-
mises. All this talk about the islands otter-
ing magnificent openings for “development
by Australian enterprise and there being
opportunities for stupendous trade differs
neither in tone nor in substance from the talk
of every brigand the world has heard of since
Naboth’s vineyard attracted attention. It
is, no doubt, true that certain interests could
grow fat by setting up in business on an
island or two, importing coolies or inducing
the Government in the rear to so tax the
local inhabitant that he must take a job.
But that is no reason why such business
should escape being called by its proper name
or why people who want to see a fair deal
all round .should have anything to do with it.
Otherwise what possible ground of quarrel
could anyone have with German ideals, or
what sympathy with the conquered and the
oppressed anywhere? In any case there is
plenty of room in Australia for all the
energy of the population, and far more need
for Australians to concentrate on the trade
and development of the Commonwealth than
to shirk that part of the national programme
and rush off somewhere else. The islanders
have rights as definite as our own; and
unless Australia is ready to recognise this
and to tesist being stampeded into steals on
behalf of special interests, the noise about
“Hands off!” will not be received with much
applause elsewhere.
Of course if Germany wins the war the
League of Nations idea will go up in the air
as far as Australia is concerned. Another
League will transpire, and it will wear a
spiked helmet. In the meantime “Hands
off!” has the germ of a policy in which Aus-
tralia is vitally interested; but let it be
thoroughly and frankly understood that
“Hands off!” does not mean “Hands on!”
for us or any other PoAver.
A PARTY TO DESTROY PARTY.
It was, of course, the hope of parties calling
themselves Win-the-War and National that
the returned soldier would be blind to the
political trickery and scheming for office
which lay behind these high-sounding titles,
and that he would in a dull German fashion
follow those who called themselves his leaders
to the poll or any other place they might
desire to bring him. But the soldier isn’t
like that. He looks the facts straight in
the face and sees that both Win-the-War and
Labor are cheating the country out of
needed legislative work by the folly of party
strife. For one moment in one State the
alleged National party did secure the support
of some young soldiers who stood in its
interests at a recent poll; but already the
foUy of that move is recognised by the great
body of returned men, and a new Soldiers’
and Citizens’ party sets forth its desire ‘’to
keep the soldier sentiment from being ex-
ploited by any party at present in existence
by co-operating with patriotic citizens to use
that sentiment of self-sacrifice foi* the
development and protection of Australia.”
This is solid ground to stand upon, and
ground of big hope for the CompKinwealth.
Such a party, bent on the destruction of
party, was bound to come ; and that it should
rise among the soldiers themselves at the
moment when the Labor party is most care-
fully and enthusiastically destroying itself,
and the alleged National party is a flat
fatuity without purpose beyond eating the
bread of office, is all very much to the good.
* * * *
The first proposal on the new party’s
political programme is Proportional Bepre-
sentation. Proportional representation is a
means of getting accurately at all the views
of the democracy by giving a voice to minori-
ties. But that alone will not demolish
the party curse. In Tasmania a similar
scheme has worked out in endless political
deadlocks, proving that as between the
claims of Tweedledum and Tweedledee
the public mind is about evenly divided.
What the soldier democracy which aims at
getting the very best possible deal for this
continent should strive for is not a perfectly
just and equitable choice between Tweedle-
dum and Tweedledee every three years, but
a perfectly just and equitable choice between
right and wrong at any and every moment
of the Commonwealth’s existence. This is
to be secured not by proportional represen-
tation nor any other sort of representation,
but by the direct vote of the people upon the
questions concerned, added to a system of
Elective Ministries. With the popular initi-
ative of questions to be submitted to the
referendum, party, as we now know it,
could not exist. There would be fresh
groupings of politicians on every question
submitted; and no refuge would remain at
all for the mere trickster and office-seeker.
The business of the advocate of refoimi
would be to persuade the people by plain
argument. He could create no combine in
restraint of good sense by any sort of device.
This is the real direction in which the re-
turned soldiers might effectively clean up
the mess we call politics.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Bolshevik Bank, Unlimited.
There are some wonderfully original finan-
ciers abroad these days. For instance,
Catts, M.H.R. of N. S. Wales, is at large
and representing a big Ma State constituency
by a solid majority. And this is an airy
trifle which Catts, M.H.R.. wants to have
embedded in Labor’s Parliamentary pro-
gramme :
(1) That the Commonwealth Bank be a bank of
issue, deposit, exchange and reserve.
(2) That all private banks and companies be
compelled to deposit their reserves in the Common-
wealth Bank.
(3) That the Commonwealth Bank act as clearing-
house for all Australian war credits.
(4) That all war stocks and bonds be consolidated
and redeemed by the Commonwealth Bank at a
regular rate of, say, £40,000,000 a year.
The Commonwealth Bank is, of course, a
bank of exchange, deposit and reserve
already. Every bank is or tries to be. As
for making it a bank of issue, it and all
the other banks are already issuing Federal
Government notes in any quantity to every-
body who wants money and can show an
honest and solvent reason why he should
have it. Unless the Commonwealth Bank
is to start an issue of its own in competition
with its proprietor, the Commonwealth it-
self, it is hard to discover what is meant.
And what would be gained by having two
kinds of notes issued by one authority on
one security-—the public credit —is also hard
to discover. It would certainly cost a lot
of extra printing. The idea of making all
“private” banks and companies deposit their
“reserves” in the Commonwealth Bank is a
large matter. If reserve funds are meant
they are invested in all sorts of things
besides cash —buildings, mortgages, lands,
discounted bills, loans with or without
security, advances to build houses, advances
on crops, stock-in-trade of all sorts, ships,
wharves, tanks, cattle, horses, sheep and
other things too big to go into the bank door-
way. They can’t be kept in coin* for there
isn’t enough coin tb keep them in. So a
concern like the Adelaide S.S. Co. would
have to deposit a couple of ships on the
counter, and other concerns would have to
lay a plantation or a sheep-farm in Denison
Miller’s extended hands. If they were
expected to lodge the value of their reserve
funds in money there would either be a
frightful sacrifice at forced sale, with hun-
dreds of concerns selling at once, or a
frightful borrowing at the kind of rates
which prevail when hundreds of concerns
are driven to borrow simultaneously and
buyers are non-existent. If only cash re-
serves are included in Catts’s programme
things would be different. Even very wealthy
trading concerns have no cash reserves
except the change in the cash-box; their
liquid assets are at current account in the
banks, and while they are there form part of
the banks’ cash reserves. Supposing, then, all
banks were required to hand over their cash
reserves to the Commonwealth institution
no great harm would result except that,
whenever- a customer presented a cheque for
wages or anything else, the manager would
dance a doleful jig and say that there was
no money, for Denison Miller had it all.
Whereupon wages would cease and industry
be suspended. The Commonwealth banker,
as a result of all this, would have on his
hands about £20,000,000 in gold, some silver
and copper, and more paper money than was
ever seen in one heap before. He couldn’t
earn interest on all this, for business would
be partly dead and partly a raving lunatic,
and he couldn’t simply steal the money, giv-
ing nothing in return, for if he did he would
lose his self-respect and never be able to
look his female relations in the face again.
Then an immense proportion of the war
debt is payable in gold only, and how to
wipe out £40,000,000 a year and the interest
with only £20,000,000 in all has not yet been
discovered. The debts might perhaps be
“consolidated” at 41 per cent, by making a
present of 1 per cent, to the holder of low-
priced securities and prigging 1 per cent,
from the holder of high-priced ones, where-
upon one would say that the Commonwealth
was a fool and the other that it was a high-
way robber. And the debt doesn't really
require to be consolidated any more than a
rainbow requires to be soled and heeled ; all
that is necessary is to pay off the liabilities
as they _ fall due. One way and another
Catts is suggestive of the balance-sheets
that St. John dreamt of on the isle of Pat-
mos. And, incidentally, his programme is
almost to a dot that with which the two
awful wild Catts of Russia. Trotsky and
Lenin, reduced the whole financial system
of their country to beggary and hash.

Beeby, Pecksniff and Co.
It causes a pain in that lucid interval
between the end of the waistcoat and the
beginning of the pants to read the statement
of N. S. Wales Minister Beeby re the high
nrices of meat, etc.: —
The only direction in which the Legislature can
move in matters of this kind is by cheapening tran-
sit, creating central markets in the metropolitan area,
and in other ways trying to bring the producer and
consumer closer together.
Well, and what have the politicians done?
There was _an attempt to create an Aus-
tralian capital on the Snowy River on the
ground that that fast stream represented the
most reliable mass of water-power in the
State, and by reason of its fitness for cheap
manufacturing it would easily draw a large
population right alongside a large meat sup-
ply, and, incidentally, create a splendid asset
in the shane of a big city of which the people
could be sole ground landlords. This magnifi-
cent scheme of land settlement, decentralisa-
tion and Public Enterprise was squashed by
the furious opposition of N. S. Wales politi-
cians, because it might diminish that rush
of people to Sydney which is half the cause of
the meat problem. Then it was mentioned
that there was a splendid harbor at Port
Stephens, and if the North Coast railway
touched there a city of some sort, with the
State as sole ground landlord, couldn’t help
arising unless it was forcibly prevented
by the faking of railway rates and the
denial of a few wharves. That would have
brought another lot of consumers closer to the
producer, and incidentally it might have made
tie North Coast railway pay. Naturally a
gang of blatant hypocrites wasted the public
funds on taking the railway round Port
Stephens in fear the rush of people to Syd-
ney might be checked. Sane people grow
weary of listening to the song of the Peck-
sniff bird about shortening the distance
between the producer and the consumer when
all the time the country is being plundered
and swindled to keep them apart.

If Only the Kaiser Had Grown
Cabbages!
“George Street” preaches the gospel of
Every Man his Own Greengrocer:—
The ancient and wicked gag that war is a neces-
sary evil because it keeps population down to a
reasonable level is often voiced these days. Malthus,
at the end of the eighteenth century, said some wild
things about the dangerous increase of population;
and now Knibbs, Commonwealth Statistician, devotes
a book to the business of proving that the world will
soon have too many people. Knibbs is more moderate
than Malthus ; but his estimate that the numbers
double every 60 years is still very hard to accept.
On such a theory small, healthy island populations,
which have been little affected by disease and not at
all by war, should during the centuries have so in-
creased that there would have hardly been standing
room for a blade of grass between their boots ; but a
very slight emigration to larger lands has maintained
the freedom of the garden patch from being tram-
pled under, and has even left room for the goat
and cow.
Granted, however, that with disease abolished,
infants properly eared for, the span of life extended
by attention to the rules of health and a reduction
of the pressure of life at every point, the natural
increase of nations would be very rapid, why
fear that there would be no room for that larger
world-population? By plain demonstration it can be
shown that even in temperate climates where crops
are long in coming to maturity one acre of closely-
cultivated land will perpetually maintain—even
allowing for occasional failures —a family of four
persons. In the tropics and sub-tropics still less
ground is required ; but taking four to the acre of
rich soil as a basis, 425 million acres could be made
to maintain the whole present race of men. The
single State of Brazil, in the watershed of the
Amazon alone, could supply this extent of rich soil.
So the danger of overcrowding the planet is not
exactly pressing.
Australia, with its handful of people scattered
about the south-eastern corner, is certainly in no
immediate fear of having too many people to feed.
Without any exaggeration, the whole white popula-
tion of the earth could find homes and comfort in
this continent; and there would still be room for
generations to come without resorting to the mad-
ness of war or the aid of hideous diseases to thin out
the ranks.
On mere economic grounds, war is a most extrava-
gant mode of securing standing room; and the time
devoted to hammering cannon and making explosives
would be better spent in pounding the rocky moun-
tain side into fertility or carrying water to the
desert. For neither war nor disease make a good
choice in their selection of victims. The unfit from
their point of view may be the fittest in the realm
of intellect; and it is by the brain rather than the
boot that mankind must continue its march to greater
things.
Further, it is not by any means proved that war
does seriously reduce the population. As if by way
of rebuke to the destroyers, Nature always seems to
make up the deficit of human life with astonishing
rapidity.
Still, the point raised by Malthus, and now
emphasised by Knibbs, has some importance in view
of the world distribution of foodstuffs. The food is
here, the growing multitudes can be fed; but with
the increase of cities and the calamitous massing of
human creatures into restricted areas famine is a
real and ever-present menace. The recognition of
one great principle alone can save humanity. The
people must get near to the soil and keep near to
the soil. So deep and tremendous is this need that
it becomes a religious and moral question rather
than a merely political one. Old Thomas Carlyle
declared that to know anything at all about a sub-
ject a man must be morally related to it. No man
is morally related to his daily bread who does not
know where it comes from and how to grow it. The
relation between food and thought is a very inti-
mate one; and the divorce between the people and
the soil must in the end be fatal to any nation.
Revolutionary as such a proposal might seem, and
ridiculous as it might be held by the pseudo-
economists of to-day, the real world-reform which is
needed most of all is the abolition of trade in food-
stuffs. Let it be added to the Ten Commandments —
and not added at the bottom, but at the top of the
list—that every man as a religious duty should by
his own labor grow his own food. That would
occupy probably three hours a day of his time;
the rest of the day he could if he pleased build
houses or work in a factory or write poetry; but
the reform would make him a man and the nation
to which he belonged a nation of men.
Further, it would settle forever the restlessness
which creates wars. The Kaiser with good honest
sweat on his brow, as he plied the hoe among the
beans that were to feed the princes of the house of
llohenzollern, would never have dreamt his dream
of blood. There is a peace in growing cabbages that
passeth understanding. And such world-reform would
adjust population always and under all circum-
stances to the food supply. John Bright showed
that the price of the loaf affected from year to year
tiie number of marriages in Britain. How much
more would it be affected by the condition of the
garden patch and the extent to which the woman
took up the forgotten arts of real domestic economy!
Marriage reformers insist that no man who is
unfit for war should marry. It is a poor test and
Nature is ready to provide a better. Let no man
marry who is unfit to raise the food of a family
from the free soil. Get it down to that, and the
world will have nothing to fear.

The Only Valuable Baby.
Dear Bulletin, —I know of nothing more
calculated to make a decent person feel as if
he had eaten too much bad dinner than these
people—very many of them parsons—who
clamor for more birth-rate. There are two
aspects of the case. First, there is the
effective birth-rate, which consists in having a
reasonable number of healthy children and
keeping them, alive and training them to be
good citizens and citizenesses, so that
there may be a steady increase of good popu-
lation in those countries which have space
and food for more population. In this re-
spect Australia has about the best record
in the world, and the record is still improv-
ing. Then fheye is just the birth-rate—the
production of the largest possible number of
children, regardless of what comes after.
And it is just the birth-rate that these par-
sons and their kind are always clamoring
for. The races which do best in this way
are the beastly races. They may be high
mentally or they may not. but morally and
spiritually (if the term may be allowed)
they are low. It is because the Hun,
especially the Prussian Hun, has all the crude
birth-rate qualifications that this preacher
class is so anxious about that he makes rape
a science in every country that he invades.
He has more of the animal qualifications
for birth-rating than the Briton, the
Irishman, the Frenchman, Greek, Italian,
Spaniard or Portuguese and their North
and South American offshoots. Next to
the Hun comes the Russian, who is also
a backward person. The West European
has done hideous slaughters in war-time
when he was seeing red, but the worst stories
in his history have concerned Hun contin-
gents serving in his armies. It is because
the Hun is so great on the birth-rate that
ladies in England are toiling on the manufac-
ture of innumerable baby-clothes for the
compulsory infants of French and Belgian
women and girls (nuns included). Augus-
tus the Strong of Saxony, with his 400
offspring, is a typical Hun hero to this day.
Also there is a Hun legend that the
Guelpiis started with a lady who had 22 or
more infants at a birth. Running abreast of
the Hun is the African—not the Egyptian,
who is a person by himself, nor the Arab,
who is a comparatively gentlemanly intruder
from Asia, but the plain nigger. His crazy
lust for women —especially white women —is
proverbial. Some of the peoples with vast
and hog-like birth-rates show very little in-
crease of population because the death-rates
are also exorbitant, but that isn’t the mat-
ter at issue. What our impious folk demand
is just births —stacks of births like litters of
young pigs.—Yours in anger and contempt,
Henry iioksecollar.

Bash.
The fact that the industrial side of Labor
in N. S. Wales, by a majority of its del-
egates, has committed itself to resolutions
which mean opposition to recruiting, aban-
donment of its own army and repudiation
of the attitude of British Labor on the war,
is, after all, only one sign of great changes.
And it is not the most striking sign either.
Far more important to the Australian
democracy is the evidence given during the
last big strike, and since, that Labor is
getting on to a platform of one plank. That
plank is branded “Bash.” No doubt there
is a lot to be said for Bash, so long as there
is something behind it. The bashing of
sweaters and profiteers and loafers is holy
work. But indiscriminate Bash is another
thing, and it is this that Labor is getting
tangled up with. Labor came into being as
a critical and constructive body, with prin-
ciples which made it the most suggestive
force in public life. It aimed at securing
the democratic control of affairs and the
administration thereof in the public interest
—at orderly coherent reform in the interests
of social justice. Now it is getting to the
stage of saying: “We have been going on
the wrong lines. Let us have a class war.
Let us upend the whole caboose and by
making chaos make progress.” This is the
gospel of despairing futility. It is the denial
of democracy. Jt means plunging into
Reaction. If persisted in the immediate
consequence must be to turn every Labor
candidate for Parliament into either a liar
or an anarchist.
“Direct Action.”
It is foolish to be led away by phrases; much
better to get down to definitions. For in-
sance, that “Direct Action” about which
so much is heard these days is Plain Murder
in the long run. The people who killed Dom
Carlos of Portugal and his eldest son, and
tried to kill the old Don’s wife, alluded to
the event as Direct Action. And the term
has done service as regards every other pro-
minent slaying of recent years. It means
taking the management of things, by means
not provided for in the Constitution, from
Somebody and giving it to Somebody Else.
There must be a show of force to overawe
Somebody. If he won’t be overawed but
resists, there must be fighting and killing,
perhaps a great deal of killing; and there are
side-butcheries which aren’t in the pro-
gramme, for the hoodlums who care nothing
for either cause take the chance for private
loot and rape and revenge. Direct Action
has been used to overset the rule of an auto-
crat, an oligarchy or a church. Then the few
were sacrificed in the hope of benefiting the
many. In Australia the rule is in the hands,
not of the few, nor even of the many, but of
everybody. The principle of one-adult-one-
vote is the last word in popular government
There can be nothing beyond it unless every-
body gets two votes instead of one, or unless
the franchise is extended to infants and
beasts. So Direct Action must be the rising
of the few, the only people who can rise, to
take control from the many, the only people
who can be risen against. It probably means
an awful amount of murder, for the many
who toiled so long to secure absolute control
of their own affairs will object bitterly
when the leaders of a raving minority start
to make themselves a coterie of dictators. If
Direct Action isn’t merely the froth of a
handful of overgrown boys playing a game of
tin soldiers, then it signifies bloodshed for cer-
tain. There is nothing else that it can sig-
nify. And it takes a terrible amount of blood-
shed to suppress a majority fighting in the
cause of freedom.
Hang Work!
Who didn’t feel suddenly like saying, “Hang work f
Hang pettiness! The war’s the thing.”— Sydney
Tel eg n a i'h (11/6/’IS) in a spasm of enthusiasm
about the march of departing soldiers through Sydney.
The paper’s intentions were good, but some
people are inclined to think that work has
been too much hung already. As a case in
point, it was reported the same day that the
shearers had been ordered to strike at Bloom-
field (Blackall district, Queensland), and
though they didn’t know that they were dis-
satisfied and hadn’t been informed what the
trouble was about, yet, not being free agents,
they had struck. The report added that a
much bigger strike is supposed to be pre-
paring for August, and the activity of
I.W.W. agents is regarded as evidence. So
in the name of freedom the worker is ordered
France.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Should Paris fall before the scourge of
shame
That marks green Europe with the stripes of
blood,
Not yet shall sink that high and holy flame
That shone on hope uplifted o’er the flood
Of Revolution drowning wrongs and thrones.
France shall remain—the France of splendid
dreams —
Not wrought in iron bars and carven stones,
But as a garden of the soul whose streams
Lap flowery banks of thought and sacrifice.
Oh, France is grander than the voice of guns.
More potent than all empires, though the
price
Of glory be the blood of half her sons!
And when the ruin and red lust shall cease,
Freedom and France shall be twin names
for Peace.

Wfnler Garden
about ns if he was a subject <>f the Czar in
the days when Czars were in full bloom. It
is not his to reason why; if he doesn’t obey,
he is labelled a “scab” and a traitor and a
person who is worse than a friend of the
linn. The latter flourishes almost openly
and never gets anything worse than mild
reproof; but the man who disobeys the com-
mand to leave off work is anathema. Nowa-
days the I.W.W. or pro-Hun minority has,
by organisation, push and effrontery, gained
a big hold on the Labor machine, and it
begins to look very much like a case of the
pro-Hun, with the arrogance of a Prussian
junker, ordering his servant, the free Aus-
tralian, to stop production so as to weaken
and paralyse and disorganise this large sec-
tion of the British Empire in the interests
of Germany. Taking all things into account,
then, this is no time for a bright and cheery
non-Hun paper to “hang work.” No country
is likely to win a war by working imported
and often rather antiquated machinery 44
hours a week, with many holidays and many
strikes and an occasional “go slow” policy
thrown in; in fact even before the war Aus-
tralia wasn’t earning its own board and
lodging and expenses, so that whenever the
influx of foreign loans slowed down seriously
distress set in. Now the departure of many
workers for the Front, and the greatly in-
creased striking habits of those who remain,
and the lack of new imported machinery,
have made things worse than ever. If Syd-
ney Telegraph, in a joyous moment,
“hangs” the little industry that is left it is
proposing suicide.
The Negotiations in the Shambles.
After his big push from St. Quentin
towards Amiens the German quickly switched
on the tornado in a fresh locality—from
Givinchey northwards to Dixmude. While it
lasted the damage was considerable. After
the rush, and the enemy had got his second
wind, he surged across the Chemin ties
Dames, got punched in the vicinity of Rheims,
pushed down to the Marne, and facing west-
ward seriously mauled the French army
along the front running to Soissons. Held up
fin this locality by a reinforced defence, he
promptly started operations in the next sec-
tor with the object of crossing the Aisne and
beating Foch out of the salient running up
to Noyon. After terrific fighting the Allies
were able to say that the move had been
checked. All the same, the enemy gained
some advantages, which, though limited,
widen his elbow room. The net result of the
four operations up to the beginning of the
week was that the German liad (1) drawn
nearer to the sea, (2) moved closer to Paris,
and (3) lengthened the fighting line for both
sides. Each of these is less pleasant for the
Allies than for the enemy. It is explained that
Ludendorff and co. are disappointed with
the rate of progress, and that the enemy lias
suffered losses out of all proportion to his
gains. This may be true. Also it may just
be. a convenient yarn. Certainly the same
things were said by the Germans about the
French advance in the Champagne and
Haig’s progress on the Somme and at Pas-
schendaele ; and most likely with equal truth.
Exactly what Ludendorff has on his time-
table can at best be merely a matter of
speculation. As for liis losses —well, neither
side goes into much detail. The main ques-
tion at the time of writing was in relation
to where the German would hit next. A spot
considered likely to be assaulted any day is
the valley of the Somme, towards Amiens.
Prince Ruppreciit is said to have an enor-
mous force thereabouts, and the inducement
to the force to strike seems great, for it is
not. much use trying to make out that Haig
is in a particularly comfortable position
there. What is very probable though is that
Haig has a bigger force thau before, and as
with one calamitous exception the British
divisions have taken a bloody toll when the
German attacked, and stood fast on their
ground, the enemy may not consider the
time to be yet ripe for trying to reach the
water. The probabilities are that he is more
anxious to get within sight of Paris, and,
for the present, keener on bleeding the
French than doing anything else. There is
a terrible and understandable purpose in this.
Meanwhile by way of variety the Austrians
lately launched a battering-ram at the
Italian Front, where they appear to have
been hit back hard. At the same time there
are stories about the German navy being on
the verge of taking a trip, and of accumulat-
ing chaos in Austria. After four years’
repetition these speculations are becoming
a little familiar. Much more to the point
are the reports about continued arrivals of
A merican transports.
Red Tape.
“Reggie Rueberstamp” tells how it works:
It’s time we took off our coats and centralised or
co-ordinated some of our Government departments.
Take as an example one sub-department I know of.
One. Federal department pays the office rent, one
pays the electric-light accounts, another pays what
we will call “supplies,” and so it goes on until it
takes ju»t seven, departments and sub-departments in
Sydney and Melbourne to deal with the financial
side of a small, non-spending sub-department. The
employees are, paid their wages from at least four
different places. Then take the question of, sav,
office furnishings, involving an expenditure of £o.
A requisition is signed by A and forwarded to B,
who, after about four weeks to consider, replies to
A requesting (“owing to desire for war economy”)
that the matter be reconsidered. A replies that
the job is an absolute necessity, and B then rings
up various contractors, who swarm all over A’s
office preparing a quote which is forwarded to B.
B approves of one particular quote and sends the
approval on to C (his chief) in Melbourne. C sits
on it for a few more weeks, then consults D (chief
of A’s department), who, in 99 cases out of 100,
writes A for a report as to necessity. Eventually,
after probably four or five months have elapsed, the
machine clicks back approvingly from D to A. But
then A finds the Minister’s signature is required, and
•so back to Dgo the whole sheaf of papers. At
last all is ready and the contractors sent for. But
as six months have elapsed since the quote was
given, the contractor (on account “of the increase
in prices”) cannot do the job now under £7 10s.
And so it goes on. The awful circumlocution con-
tinues because, whenever a commission or any other
old advisory crowd of business men is appointed, the
wrong people are invariably called before it to
give evidence. Instead of calling on the clerks
and other pallbearers of the show, it is the chief
corpse who, in all cases, tells the commission that
the funeral is going along beautifully and no reforms
are needed. Take the present Commission on the
Defence and Naval Departments. A friend of the
writer has seen that Commission take evidence in
a couple of places it has visited and he hasn’t heard
of any but the heads (the very men responsible for
the existing state of affairs) called before it.

Is the Devil in Australian Timber?
The question of the use of Australian tim-
bers for Australian purposes requires looking
into. From time to time there lias been an
outcry about the destruction of trees to
clear the way for cows, and once a month, at
least, someone declares that the stuff going-
up in smoke is better for most purposes than
anything else in the world. Yet certain coun-
try towns are in despair because it is im-
possible to find material to build houses
except at a prohibitive cost. Also, when the
specifications for certain composite vessels to
be constructed in Australia were recently
examined it was discovered that foreign tim-
ber was prescribed for all but a few odds
and ends. Further, a. protest has just been
made to the Maoriland Government by Aus-
tralian timber merchants against a new order
forbidding the export of white pine and other
woods. “They state,” the report runs, “that
it is utterly impossible to supply from avail-
able Australian timber the necessary quan-
tity for box-making, let alone the "require-
ments of the building trade.” Lately, too, it
was stated that Australian forests provided
nothing that was suitable for making paper
pulp. Is the stuff that grows on our land of
no value at all, then, except for post-and-
rail fences? Our hardwoods seem all right
to the mere layman in these matters; Queens-
land pine looks suitable enough for nailing
together to make a butter-box or a, case for
rabbits. All round us trees are growing
much closer to our cities and railways than
the forests of Maoriland where the soft white
pine is obtained. There ought to be an expert
pronouncement on this subject. We want to
know whether the trouble with these timber
people is that they have got so much into
the habit of importing that they simply can’t
bear to erect a local milling plant or two, or
whether the Devil has got into Australian
growths in some way that doesn’t meet the
eye, and made them totally unfit for use.
> ♦
One great, characteristic, outstanding feature
of war is dirt. Without dirt a battle would
have some resemblance to the heroic thing it
was once represented to be; for men can face
death and might even be uplifted in soul by
the bombs that separate spirit from body if
it were not for the dirt accompanying the
performance—the dirt in the trenches, in the
military organisation and in the civil life
behind. Dirt—prolonged, aggressive, unre-
mitting dirt —must always degrade; and
where it becomes master of the situation good
citizenship is almost impossible. It is about
time that tills view of things got considera-
tion in our economic arrangements. In con-
sidering the value of an industry, the im-
portance of a city or the prospects of a new
policy, dirt, should be fairly and honestly
weighed against possible cash returns. The
big factory system under which thousands of
men and women are congregated into huge
buildings and return from work to close-pent
dwellings in congested streets may make a
good showing in statistical returns; but
gauged by its capacity for producing dirt and
keeping the workers unclean, its record is
bad. Dirt, according to Tom Carlyle, is
matter in the wrong place. The human
matter which makes the citizenship of the
nation is.never so much in the wrong place
as when it is herded into great crowds ; and
a ll the plans for the future should aim at
the spreading of industrialism over wide
areas where sun and air and all the cleansing
and purifying influences might get in then-
good work.

While Britain is so hard pressed that it is
now sending men of 51 to the Front, some
good friend of Germany is suggesting reasons
why Peakce should continue to refuse to
accept a Highland regiment from Australia.
The Bulletin hasn’t much sympathy with
men who, though living in Australia, want
to mark themselves out as Highlanders,
Hibernians or anything else exotic. But it
faces this fact: there are thousands of people
in Australia who, perhaps because they
haven’t been long resident here, do take a
pride in being Scotch or Irish or French,
and if it is necessary to make concessions of
a name or a ribbon or two in order to get
these people to do their duty, by all means
let us make them. Every recruiter knows
that you can rarely get two recruits with
the same appeal. You have to come at them
all ways. Okciiakd, Minister for Recruit-
ing, being quite human knows that, and
supports the movement for both Highland
and Irish regiments. Peakce can still find
difficulties in the way. The sooner someone
convinces him that this isn’t a time to find
difficulties, but rather a time to find ways
of overcoming them, the better for us and
for our chances of success.
The old wile or dodge of the Freetraders
was voiced again by Sydney Telegraph
(13/6/T8) :
A loud lament, however, goes up in the forgotten
voice of flscalism over the refusal of the Government
to keep Parliament together until a new tariff is
elaborated for “the protection of native industries.”
....The Department, says Mr. Webster, is obliged
to restrict the extension of telegraphic and telephone
conveniences because the material, which has risen
in price from 50 to 300 per cent, and owing to the
shipping scarcity is in some cases unprocurable for
love or money. The building industry is seriously
hampered by the prohibitive import prices of slates,
tiles, galvanised iron and other things. Imported
food staples have risen enormously But the Pro-
tectionists of the old school demand a tariff to
block the imports of articles that there would be
no ships to bring here if the Custom Bouse was
turned into a free warehouse for imported merchan-
dise.
The incurable Freetrader of the old German
school remains solid on three points:—
(1) That the old low-tariff and often no-tariff
importing policy, which made this country so
dependent on foreigners for telegraph and telephone
material, building material, food staples and ships
to carry them and other things that, though no
enemy has set foot on its soil, it is being ruined by
famine prices, is a darned good policy.
(2) That it is better to endure these famine prices
and the worse ones to come than to encourage
“native industry.” Therefore it must be carefully
explained to the prospective manufacturer that, if
he wants to supply the shortage caused by the high
price of the imported things which the Telegraph
enumerates, and hundreds of other imported things,
and of imported ships to carry them, he will do it
at his own risk and loss. For the Freetraitor holds
that the old tariff policy is the right thing, and
when the accidental war interruption is over the
Bun competitor must find his market awaiting him
as of old.
(3) And even the proposal that a new tariff should
be passed to come in force at the end of the war, so
that the possible manufacturer may know right now
how he will stand when the Bun and pro-Hun im-
porters are no longer shut out by war conditions, is
a foolish device of the “Protectionists of the old
school.” For if such a thing was done the Australian
manufacturer, finding that he wasn’t to be a mere
warming-pan for the Freetraitor’s pet Bun, might
start right now to relieve the scarcity of things.
And -the sight of him doing that under such con-
ditions would make every real Freetraitor boil with
honest indignation.
In the years before the war Britain stood
for a steadily-decreasing and Germany for a
steadily-increasing proportion of our imports,
and the tariff that produced these results is
not to be lightly interfered with by Protec-
tionists of the old school.
Australia can get a good many hints from
John Rathom’s revelations. For instance:
Careful examination of our wireless reports showed
a constant and suspicious connection between many
large commercial and shipping houses in the United
States and the German Foreign Office. Investigation
disclosed the fact that the code number and combina-
tions of letters used by the German Embassy in its
messages to Berlin were in many cases duplicated
by messages sent out from the Atlantic Communica-
tion Co., the Hamburg-America line and North Ger-
man Lloyd line, and many other concerns. Starting
with this knowledge, it soon developed that the
great strength of the German propaganda svstem in
America was largely due to the fact that these great
commercial houses were nothing more than outposts
of the German Foreign Office, heavily subsidised, and
acting directly under the orders of their home
offices, which in turn took their orders from Foreign
Office officials... .The Hamburg-America line and the
North German Lloyd line made regular reports
through their home office to the Foreign Office in
Berlin. These great corporations were used also for
other purposes by the German and Austrian Govern-
ments and the Embassies in Washington. Truly, the
German Ambassador, Von Berxstorff, was not under-
estimating the boundless credulity of a democracy
when he once said in his Embassy, in a burst of par-
donable pride in his ability to make the American
people believe \vhat he wanted them to believe: “In
this land of unlimited possibilities everything is pos-
sible.”
Rathom’s revelations are a lesson to all
English-speaking- countries, and especially
to Australia. For what he says of the offices
in America applies to the German Foreign
Office outposts in our miclst
The great majority of the men working in these
establishments were German and Austrian aliens, but
they invariably included among- their general mana-
gers or directors several who had acquired American
citizenship solely to permit them to conduct their
propaganda work with more freedom.
Viewed in the light of the proved fact that
these shipping offices were practically out-
posts of the German Foreign Office, the
managers and directors stand convicted of
connivance in the German scheme of World
Conquest. The case against them is that
they remained in their jobs up to the
very moment of war, whereas they couldn’t
have continued to hold them had they been
loyal friqnds of the country they lived in.
“Norseman”: “The Wanderer’s” par. (B.
18/4/T8) about the new Dorrigo North
(N.S.W.I public school being threatened in
the specifications with ceilings of American
Oregon, though superior native woods art-
growing all round, recalls my friend’s ex-
perience. He was at a N, S. Wales Mallet-
school on the Murray, and there his pre-
decessor had made a couple of desks himseii.
and so kept down the State expenditure.
Not being a carpenter, and wanting six
more, my friend wrote to the Department
suggesting that the desks be made on the
spot from Murray Pine, mentioning the two
desks as clinching the argument. ■ But no!
Eight months later waybills arrived stating
that the desks required had been sent from
Sydney per Albury and Melbourne. Then
the things went to Swan Hill, to' be finally
shipped per river boat. There was no wharf
within five miles, so my friend had to get a
carrier to do his bit for 15s. The curtain
was rung down on the horrible farce when
another 10s. was spent in having the iron
legs fixed on. You can’t teach Dead Tape!
Campbell Jones, for some time past man-
aging editor of the- Sun (Sydney), has been
appointed now also a director. Jones was
one of the bright Argus men who helped to
burnish the Sun when it ceased to be the old
Star. He joined the Argus in 1898, passed
to the Sun in 1912, established the paper’s
new cable service the same year, and returned
to Australia as managing-editor in 1916.
From Repatriation Minister Millen: —
In a recent issue you refer to a statement by Vic-
toiian Chief Secretary Bowser that StucUey Hall had
been offered as a gift by an anonymous patriot to
the Repatriation Department, and that the offer had
been refused. No such offer has been received.
When it .materialises, unless accompanied by impos-
sible conditions, it will be promptly accepted.
If Australia hadn’t been so preoccupied
with the Kaiser’s riot and bloodshed more
interest would have been taken in the violent,
death of Catherine Tatum, who was fatally
shot in a women’s street row in Sydney dur-
ing May. Deceased had the characteristics
of ashe-fiend. She had fired at her neighbors
with revolvers, and laid siege to their houses
with stones. Once she tried to stab another
woman at the Central Police Court, and on
an occasion when her arsenal was short of
weapons she went for an acquaintance with
a corkscrew. If there was a bottle to swing
it was swung according to schedule, and her
husband testified that he had taken four
razors from her. The end of this sort of
thing was not surprising. The aggressive
female stopped a bullet herself and took the
count. The jury on the murder charge said
“Not guilty.”
So far as the Homebush (N.S.W.) Abat-
toirs inquiry has gone the bulk of the evidence
seems to suggest that they are an absurdly
slow, expensive and ill-constructed haunt of
dirty meat and blowflies. One good point
about them was that they were supposed to
be profitable, which is something, seeing that
out of 23 State enterprises 10 showed a dead
loss for last financial year. But the hard-
hearted Government Auditor mentioned that
whereas interest, depreciation, sinking fund
and insurance were set down as £15.000. in-
terest alone should have been more like
£48,000, so the place where the meat is
viewed with disrespect seems to have lost its
chief redeeming feature.
According to a London writer, H. A.
Spender, the late John Redmond expressed
the view that Australia would have carried
Conscription if Home Rule had been granted
to Ireland. Well, what about father Bull
trying it on the family?
