“The Nigger” must appeal to Melbourne
audiences as a well-knit and powerful drama,
dealing with the liquor question in its
bearings on the U.S. negro problem. The
play, produced at the King’s. (Melb.) last
Saturday, was written by a high-minded
nigger of the type that figures in the name-
part. It is a sermon clothed in romance and
it is admirably acted and staged—a, show far
more intellectually impressive than anything
seen here since Ethel Irving appeared in
“Dame Nature.” Philip Morrow is the re-
presentative upright young man of a South-
ern State. Sheriff in Act 1, and afterwards
Governor. During his term as sheriff he
stands for the justice of the law to save a
black grandson of his old mulatto nurse from
being lynched for outraging a white gal.
Needless to say, the guilty pusson was in
drink at the time of the offence. Subse-
quently the question of heredity arises in
connection with Philip Morrow’s feeling
towards the darkies, whom he dislikes for
their innate savagery, but for whom he
claims fair play. He learns, later, that he
himself is a nigger in the Yankee sense
of the word. His giddy military grand-
father, having lost his white wife and her
offspring at its birth, foisted the baby of his
own pet mulatto girl upon the family, and
thus saved the estate from being inherited by
an ancestor of one Clifton Noyes, a wealthy,
blustering distiller in Philip’s district. Noyes
knew this whilst he was running Morrow for
the Governorship, but kept his knowledge to
himself. Old nurse Jinny also knew it. The
foisted baby’s mother was her own pretty
sister in the long ago.
■* * * *
, Georgiana, the heroine, has an even stronger
derry on niggers than her sweetheart Philip,
who has now been convinced that the root of
the racial trouble is nurtured by the liquor
which Clifton Noyes distils. So, as Governor,
lie decides to sign a Prohibition Bill which
awaits his assent. Noyes thereupon taunts
him with the black strain in his ancestry,
and mammy Jinny, confirming the truth of
the assertion, Governor P. Morrow sits star-
ts at the ruins of his ambition. He must
break off bis engagement to Georgiana in
the first place. VY hen he confesses the reason
she is horrified. The girl’s terrible emotion
shocks Philip mto forgetting the. exemplary
rectitude of George
Washington. H e
takes back the
truth ; swears he is
all white, and offers
to hug the lady as
before. She repels
him, and he goes
mad with passion.
The nigger strain
of savagery domi-
nates the white
blood in the man,
and he is proceed-
ing to be brutal to
his gazelle, when
an interruption re-
stores him to sanity
and th e curtain
falls. The rest is
mostly peace and
goodwill. The grief-
stricken Governor
is persuaded that
his mission in life
is the uplifting of
niggers, and he
commences by put-
ting his seal to the
anti-liquor 1a w.
When hi s lover
comes to him and
asks him to go
ahead from where
“The Nigger,” at Mel-
bourne King's.
A. E. Greenaway as
Senator Long.
she interrupted him he refuses. With
the black streak in him, marriage, he decides,
wouldn’t be a fair thing. He also makes up
his mind to confess himself a nigger to the
people, and, as a matter of course, resign
office,
* X- ‘ * *
Such a play demands convincing acting,
and, in a measure, compels it. Half a dozen
characters give reality to the story of Mor-
row, the Governor, and two others (played
by Tilden and Eardley Turner) are pictur-
esque figures of melodrama in the first act.
No subtle shades are required of Lionel
Belmore as the distiller, for he is always
boastful, argumentative, expostulatory or
threatening. But hearty sincerity makes
his impersonation a striking success. More-
over he is an excellent foil to Guy Bates
Post, whose artistic reserve and restraint are
essentially cold-blooded. The element of
“greatness” is in Post’s acting, insofar as he
suits the character to his- mannerisms,
rather than himself to the character. He is
a master of egotistical art, making no jiotice-
able oratorical effort, using only the irredu-
cible minimum of gesture, and expressing
full consciousness of his individuality with-
out obviously asserting it. His silent effects
in,the scene where Belmore first calls him a
nigger, and Katie Towers (as old Jinny) is
brought into substantiate the title, are fine
touches of realism, whilst the air of self-
righteous dignity in his chilly demeanour
gives intensity to the contrast when the lion
shakes the icicles from his mane and crushes
to him his captured female. It is
good to see a rarely appreciated Australian
actress like Miss Towers make a notable hit
in a first-class play. Her performance of the
old nigger mammy is marked in the strongest
scene. Miss Thais Lawton’s acting is inter-
estingly uneven, as it was in “The Masquer-
ader.” She has charming moments of light
comedy and her ability to rise to tragic
occasions is proved in the harrowing second
act. But there are times when her diction
is irritatingly artificial, one of these occasions
being at the finish of the play on Saturday
night. Raymond Lawrence, as the Governor’s
private secretary, is in the picture all the
time, and Greenaway', disguised as the origi-
nal Uncle Sam, imparts an air of all the
virtues to his graceful little sketch of Sena-
tor Long.
■ “Katinka,” at Her Majesty’s (Alelb.),
continues to reflect on the character of Rus-
sian ambassadors in general and Boris
Stroggoff in particular. The fact that his
first wife left him to join a Turkish pasha’s
connubial auxiliaries doesn’t in itself prove
his unpleasantness, but when the lady turns
out to be Olive Godwin, one feels that Boris
was. an awful barbarian to let her go.

untitled citizens who never put on airs with
their tailor. When Graham Browne has
laid down the sword and the tape-measure
Muriel Starr will come along in a new
drama.

At the Opera House (Sydney) this week’s
drama is luring custom under the title of
"Through the Divorce Court.” The story
is that of an English belted earl with a
young Spanish wife who is madly loved by
one Gusman Di Mendoza, the lady’s former
guardian. (None of the parties are men-
tioned in “Whitaker’s Peerage, Baronetage,
Knightage and Comnanmnfiw.’n To
the jealousy of Lord Penarth and cause him
to bring Dolores into uoume w.-a
himself, Mendoza plans a meeting between
his beloved and her “natural” brother, Leon
Di Valdez, the secret of whose birth the girl
has sworn to Her Dying Mother never to
reveal. Foiled in his first attempt to have
the husband discover his wife in Di
Valdez’s arms, Mendoza thinks hard, and
this time his devilish plan succeeds.
The heroine is thereupon ushered into
the Divorce Court, and, until the end of
a strenuous act, her future looks as black as
the approach of a southerly-buster. The
clouds roll by, however, in Act 4. Then
there are the usual reconciliations, and the
villain sneaks off weighed down by his
wickedness.
*** N #
Lord Penarth was played by George Cross
in a rather too restrained manner, while
Lorna Forbes made a very attractive but too
emotional Lady Penarth. A more subdued
tone at the end of Act 2 would have made
the curtain more effective. Rutland
Beckett’s Mendoza was quite good, and
Gerald Harcourt and Dorothy Hood (child
Sybil) were accorded a vote of thanks from
a packed house on Saturday.

“Within the Law,” revived at Sydney Cri-
terion, looks all the better for being a little
kindlier than of yore. Inspector Burke, of
the N’York police, is still an amazing and
incredible apparition, and Detective-Sergeant
Cassidy a wholly unbelievable person. But
owing to the declaration of war or something
they have their human moments, which is
just as well perhaps, or the democraev would
be wondering most of the time why Brother
Jonathan Wilson needed to leave home in
search of Huns on the other side of the
Atlantic. Anyway, it is possible that the
police business in “Within the Law” was in-
troduced as a sort of comic relief or as a
satire on the popular brand of fiction which
presents the American “bull” as a barbarian
addicted to snarling through the side of his
mouth and secretly putting crooks to the
tortures of Torquemada. The real sting is
the revelation of the opulent draper’s tup-
penny soul. Muriel Starr is perhaps as well
equipped to play Mary Turner as anyone
Australia has studied for a long time, and
much practice in the miseries incidental to
doing three years on the cooler “for some-
thing I nev-er-er did” have done her no
harm. Frank Harvey, as the bright, purpose-
ful son of the dreadful old rag-merchant, does
rather better than any of the rest of the
cast, though reservation must be made of
Norma Mitchell as the young person who
met Mary behind the bars, and later on got
away with a hatful of coin on a breach-of-
promise case. Miss Mitchell, who gets the
laugh of encouragement with great fre-
quency. has something akin to genius in these
unpleasantly humorous parts. “Within the
Law” stands sampling twice surprisingly
well.

Gerard’s story of his “Four Years in Ger-
many” is being told on the Town Hall screen,

produced, possesses educational and histori-

Married,” with cheerful Madge Kennedv in
the lead.
There is one Melbourne man who reckons
policewomen should be supplied with uni-
forms. The lady sitting next him on a
North Carlton tram dropped a parcel. He
accepted what he thought was a hint, and
picked up the bag like a gent., and while
stooping down contrived to let her know
that he had taken the hint that she was
that sort of woman. The result was a
charge of indecent assault and a fine of £2,
in default 14 days. The assaultive was a
policewoman!
Something like the sound of a wounded
elephant smote the welkin when a heavily-
jewelled dame was caught with a for-
bidden osprey at Flemington recently. The
brilliant ’tec. who had spotted the ornament
requested its instant removal; but the lady
shrieked that she wouldn’t, and with every
protest there was a sob. When she finally
consented to surrender, she tartly asked the
detective if his solicitude for the feathered
world embraced a colony df night-birds who
had burgled her cash-box a few weeks before.
It was a nasty jolt for the Force.

“Lance-Jack”: On King’s Birthday the
Vic. Railway Department spared no trouble
to see that picnickers and race-goers were
properly accommodated. There were no com-
plaints.' But things were different with 600
men who left for Sydney. They were neither
bookies, punters nor pleasure-seekers. They
were outward bound from Broadmeadows
camp, and they were reinforcements for the
Front. They included members of . the
Sportsmen’s Thousand and Italian reservists.
Those who got seats were packed like sar-
dines, and those whq didn’t had to stand in
full kit at least as far as Albury. Several
of them determined to get out at the first
stop, and pay their own fares to Sydney by
express. When a Melbourne newspaper asked
the Railway Department what it thought of
the scandal, the reply forthcame that the

number of soldiers was greater than that for
which provision was arranged by the De-
fence Department. The Defence Depart-
ment, in its turn, said that the number of
troops was not exceeded, but that the accom-
modation asked for was “slightly below re-
quirements.” So there you are!
But the N. S. Wales Railway Depart-
ment is no better. During the last big strike
a returned officer had to see one of its poten-
tates about shifting some men between Syd-
ney and Liverpool. The time was arranged.
“It’ll be a special?” said the officer interro-
gatively. “No, no no,” said the Railway
boss. “Please don’t call it that. You see,
if we gave you specials we should have to
put them on, too, for the race people, and
we can’t spare them at present!” “And I’m
going away again to fight for worms like
that!” said the officer afterwards.
“Smokehouse”: Recently died at Steven
Island, in Torres Strait, an old neighbor of
mine, an ancient brown crone, the last of the
old stock and a link with the island’s super-
stitious and bloody past, taking with her the
secret of the whereabouts of a remarkable
diamond. Some 40
years ago, so the
legend runs, when a
windjammer was
w recked on the
island, the captain
brought ashore a
despatch-box, which
he never Allowed to
leave him. This ex-
cited the natives’
curiosity, and he was
forced to open the
case and show the
contents, which
turned out to be a
stone, said to be of
remarkable size and
brilliancy. Hepitched
some yarn about its
magic properties, and
as a result the local
pourri-pourri man de-
capitated the posses-
sor and got away with
the stock-in-trade.
This man shortly
before his death, con-
fiding to his next-of-
kin, the above-men-
tioned dame, that he
was afraid of the
stone and had buried
it, passed on the
secret of its resting-
place. Some years
ago attempts were
made by white men
to find the gem,
which the natives of
the island know as
“Fire E y e” on
account of the red
light it emitted at
night. After much
persuasion and many
gifts the old woman
pointed out the spot
where she said Fire
Eye was buried, but
no sign of it was dis-
covered and t h.e
chances are that it
never was anywnere near that spot. Some say
that it has been found, but the natives are
confident that it is still undiscovered.
“Burrundie”: Another home-made North-
ern Territory ordinance lately got a bang on
the nose at Darwin. In this case the usual
sly-groger was being biffed, when his lawyer
got up and remarked that, as the penalty
was £SOO and 12 months, or both, and as the
Interpretation Act had something to say
about such matters being indictable offences
and not matters of summary jurisdiction, the
information must be given the boot. “The
Crown” showed great indignation, but the
magistrate tossed out the .information as
requested. Then Gehenna broke loose.
Previous offenders who had been wrongfully
convicted got busy. Results —two writs of
certiorari, which have been upheld. Now
one man who paid a £2OO fine and did six
months has received his fine back, and as he
can’t be refunded his six months he wants
money—a few thousand to help him over
the next race-meeting. And there are others
waiting.
* * * *
Australia holds its end up at the training-
camps in England as well as in the' field in
France and Palestine. At Durrmgton there
is an old-established camp for artillery
officers. Nowadays each “school” consists of
from 25 to 30 officers—as a rule, about four
or five Australians and Canadians and the
balance Tommies. An Australian had topped
each or the last four schools to the end of
March.

“0.K.” in protest and exhortation :—-
The Agricultural Society of Victoria has decided
that its next annual show shall be “dry.” This
will mean sacrificing £SOO or so for the. sale of
liquor-bar privileges, but the lessee would need to
sell at least £7OOO worth of liquor to make a profit
on his speculation, and a public consumption of
£7OOO worth of beer and whisky is vaste in war
time, and of no moral or physical benefit to the
community—especially soldiers. Commonsense is
making steady progress. An agricultural society is
as much concerned as anybody else (save brewers,
distillers, hotelkeepers, magsmen and prostitutes)
in encouraging sobriety; and Governments will yet
have to confess that they are expected to put that
concern into general effect. This will mean aban-
doning the ancient fallacy that you can t make
people sober by Act of Parliament. Governments
would have thrown the fallacy overboard long ago if
the campaign had not been collared by the old pio-
fessional killjoys. But these have almost ceased to
count in the forced march of commonsense. Com-
monsense means patriotism; and as alcohol has
proved itself a deadly' enemy since the war started,
commonsense won’t have it. Reasonable republican-
ism “follows the King” for the time being. Un-
reasonable publicanism tries to push the other way,
and is being swep't back by the tide. Amen.
POETRY AND PROSE.
“Darling, is it possible such love can exist?"
“Honey, you are squashing my nose.”’
“Amalekite” : It’s about time the Victorian
Mallee had the “pinky” curse lifted from it.
The wise legislators who forbade pubs and
licensed wineshops ought to spend a summer
week-end on one of the Mallee lines and see
what the alternative is like. So-called
“Colonial wine,” diluted and mixed accord-
ing to local circumstances and the
saloon-keeper’s conscience, is deadly
stuff —one glass will just about
loose the chains of Satan in a man,
and two will render him an A 1
candidate for an asylum. But when
there’s nothing else to drink Cocky
naturally takes it on a day when
the heat pretty well melts his wag-
gon tyres. I have seen (and
heard !) some wild jamborees after
a meeting of the Progress League
or some such institution, held at the
wine-shanty beause it happened to
boast the largest and only room in
the place, with of course the inevit-
able “bob in” afterwards. The worst
feature is that mere lads can got
the poison, whereas in a properly-
regulated pub-license district they
would mostly be denied the chance.
A couple of years ago a meeting
of Anglican parsons at St. Arnaud
(with a bishop and archdeacon
amongst them) voted for the aboli-
tion of the wine-saloon and the establishing
of licensed pubs. But the Governments have
done nothing, and the wreckings continue.

“Cornstalk”: Maorilanders are never tired
of sneering at Australia for turning down
Conscription, and, if they think they would
have done anything else themselves, or that
it is calculated to improve the position, I
don’t mind; but I do kick when these
patriots have the gall to cast a slur upon the
Australian on the field. Col. R. A. Chaffey,
conmmanding the Canterbury district, un-
loaded himself of this at the Overseas Club’s
Empire Day demonstration :
Out of every 19 j boys sent from Maoriland only
one failed to reach the firing-line; while of the Aus-
tralian forces olie in every four failed to reach the
firing-line. This bore out what the chief men of
Maoriland were saying, that the training was sound
and good. I wonder if anyone has any idea how
many Maorilanders are prisoners. The last record,
some six weeks ago, showed that there were only
125. Compare that with Australia, who have 3400
prisoners in the hands of the Germans. Of course
they sent very much larger numbers, but still the
proportion of prisoners is ten or twelve times larger
than the prisoners from Maoriland. It either proves
that our boys go on fighting until there is nobody
left, or else they are so cunning that they take good
care they are not taken. The latter, I am certain,
is not the case.
This warrior has soldiered in Otago, Canter-
bury and Amuri, and he holds the M.L. Long
Service medal, so no doubt he considers him-
self more qualified to speak of the fighting
qualities of Billjim than, for instance, Bill-
jim’s cobber from the island that Chaffey
adorns (on parade). But the sooner the
M.L. Defence Minister asks Chaffey if he
thinks he is helping to win the war by
attempting to sow discord amongst the over-
sea troops the better. Nobody, I suppose,
can stop the ass from braying; but the
Minister can at least see that the ass does
not bray in any representative capacity.
From William Webster, P.M.G.: —
In your issue of 6th instant you complain of the
refusal of the Telegraph Branch to forward your
wire which stated that Henry Parkes was either a
Freetrader or a liar. Your allegation that such
action is akin to the censorship during the war is
Bui.i.ETiN-esque, in other words unsound, seeing that
Clause 96 of the Post and Telegraph Act of 1901
enacts that telegrams containing offensive words
shall not be transmitted. That clause has been
repeatedly used during the last 17 years, and rightly
so. No one would rest'ht such a message more
than The Bulletin, if addressed to the members of
its eminent staff. The obvious intention of the
paragraph is to pursue the usual Bulletin course,
i.e., to vent its spleen and animus on me personally.
Evidently it is a crime in the eyes of The Bulletin
for one to succeed in economically administering a
vast department during war-time; hence they use
every weapon, legitimate and otherwise, to destroy
anyone who dares to rob them of tiieir stock argu-
ment, viz., all are frauds except the mighty
Bulletin.
Mr. Webster over-rates himself. This paper
doesn’t know him “personally,” and is only
interested in him as the gentleman who loses
the mailbags and now declines to transmit
the people’s messages. As to The Bul-
letin’s message, by the way, it didn’t say
that “Henry Parkes was either a Freetrader
or a liar.” It didn’t mention Henry Parkes.
It didn’t mention anybody. Webster already
handles mailbags carelessly. This paper
trusts he won’t get into the same habit with
regard to the truth.

MID-JUNE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a breath of spring in the air,
With the winter but half-way gone.
Earth has a bloom to wear
And laughter trills from the dawn—
A song that is heartsome and sweet,
With dancing joy for the feet,
Blows lilting out of the dawn.
There’s hope in the old world’s heart.
Though the fires of Hell increase,
How long may they hold apart
The nations from dreams of peace?
Mighty the strength of their steel,
But the world begins to feel
The balm of the coming peace.
Spring clay in the winter’s cold.
Grim Mars sits limp , in his car.
The war grows weary and old
And the graves are many and far.
Weary of slaughter and death,
The world with a sighing breath
Turns to the dreams afar.
N.S.IV.
MARY McC.

When on June 8 this old planet had a
spare moment to look up from its own red
business, it found it had a new neighbor.
And according to the wise men, the new-
comer has its worries, too. In fact, it is just
a stupendous worry, and nothing else. Nova
Aquilse is, at a guess, the result of a col-
lision between a dead sun, romping through
space at anything up to 100 miles a second,
and a brother scorcher, also hogging without
headlights; or between a dead sun and an
invisible nebula. The smash was so terrific
that it set the whole neighborhood blazing.
When this happened no one knows to within
five minutes; but Ernest Cooke, the astro-
nomer, opines that it was about the time of
James 1., and it has taken the light all these
centuries to get this far. For the rest, its
color is bluish-white, which suggests that it
is hotter than our sun, which is estimated
to exceed 10,000 degrees; it is more brilliant
than Nova Percel, discovered in 1901, and
probably the most brilliant since Kepler’s
discovery in 1607. But it seems to be grow-
ing less refulgent, from which it may be
argued that the fire brigades have got the
water on.

“Henry Horsecollar” reminisces:—
I have had adventures in my time. I have faced
six crocodiles at two yards range. They were in
the lake attached to the palace at Jeypone, and I
went to encounter them with a guide and a bag of
meat and a palace hanger-on who had to be paid,
and a boy who insisted on carrying the meat and
had to be paid, and four brown loafers who came
to help the boy and wanted to be paid. Then as
the crocodiles were asleep on the opposite shore a
partially nude person who had to be paid ran
swiftly and roused the gorged creatures with a
stick—l never saw animals whacked like that before
—and, knowing their duty, they came and rushed
at me in moderation. I was quite unarmed, but I
did not blench, nor did the boy with the meat. I
have met the baboon in his native lair by the Zam-
besi ; he was an aged outcast from the herd and he
ran or toddled for his life. He was more of a town
hanger-on than a baboon. I have encountered the
boa-constrictor where the fearful animal hangs out.
He was in an advanced state of decomposition, and
a colored person took me to him for a pair of
shillings. He smelled high. I afterwards heard that
he was a fraud imported from a distance after death. I
might have gained the Victoria Cross once at the
sale of a deceased soldier's effects. I have even
been mixed up with lions, for I put a little money
into a fund for the benefit of a Rhodesian farmer’s
widow, whose farmer had tried to kill two of them.
He really did kill them, but his Kaffir, who was
standing behind him with a spare gun, started
dancing in a frenzy of excitement, and the spare
gun went off. The Kaffir, I believe, was flogged by
tin? executors, but that didn’t bring his employer
back to life, and as the widow was an Australian
girl and was left in poor circumstances I did my
bit along with Boers and other well-wishers. I have
been at fairly close quarters with wolves (unless
they were dogs), only I was in the train and the
wolves weren’t. And I have argued with a large
and forbidding monkey for the use of a seat on a
lonesome railway platform where no help was in
sight except the nigger station-master. That was
in Rajputana, where the monkey is treated as a
THE NAKED TRUTH.

nobody can ever say I tried to hide
behind YOUB petticoats. Eh, what?"
friend, and will almost carry your bag for you if
you don’t take liberties with his tail. But in the
autumn of my stirring life I am glad that
I wasn’t in that church at Orange, where
the parson was preaching peace and submission, and
a shindy arose, and the pacifists fought the defence
party all over the holy edifice and in the street
outside, and refused to arbitrate or to suspend hos-
tilities, or to regard anybody as a brother—except
themselves. I' would sooner meet two decomposed
boa-constrictors than one passive resister. He has a
tendency to aim wallops at the lower stomach which
defies all rules. I am half-inclined to think that if
an army corps had been conscripted of Quakers,
passive resisters, pacifists, Peace Society men,, con-
scientious objectors, I.W.W.’s, pro-Huns, indiffCrent-
ists and cold-footers, and it had been put in the
front line under rigid discipline, Cambrai would have
been saved.

“Macko” : After a couple of years in the
trenches of Flanders and France, I thought
a trip to the clouds would at least give me
a change of air, so I applied for a job in the
Aviation Corps. To my delight I got orders
to weigh in. I tipped the scale at 14st. 71b.
The officer shook his head. “Stop where you
are, laddie.” he said ; “we’re not sendin’ up
tanks yet.”

Adelaide Art Gallery has had a curious
find.. In 1882, a canvas by Wyllie, the
marine painter, was bought for it in London
by Poynter, now president of the Royal
Academy. Wyllie has just written to say
that there is another canvas at the back of
the frame, and that he would like it taken
out and sold for the benefit of a French war
fund which his wife is interested in. Mrs.
Wyllie adds“My husband had stretched the
picture over another beautiful one. You
know what artists are—or perhaps you don’t.
Well, rather than have th« bother of re-strain-
ing the back picture, ho let the two go
together.” The painting, of French fishing
boats, has been brought to light. It is not
nearly as good as “Our River,” which used
to hide it, but it will bring in a sizeable
cheque.

“Umntu”: Your whole-hearted Afrikander
has many pet bunions for others to tread on,
a. prominent one being that named equal-
rights-for-both-languages. On this the S’Afri-
can Railway Department seems unable
to escape treading. Some time ago General
Ilertzog had to. complain that the menu-cards
used in the dining cars were never printed in
Dutch, and the explanation that chefs had a
language of their own which was mainly
French hardly pacified him. The other day
the Department tactlessly came down on the
same corn again. A new line was to be
opened which had reached a temporary stoo-
ping place in the veld of a particularly
Dutch district. At the opening luncheon a
local champion severely criticised the Depart-
ment for choosing in a Boer district such an
English name for the terminus as “Pro Tern.”

“Bacon Stealer”: A block had occurred on
one of the many roads leading to the front
line in France. A Billjim driver, in a big
hurry to get through with his load, pulled up
with a jerk in rear of the long string of
transport waggons. Hailing a sergeant, who
was coming back from the front line, he
asked: “What have I struck, Sergeant—a
rest-home for horses?”
“Aus.”: Although we knew that horse-
flesh was considered something of a luxury
by the civilian population of the surround-
ing villages, it was looked upon with cold
disfavor by the Bill
jims in our platoon,
and each of us was
prepared to wager
the last dixieful of
stew that we would
know the meat if it
was served up to us.
One of the lads, hav-
ing just cleaned out
the two-up school,
invited the section
to eat at his expense
at a hash-house in
the vicinity. Mad-
emoiselle assured us
she had “plenty good
bif steak,” and we
were soon bogging in
to a hefty feed and
enjoying the change
from our usual bur-
goo. The tenderness,
flavor and quantity
of the meat were
bon. Next day w 7 e
learned that one of
our prads, having
broken a leg in the
transport the previ-
ous morning, had
been shot- and that
two of our push had
sold out to our Allies
at a franc per lb. That’s bow we got the good
bif steak!
* * * *
The “and—or” question which has been
worrying the S. Aus. Supreme Court for
half a year is settled at last. The trouble
was that an old Act of 1850, still in force,
said that additional evidence may be taken
on an appeal by consent of the parties “or”
order of the Court. But another copy of
the Act, apparently just as old and just as
authentic, says consent “and” order. The
difference is remarkable. In the one case
the parties to an action can proceed without
the Court’s leave, and the Court need not
wait for their consent. In the other the
whole lot have to be of the same mind. A
requisition on London has produced a formal
signed copy of the Act, deposited in the
Colonial Office; and that says “or,” which
settles the question.
“Wahsj”: Here’s another versatile Aus-
tralian ; and this one isn’t at the Front,
though it’s no fault of his. He was sent up
lately to take charge of a one-man school
near my tin-scratching show; and as Empire
Day came round, and the youngsters started
to talk about the fine time the big-town kids
were going to have with Ministers and
M’s.P. and mayors and returned V.C.’s, he
felt sad. “Never mind,” said he, “we’ll get
the lot somehow.” He solved his problem
when the five visitors and the 25 kids turned
up on Friday by introducing himself as “The
hon. member for Tin Show,” and calling on
himself for a speech, which he supplied.
Next he stepped off the packing-case and
stepped back again, this time, as teacher, to
conduct “Advance, Australia Fair.” The
Mayor and the V.C. followed; and finally
he impersonated the Director of Education,
congratulated the teacher on the success of
the function and graciously granted the kids
the remainder of the day off.

THE BOYS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Silent stands the callous ass,
Getting on in years;
He, when troops of soldiers pass,
Mocks at shouts and cheers,
Mingling his contempt for “gas”
With the slackers’ sneers.
Others stand, with folded arms,
Cheering now and then
Young recruits from shops and farms;
I just wave my pen—
I but dream of war’s alarms
Faced by fighting men.
For his mater and his dad,
For his manhood’s sake,
Goes the sturdy soldier-lad,
Game to give and take.
His big heart is bold and glad:
Mine with pain must ache.
Luck be with you khaki chaps,
In your martial stunt —
Workers, clerks, and “crooks,” perhaps,
Going to the Front!
You who go to fill the gaps
Help to bear the brunt.
Some of you must bravely die.
We stay free from fear.
Some who watch you passing by
Raise a feeble cheer;
Others grin, and others sigh,
Whilst the slackers sneer;
Others clap their hands —and I
Can but raise a tear.
VICTORIA.
O. K.

John Sarle Trew, who has just died at
Malvern (Vic.), aged 79, had a father who
was once Governor of Nova Scotia. John
came to Victoria at 16, and after a spell as
jackeroo opened branches of the London
Bank at several of the early gold rushes.
His two boys are Major W. M. Trew, who
came back from Anzac and France with the
D.S.O. a fortnight before his dad went west,
and Major 11. F. Trew, Commissioner of
Police at Pretoria.
Charles Johnston, Speaker of the Maori-
land Leg. Council, crossed the border last
week. He was 73, tall, white-headed and
florid-faced, and had been a rather casual
member of the Old Men’s Refuge since ’9l.
Otherwise there was nothing about Charles
Johnston to take notice of except that he was
very rich. The original Maoriland Johnston
was a London stockbroker who, after getting
“hammered,” migrated to the Windy Isles,
CHARLTON, M.H.R.
and, starting: in business at Wellington as a
merchant, prospered greatly. Three sons in-
herited the proceeds. Charles stuck to the
business in a decorative way, made more
money and incidentally married one of the
Featherstones. Brother Walter became a
squatter, held a portfolio in several Bight
Thinking Ministries of the long ago, and was
waiting for Charlie over yonder. The family
has been hard hit by the war, and the just-
dead man had a sorrowful year or two at
the finish. He was the last but one of the
Leg. Council “lifers.” The survivor of them
all is old Colonel Baillie, the sole surviving
white officer of the battle of Chillian wallah.
Colonel “Charlie” Butler, son of the State
Treasurer of S. Aus., has now got permis-
sion from the Defence Department to raise
a reinforcement of 500 men and take them
to the Front with him. He came home
with trench-fever.
Lieut. P. Y. Storkey, Australia’s newest
V.C., was dux of the Napier (M.L.) Boys’
School before he crossed to Sydney to take
his law course. He enlisted in 1915, his
final year, was severely wounded late in 1916,
and in 1917 his ankle was crushed —
but he was all there when liis chance
came for the V.C. F. N. Lipscomb,
an English-born lieutenant of the same
(19tli) Battalion, joined him in his
wild enterprise. Lipscomb used to be a
farmer, arid is 25 years long one way and
6ft. Ifin. the other. Storkey is 26 and five
feet seven and a half.
Brisbane Anglican Synod keeps its jubilee
this month. Only one member of the first
Synod is left —Canon “Tommy” Jones, who
has sat in every session for the 50 years.
But he doesn’t hold the Anglican long-
distance: record for Brisbane. Mrs. Hard-
grave. who was 90 the other day, has been
attending Brisbane Cathedral (or the
churches which preceded it) for 71 years.
“ J.F.11.” resurrects an Australian pioneer :
There are probably .still a few oldest inhabitants
who remember Archbishop Ullathorne, one of the
EVERYTHING COMPLETE.
“Cheero, Hill. You look as if you’re off to
Turkey! ”
most striking and picturesque personalities of early
Australia. His biography has been published by the
London Catholic Truth Society. He went to Aus-
tralia in 1832 at the age of 26, with jurisdiction as
Vicar-General over the whole continent. Governor
Bourke playfully dubbed him “Agitation-General of
Australia,” on account of his propensity for insisting
on getting grievances redressed. After declining-
three Australian bishoprics—Adelaide, Hobart and
Perth —-he accepted a couple of English sees. That
is an ecclesiastical record likely to stand until the
crack of doom. Perth, by the way, is described in
this book as being in N. S. Wales: hazy ideas of
Australian geography still prevail in old England.
Dr. Ullathorne saw a great deal of the convict sys-
tem, published a book on “The Horrors of Trans-
portation,” and gave evidence before a House of
Commons committee on the subject: Marcus Clarke
found some of his first-hand information useful when
writing “His Natural Life.” Returning to England
he became the first Bishop of Birmingham and was
a foremost figure in English Catholic life and
literary activity for the next 40 years. His last
book —he was guilty of over 30 —was a memoir of
his old friend, Dr. Willson, the first Bishop of
Hobart. 'Although he saw so much of convict life
in the Australia of the ’thirties and ’forties, it was
not until the ’fifties, when he was Bishop of Bir-
mingham, that he had his one and only experience
as a prisoner himself. Committed to Warwick
Castle for a week for some technical breach of
the law in connection with an estate of which he
was an ex officio trustee, lie took his imprisonment
with philosophical lightheartedness, and laughingly
remarked that it was the first period of real retire-
ment from the world he had ever enjoyed.
“The Oldest Inhabitant”: Concerning the
father of Vic.’s new K.C.M.G., Mitchell,
K.C. William Henry Fancourt Mitchell was
a son of Rev. George Barkley Mitchell, vicar
of St. Mary’s, Leicester, and chap-
lain to the Duke of York of very
pious memory. Arriving in Van
Iliemen’s Land, he for a time filled
the office of Colonial Secretary.
He crossed to Melbourne about
1840 and went squatting; but at
the time of the gold fever Latrobe
invited him to organise a police
force and become Chief Commis-
sioner. It was he who introduced
the cadet system by promising a
number of dashing young fellows
commissions and outfits on their
passing through a successful pro-
bation in hunting bushrangers and
doing gold-escort duty. In the
’fifties Captain McMahon, head of
the City Police, became Acting-
Commissioner and eventually took
over the job. Mitchell, sen.* entered political
life as one of the five original M’s.L.C. for
the N.W. province (1856).
Knox and Cox came back to Melbourne by
a recent transport. George Hodges Knox, of
the 23rd, went away as a lieutenant, and re-
turned by way of Gallipoli and France as a
lieut.-colonel. Like young Athol Teare, M.C.,
who travelled home by the same boat, he was
an orchardist in more peaceful days—an
occupation also followed by Major-General
Gellibrand, a Tasmanian leader of whom
more will shortly be heard. Major Cox
belonged to the 14tli —Jacka’s battalion —and
has been three times wounded. His brother
A.R. —same rank and battalion —has been
wounded four times. They were both in
Gallipoli.
“Philosopher” Smith did more than dis-
cover Mt. Bischoff’s tin; he gave his name-
sake, Laird Smith. M.11.R.. a
push along the track to the Fed.
Parliament. When Laird
Smith was a youth knocking
about the N.W. coast of the
Speck, the old battler encour-
aged him to read and acquire
that ability to quote Aristotle
and other long-dead birds which
convinced Hobart Laborites
that he was the very man to
go to the House of Reps, for
them.
An early predecessor of Post-
master-General Webster a t
Melbourne was an ex-lieu-
tenant of the 50th . Regi-
ment, Benjamin Baxter, who
was more at home in the newly-
established Club, on the race-
course or in private theatricals
than at sorting mails in the
small weatherboard cottage in
Flinders-street. So his wife did
all the work : and soon she fell
foul of the Sydney authorities.
Letters from Melbourne to
Europe were sent via Sydney,
which received the postage, then
heavy. Mrs. Baxter took ad-
vantage of the sailing of the
Thomas Laurie, the first wool-
ship from Port Phillip, to send
a mail direct to London; and
thus she struck the first strong
blow for decentralisation. But the subse-
quent storm nearly blew the roof off Cape
York. The Baxters soon after resigned,
and Skene Craig, a merchant, undertook to
manage the Melbourne office.
Harry Holland, lately elected to succeed
Paddy Webb for Grey in the M.L. Parlia-
ment, scraped through with only 210 votes
to the good. It was his first win of the
kind, but he well knows the bitterness of
defeat, having unsuccessfully contested
Senate elections and Federal and State seats
in N. S. Wales more times than he cares to
remember. Harry was born at Queanbeyan
(N.S.W.) in ’6B, and for nearly 30 yeai's
worked on Revolutionary Socialist and Labor
papers. He started on the Socialist, at
Sydney, in ’94, then went to the Northern
People at Newcastle, which later came to
Sydney, and is still going as the People.
Finally he gravitated to the Maoriland
Worker, and from that sanctum moves to
Parliament. A ready and forceful talker.
Henry Holland will insist on being noticed
by the Speaker.
W. Cameron, the stolid cocky who dished
Laborite Toombs for the Upper Hunter
(N.S.W.) Assembly seat, is a native of the
district, born about 40 years back at Rouchel,
where his family were amongst the earliest
settlers. He fought in S’Africa, and was one
of the party which escaped from the Boer
ambush at the Zand River. Later, he was
with the Australian contingent at King
George’s Coronation, and later still went to
Ceylon with E. F. Waddy’s cricket team.
George Hibbert, a soft-drinks maker of
Sorrento (Vic.), has a daughter who is
doing her bit in rubber top-boots. Before
the war her part in life was mostly orna-
mental ; but when the Hibbert boys began
to go off to the Front she put on one of
the lads’ outfits and started to do a man’s
work at the factory. There are now four
Hibbert soldiers in the field.

From the Front: —
Lieut.-Colonel Lamb, brother-in-law of the late
“Jimmie Pannikin,” has returned for the second time
from the big fight. He left as captain in the 3rd Bat-
talion, received his majority in Egypt and took part
in the Landing, and was soon afterwards shot through
the arm and invalided to Australia. After a spell
of six months he again left, as lieutenant-colonel in
charge of the 34th. He saw active service in
France till bad weather and hard fare downed him.
In England he was in charge of Lark’s Hill camp,
and later was a president of court-martials; but
this sort of soldiering did not fit in with Lamb’s
notions, and he applied to be sent into the firing-liHe
again or to be ticketed for home. He arrived last
week labelled, as he hopes, T.U.G.S. (Temporarily
unfit for general service).
The Hector Observatory at Wellington
(M.L.) has sprung into world’s prominence
by being the first to record the new star’s
arrival on the astronomical chart. G. V.
Hudson, an unassuming but fearfully indus-
trious scientist, who, after pursuing beetles,
inspects the heavens every evening from the
near-by suburb of Karon, would appear to
have given the preliminary alarm, and the
glad tidings were promptly announced to the
world from the nearby observatory named
after the late James Hector, explorer and
geologist. The Hector Observatory is a
small but efficient establishment perched
among the gales on Kelburne Heights, the
local telescope and other implements having
been removed thither so that Richard Sedclou
might be buried on the site of the old obser-
vatory. The elderly King having given up
the' job of registering meridian and other
time, E. C. Adams succeeded him in charge,
and blossomed into being Dominion Astrono-
mer. Adams, a brother of Arthur, of Syd-
ney, is a brilliant mathematician, and not
long ago spent a happy year at the famous
Tuck Observatory, in America, measuring
comets’ tails and making other vast calcu-
lations.
Still living in Ballarat, aged 83, William
Roberts, the last survivor of the party of
miners who found the Welcome Nugget—6o
years ago on June 8. The speck was dis-
covered at a depth of 100 ft., and weighed
21950 z. With the idea of disposing of it by
art-union, the Wittkowski brothers bought it
for £lO,OOO, but this was before the days of
patriotic raffles, and their scheme w T as
blocked. Eventually the nugget was sold in
England. One of the finders, who had
bought a sliare in the claim for £l3O, died
a while ago in the Ballarat Benevolent
Asylum.
A LEAF FROM NATURE’S BOOK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Stricken by the season’s wind,
Blushing an autumnal tint,
In your fall, O leaf, I find
Mother Nature’s gentle hint:
Spring and summer, autumn—then
Winter is the end of men.
Life of man is but a year.
Seasons four we sinners know;
Autumn tells of winter near
When it is our time to go,
Rotting like a fallen leaf
In this world of joy and grief.
Time is endless ; yet a span,
Measured by the hand of Fate,
Covers all the life of man—
Thus the solemn preachers prate.
Some men live till they be old,
Palsied by the winter’s cold.
Some, like leaves by summer storm
Torn from out the forest screen,
Perish whilst their hearts are warm ;
Others pluckt when newly green—•
Like the leaves that wilt or thrive, .
Some must die and some survive.
Youth and love, and manhood’s prime
Pass in turn from green to red:
Human leaves are changed by Time,
Lasting on till they be dead.
Memory has most to give
To the men who longest live.
But, in my late autumn days,
Thinking how I soon must drop,
I regard the sweet bouquets
Fashioned in the florist’s shop :
Autumn sprays with buds combined—
Leaves and roses intertwined.
I would be like one of these,
Spared the chilly winter’s breath,
Privileged to aid and please
Beauty whilst I sink to death....
Autumn leaf and faded youth—
Hackneyed thought, eternal truth!
VICTORIA.

Whatever the faults of its public finance
M.L. has at any rate made something of an
effort to collect a part of the war profits
which its people have made. Bates and taxes
paid by the B.N.Z. have moved as follows:
1914 .. £61,267 I 1917 .. £198,052
1915 .. 74,549 1918 .. 346,385
1916 . . 137,096 j
The declared profits are, nevertheless, greater
for the past year than for any period prior
to the war. Yet not very much greater. If
something substantial has not been put to
internal reserves, The Bulletin would be
inclined to say that the excess profit
at present is not sufficient in view
of the increased risks. The outlook
has no doubt been vastly improved by
the purchase, announced during the past
week, of the Australian wool clip for two
years certain at a minimum of 15d. per lb.,
apparently f.o.b. Whether the M.L. clip will
be taken is not yet clear; but in any case
the minimum market value for at least two
years ahead is fixed. And the fixing of the
wool clip at so high a level automatically
ensures a high price for mutton and lamb car-
cases ; and these are the lines that mostly
concern Maoriland. Further, prices for but-
ter and most other foodstuffs will be affected
by the prices. for meat. Timber also will
certainly continue in keen demand. These
things, while helping to maintain the cur-
rent profits of the B.N.Z. and other financiers
and dealers in exchange, will also stabilise
land values, and that will lessen the risks of
heavy and numerous capital losses.
After previous wars one thing has been
noted: the rapidity with which agricultural
countries recovered. This time it will pro-
bably be noted that they will suffer least
from reaction. It will be the manufacturing
countries which will be thrown into greatest
confusion through the sudden diversion of
the world’s plants to peaceful production and
the stoppage of munition-manufacture at
almost any price that those interested chose
to ask. The market values of a thousand
things will fall rapidly ; and in that fall there
is the genesis of serious financial trouble. We
shall not escape some of that. It is the
easiest thing in the world to sell at a profit
on a rising market: it isn’t at all easy to
avoid losses on a falling market. This is
the one thing that can be said in justification
of what is known as profiteering. The trader
who has made profits during the past two
or three years and hasn’t laid some of those
profits by for contingencies is taking risks.
And, though for the reasons given above the
outlook is vastly improved for Australia and
to an even greater extent perhaps for Maori-
land, banks which haven’t put something up
their sleeve to provide for possible commer-
cial failures are likely to be sorry before the
world settles do\Vn again.
How easy it has been for the B.N.Z. to
make increased profits—on paper, at any rate
—this next comparison shows :
1914. 1917. 1918.
Notes in circula-
tion .. ..' £989,882 £2,560,325 £3,312,995
Bills in circula-
tion etc. . . 1,403,457 2,007,968 2,329,559
Deposits. . . . 18,070,613 29,052,791 30,437,937
Liquid assets . . 12,590,187 22,082,870 22,626,103
Advances . . 11,360,486 15,746,485 17,786,755
Premises . . 449,577 450,813 425,824
Since March, 1914, deposits have increased
by no less than just upon 70 p.c.—a simply
amazing growth, attributable, of course,
partly to the very high cash prices which
Britain has been paying for practically
everything that M.L. produces, and partly
and still more largely to the issue of paper
in the shape of notes and national lOU’s.
Maoriland has no Dominion State Bank;
but the Government holds the £750,000 of
pref. capital in the B.N.Z. and guarantees
the £529,959 of 4 p.e. stock. This is a
relic of less happy days when, at the end of
a wild borrowing boom, the pistol was held
at the head of the Government and it was
ordered to fork out or take the consequences.
It forked out, and the consequences have
been very pleasant. On its £500,000 of
“A’’ pref. capital it gets 10 p.c.; on its £250,000
of “B” pref. capital it collects 15 p.c. ; and a
shareholder who wants more than 15 p.c. on
pref. capital secured by £1,000,000 of
ordinary capital and nearly 2-J millions of
reserves, is hard to satisfy. A further re-
sult of the bank’s connection with the Gov-
ernment is a very big exchange business.
Because of the large payments made in Eng-
land and the comparative absence of shipping
and imports, money at call and short notice,
Government securities and other securities
in London now amount to no less than
£12,153,745, with another £259,136 in tran-
sit. In 1914 the assets in London (apart
from bills) were £5,076,749. Including the
London holdings and public securities held
in M.L., the liquid assets are over 62 p.c.
of the total liabilities —an indication that the
bank is holding itself well in hand. While
the deposits, as before shown, have increased
by £12,367,000, advances are only £6,426,000
greater. Liquid assets are up £10,036,000.
Granted, then, that the increase in the
figures is largely due to conditions which
are not stable, it can at least be said
that the B.N.Z. appears to be so man-
aging its affairs that it will not only come
well out of the ordeal, but be in a posi-
tion to help deserving lame dogs over the
stile.

This is the half-year of probably the most
successful concern of its class in Sydney—
or, for that matter, Australia. True, in
years gone by other land and building cos.
have shown big profits on paper; but they
didn’t wash up well. This seems to be the
other sort of co. For instance, its assets
are valued at £323,019, and of that lot only
£43,207 is freehold property; and as that
includes fine city office premises, the book
value of estates and unsold remnants of
estates must be infinitesimal. Further, the
co.’s total liabilities, including provision for
taxation, are only £30,391; so that it can’t
be embarrassed by depositors and other
creditors dropping in for their money and
compelling the directors to sacrifice assets.
The co. could, in fact, pay off all its deposi-
tors with the proceeds of its Commonwealth
Loan investment. No doubt it carries risks:
“Borrowers’ and purchasers’ balances”
total £253,385. Six months ago the direc-
tors said as to this item : “Of the total sum
over 80 p.c. is secured on rent-producing
properties, the remainder representing bal-
ances on sales of land. Ample provision has
been made for possible losses.” The co.
looks, therefore, nice and snug. What sort
of a future it will have will depend mainly
upon the assistance the Commonwealth gives
new and old industries. With Protection
and a saner view as to the amount of work
a man ought to do, the co. should still be kept
busy, while avoiding any capital losses
worth mentioning. Its reserves, notwith-
standing frequent transfers to capital, are
so solid that 5 p.c. earned by its own funds
is equal to 9 p.c. on its capital.

Sydney liad a little boom in company flota-
tions in 1910, and this was one of the con-
cerns of that period. W. E. Smith, who had
built up the business, played a lone hand;
and when he went the organisation was not
there to carry on. Yet in a case of this
kind goodwill is mostly organisation; so
that the greater part of what was paid for
goodwill was paid for a myth. Also, stock
had been badly taken. Presently other
hands got hold of the business; and it was
decided to cut out the dead wood. The capi-
tal had been £75,000 in £1 shares. This was
written down to £45,000 by wiping out Bs.
per share; and then a fresh start was made.
Stocks have been hard to get; on the other
hand, trade generally has been so brisk in
Sydney, owing to artificial stimulation, that
in this and other lines it hasn’t been difficult
to pass on the extra costs. Hence the
steady improvement in profits. And, as the
next table shows, in the financial position
also:—
April, 1915. Oct., 1917. April, 1918.
Liabilities ..£23,669 £20,642 £19,863
Stock . . . . 32,155 38,355 34,766
Book debts . . 14,255 15,307 18,220
Premises and plant 15,174 13,027 12,55.3
Cash and sundries 8,214 7,694 9,696
Meantime a dividend averaging about 5 p.c.
on the written-down capital has been paid.
It is nothing to feel gay about. Certainly
the latter half of the period has been much
better than the former half; but, taking all
the circumstances into account, the figures
don’t hold out much promise of anything
extra-good under the sub-normal and normal
conditions to be faced presently. The co.
is in a business where there is very keen
competition, largely from concerns with
small overhead charges; and its premises,
though extensive, are not well placed to
catch the trade in small stationery lines in
which there has been large profit of late.

This co. and W. E. Smith’s are in the same
category; and there are others of about the
same age which have had the same life his-
tory. Actually there isn’t a trace of the
old A.B.C. business left: the two shops it
had (on short leases) in Pitt-street are
closed—one was found almost at once to be
unprofitable. The directors , (the co. is in
the. Arthur Rickard stable) lately, therefore,
decided to lop off the dead wood here, too,
and during the half-year goodwill was writ-
ten off (£9384) and plant written down
(£1116). That meant writing off £10,500
of capital, the £35,000 shares being reduced
from 20s. to 14s. Yet it is probable that
the goodwill is really worth to-day more
than it was when the co. was formed; for
the sign of “Miss Bishop’s Cakes” may be
just as much a draw as “the A.8.C.” "was
—of late years, at any rate. The “Miss
Bishop” shops now number 14, half of them
opened during the past 18 months. In these
days of very dear provisions it looks as
though there should be more profit, because
less waste, in these light-refreshment shops
than in heavy-lunch rooms. Supervision is
also simpler; and where there is food to
handle supervision may easily make all the
difference between loss and profit. The out-
look for the co. is therefore brighter than it
was ; though close management will still, of
course, be required. The co. is handicapped
with a factory which stands in the books at
£17,000, and cost more; and to finance that
it had to take a mortgage and issue deben-
tures. However, since September, 1915, the
liability on these accounts has been reduced
from £IO,OOO to £SOOO, including £IOOO paid
off since March 31. There are also sundry
creditors for £7330. Presently—after the
debenture debt has been wiped out —the co.
looks as though it will be able to buy to
better advantage. But that will mean, of
couse, keeping a tight rein on dividends and
accumulating cash to pay for stocks.
* * * *
From a Maoriland policy-holder
From your review of the report of the A.M.P.
it is quite evident that policy-holders having
“non-profit” policies with the Society are in a much
better position than “with-profit” policy-holders,
owing to their policies not being affected by increased
taxes, unfavorable death experience and the tendency
to increased expense ratio. One would think it neces-
sary to revise the without-profit rate in order to even
things up a bit. I have no data by me to go on,
but a comparison would be instructive.
It is true that profits or bonuses are for the
moment severely cut; and if present condi-
tions continued it certainly would be more
advantageous to take non-profit policies.
But present conditions will not continue.
As this page has pointed out frequently,
higher interest earnings will rule for years
after the bonuses have ceased to be prejudi-
cially affected by the war; and a large pro-
portion of the earnings will be tax-free.
This point has so impressed some English
offices that one or two have actually
reduced the non-profit rates since the war
started, to bring them more in harmony
with the net with-profit rates.
On the whole question of participating
v. non-participating policies general-manager
Apperly, of the A.M.P., makes these observa-
tions :
If the life assured dies soon after the policy has
been taken out the non-participating policy shows
the better result; but if the life assured lives manv
years the participating policy is the better. No
exact line of demarcation can be laid down; but
take "an example. At age 32 the participating pre-
mium is about 25 per cent, more than the non-
participating. ltoughly speaking, if the accumulated
bonuses are more than 25 per cent, of the sum
assured the participating policy is the better.
In pre-war times the bonuses would have reached 25
per cent, of the sum assured in about 10 years, and
as the great majority of men who reach age 32
live more than 10 years afterwards, the participating
policy was generally the better. At present, rates
of bonus are depressed by war claims more than they
are raised by increased interest, and if the war lasts
•indefinitely it is quite possible that the non-partici-
pating policy will prove the better contract. If, on
the other hand, the war terminates, and rates of
interest keep up, the bonuses will tend to rise
rapidly and the participating policy will be the
better.
The Society has no means of knowing how long the
war is likely to last, or what the future rate of
interest is going to be; but it is keeping a careful
watch on the position and, as it has done in the past,
will readjust its rates of premium on new policies
(those on existing policies cannot be increased) if
such appears necessary or advisable.
In Britain the general, trend of premiums
for non-participating insurance has been
downward during the past 10 years; but
the A.M.P.’s rate is still lower than that of
most British offices, though there has been,
apparently, no change in the A.M.P.’s pre-
miums during the period. Take this com-
parison of premiums per £IOO of insurance
on whole-life non-participating policies:—
Age at entry.... 30 40 50
A.M.P £1 17 9 £2 12 2 £3 17 2
Alliance .. .. 118 10 213 5 318 2
Com. Tnion.. . . 118 6 212 5 315 10
Equitable .. .. 115 9, 2 9 2 311 4
Prudential . . . . 117 7 212 2 316 4
Scottish Widows .. 11911 214 8 319 8
There are offices, such as the Clergy Mutual,
which cater for special classes; and their
rates are exceptionally low (the Clergy
Mutual, for instance, issues a £IOO policy at
30 for £1 15s. Bd.) ; but these don’t fall
properly within this general comparison. Nor
perhaps does the old Equitable, which has
special features, such as non-canvassing for
new business as other offices know it.
* * * *
F. G. Murdoch, secretary of the Wallaroo
and Moonta Cos., has communicated a
scheme for remodelling Federal and State
income taxes. He concludes, of course, that
the two ought to be collected bv the Fed-
eral authority. But though the Federal form
is based on equity, the overhead charges are
heavy and “it is open to doubt whether the
small taxpayer would not be just as well
off under a less complicated system.” He
suggests:—•
(1) Adoption of a simple graduation scale of tax.
(2) Taxation of dividends of companies at the
source.
On the latter point he says:—
This represents one of the chief differences between
the Federal and State representatives. The Federal
authorities do not agree with taxation at the source
because they think that thereby the small shareholder
pays more heavily in proportion to his income than
the large holder. This is undoubtedly correct; but,
at the same time, the work and expense required by
both the individual taxpayer and the Government to
attain this acme of perfection in taxation are so
great that it is urgent that some compromise should
be arrived at.
A company s profits may be said, roughly, to be
used in three ways:—
(a) Payment of preference and/or debenture com-
mitments.
(b) Payment of dividends.
(c) Establishment of reserves for use in the busi-
ness.
As regards (a), the payments are always of a fixed
nature. It is evident that a co., having issued deben-
tures or preference shares on the basis that certain
taxation be paid by the shareholders, should not be
saddled with this taxation while being forced to
continue to pay the same rate of interest. Conse-
quently it is only equitable that a co. should be taxed
separately on this item and be allowed to deduct an
estimated amount from the interest paid to cover any
extra tax involved.
In connection with ( b ) the suggestion is that this
be treated as an entirely separate item of profit, and

that the dividends be taxed at the source on a gradu-
ally rising scale.
The dividend would be taxed as under, the per-
centage and capital figures being given merely as an
illustration: —
Ist 4 p.c. of capital ( £100,000)= £SOO exempt.
2nd" „ „ ~ = £SOO tax 3d. in £
3rd „ „ ~ £SOO tax 6d. in £
4th ~ ~ ~ = £SOO tax 9d. in £
And so on. On a gradually rising scale of 3d. per
-1 p.c., the tax on the last £SOO of a 10 p.c. dividend
would be ss. in the £, while the amount of the divi-
dend over 10 p.c. could be taxed either at a flat
rate or on a continued ascending scale.
The result would be that cos. could provide, out
of the amount available for each dividend, the tax
required under the Act; there would be no necessity
for the cos. to send in returns of shareholders’ divi-
dends ; the tax would be paid to the authorities in
one amount; the shareholders would benefit by the
graduation of the tax, in which the smaller share-
holders would participate.
Wallaroo and Moonta’s contribution is a
welcome attempt to get over a large diffi-
culty. In theory, the Commonwealth sys-
tem is full of merit. But no system so
obscure that the taxpayer cannot understand
and check his assessments will ever be satis-
factory. Nine-tenths or more of the Com-
monwealth’s taxpayers, it is safe to say,
“take it blind.” A few make an effort to
check the figures. Very few succeed. No
system of taxation of which this can be said
will give satisfaction. More than that, it is
very doubtful whether any system is defen-
sible which does not permit of a complete
check by a person of, at most, average
ability. The present system, then, despite
its theoretical merits, is in practice a failure.
The business of “curves” is, however, not
one of principle, and could be got over
easily enough. The question of taxation
“at its source” or otherwise is very differ-
ent. The Wallaroo suggests as a compro-
mise a simply-graduated tax on the ordinary
dividend, making this payable by the co.
The Bulletin sees grave objections to that.
It would penalise those cos. which have
laid aside large reserves. The co. of that
kind which is paying 10 p.c. may be far
better entitled to consideration than another
which has either capitalised its reserves,
issued a huge proportion of its shares for
goodwill, or paid out every cent it could lay
its hands on and therefore now pays a divi-
dend of only 7 p.c. Before such a method
of graduation could be equitable cos. would
have to be given the right to capitalise their
reserves —which, of course, would mean re-
valuing their assets; for the best reserves
are often not shown in the books.
* * * *
“Ex-Banker” : Fusion of banks here is not
the same thing as the establishment of a
banking monopoly in London by the union of
joint stock banks. Here the Commonwealth
Bank represents the nation, and can prevent
evils likely to accrue from monopoly. There are
too many banks in Australia, and consequently
too many branches. As an illustration, Ben-
digo has 10 branch banks, or five too many.
Five offices could easily and better do the
banking business of 25,100 people. If there
were only nve branches the employees would
be better paid, the expenses considerably less,
and the net profits larger. The amalgama-
tion of the National and Colonial will close
one office there, and the furniture, books,
notes, gold-scales and old coats will be
handed over the fence to the surviving office
within 60 minutes, and the public will be no
worse off, while the manager who is left
behind 'will get a rise in his screw,
and even the “pig-boy” who carries
round the exchange books will get,
or ought to get, another 10 bob a week. The
next amalgamation ought to be that of the
Victoria and Royal. The former Bank of
Victoria, existent before the deluge of 1893,
had to fight in 1852 to get its charter from
Parliament against the opposition of the Bank
of Australasia and the Union, two London
institutions, and the Bank of N. S. Wales.
These rivals succeeded in prohibiting the
Bank of Victoria opening in the other
“colonies” and prevented the bank ever
becoming- a serious opponent. Now if the
Royal Bank buys out the Victoria, the new
concern can open where it pleases and also
shut where it likes. The main figures of a
Royal-Victoria Bank would be, roughly,
deposits, £10,000,000; advances, £9,000,000;
capital, £2,000,000; and reserve funds,
£550.000. There you have a pretty
strong bank. The new bank would benefit
by a strengthened executive and administra-
tive organisation. Of that there can be no
doubt. The Victoria needs fresh blood
higher up and the Royal can supply it. The
mingling of the two Boards of Directors and
of the two staff's of managers w r ould be a
great benefit to the older bank, which has a
good name and a fine connection of cus-
tomers in its home State.
* * * *
The S. Aus. Gas Cos. has had a sort of
Ministerial crisis. It called the shareholders
together to authorise the issue of 6 p.c. bonds
to the value of £50,000, and when that was
done they claimed a prior right to subscribe,
on the strength of the circular issued to
them, which said that forms of application
would be available immediately after the
resolution was earned. The bonds, how-
ever, had all been subscribed in advance, and
the directors claimed that in these difficult
times the only safe course had been to get
them underwritten. The meeting got
angry, but when the Board played the
Parliamentary game, and treated the oppos-
ing motion as a direct want-of-confidence
affair, shareholders said they didn’t mean to
go as far as that, and watered down the
resolution to read that they should have
first chance “in future” and “as far as possi-
ble.” But there is a vast difference between
underwriting a loan, which is merely an in-
surance against failure, and receiving “hun-
dreds of applications,” as in this case, while
most persons interested were led to believe
that they could do nothing till the meeting
had been held. At its best it was a clumsy
piece of management, followed by a clumsier
attempt to excuse it.
Broken Hill South is reconstructing,
partly to put all shares on the same footing,
partly to make the stock handier for market-
ing : it has been quoted at over £ll lately.
The co. is in 200,000 shares, 130,90 S fully
paid, the other 09.092 paid to 9s. Od. ; and
the fully paids will probably have 10s. Od.
refunded to make all alike. The present in-
tention is to cut the shares into quarters.
The 73rd dividend will be paid on June 28;
it will be the usual quarterly os., and will
bring the total distributed to £2,355,000, or
£ll 15s. Od. per share; and there is a liquid
surplus of well over £3 in hand. That monu-
ment to T. J. Greenway hasn’t been erected
yet. You could have bought the South and
the North for a few thousands after it had
been proved that those mines contained
hardly anything but sulphide ores of low
silver value. It was Greenway who tackled
the concentration of this ore. The B. H.
Prop, was much too affluent in those days to
worry much about such “refuse” —the refuse
that the whole of the present South co.’s
dividends have come out of.
* * * *
“Quidnunc”: Victorian Premier Lawson
is to unburden himself at Castlemaine next
week on the brown coal question. There is
every indication that Cabinet intends him to
administer soothing-syrup to both the Kiewa
River and the brown coal enthusiasts. So
Lawson may tell Private Enterprise to go
ahead with the Kiewa scheme, at the same time
saying something to the effect that the State
recognises the value of brown coal, and that
it intends to take into very serious consid-
eration the Morwell deposits with a view to
their economic development, etc., etc. On
the other hand Lawson may be big enough
to see that phrases like that won’t meet
Victorian industrial needs. Electrical steel
enterprises and woollen mills are praying
for cheap power. Most people don’t know
that with a little fixing of grates, not only
in factories but in the domestic stove, brown
coal burns splendidly and has a good calo-
rific value. With black coal at 41s. a ton
to the householder Minister Barnes ought to
have demonstrators round Melbourne show-
ing housewives how to burn the brown stuff.
When he gets time he might consider
the question whether, as a mining mana-
ger, he would tolerate in his own mine
costs like those being recorded at Morwell.
Finally, he might call on the Railway
Commissioners to explain how it is that
in the United States it costs only 10s.
per ton to carry a load of coal 1000
miles, while in Victoria it costs 4s. 6d. to
lift the Morwell product 80 miles. Both
Minister Barnes and Premier Lawson have
the industrial fate of Victoria in their hands
if they are bold enough to grapple the pro-
blem of cheap power. If they don’t care to
undertake the big job themselves they might
delegate the duty to a working trust.
* * * *
. Bendigo Amalgamated has not a monopoly
of the bad luck of the field, for, with its
yields included, the whole district produced
only 23,4270 z. for the past five months. This
compares with 48,0820 z. for the same period
in 1916 and 65,7710 z. in 1913. Unless some-
thing is done on the side-lines, or Teddy
Dyason’s luck changes, there isn’t much hope
of an improvement either, because the scrap-
ping of plants at so many mines means an
end to tributing or prospecting from them
on to the minor saddles.
* * * *
After having a look over the Glengarry,
outside Bendigo, director John Northe.v
flashed this brief, enthusiastic message over
the wires: “Good, quite all right.” At the
surface the Glengarry didn’t count for much.
Bhoots were short and the reef small and
ill-defined, so that the formation looked to be
squibby. Prospector Grant, who helped
Herbert Daly’s Jubilee to see the light,
declares he has better promise at the
Glengarry than he got at the surface at that
venerable old dividend-payer. If correct, John
and Paul Northey have picked a cheap min-
ing line. As for bottom-level developments,
the few feet of driving done on the 2Ut. of
stone exposed doesn’t disprove that surface
work was wrong any more than it proves the
accuracy of the “quite all right” tip.

* * * *
Perth’s recently-formed Mining Associa-
tion has started to do things. Headed by
president A. E. Morgans, it has set out to
secure the amendment of mining and taxa-
tion laws so that capital will be attracted.
A proposal to make a canvass for funds is
now under consideration. Meantime an
elaborate pamphlet has been issued setting
forth the golden records of the past and the
immense possibilities of the future. There
are 3110 abandoned gold-mines in the West,
and the Association urges that some of these
shows should be given another trial. Atten-
tion is also directed to the possibilities of
Westralian base-metals, which have not as
yet been seriously developed, though produc-
tion to date exceeds 5f- millions in value.
* * * *
The Golden Butterfly has abandoned its
Blue Streak option at Tuckabianna (W.A.).
The co. wanted a year’s free extension;
but the vendors asked £7OO cash, a
further £2300 at the end of the year, and
also stipulated that the shaft should be sunk
to 200 ft. The Butterfly directors did not
take long to decide that the cash-box couldn’t
stand the strain.
* * * *
. American oil men are beginning to talk in
billions about the oil-shale industry of
Colorado, Utah and Nevada. The growing
shortage of well oil has galvanised the oil
people into activity in locating shale deposits
and in testing them with new processes.
Some of the results are remarkable, as high
as 100 gallons of oil per ton of shale having
been distilled at the testing laboratories of
the U.S. Geological Survey. What is possi-
ble in Utah ought to be feasible in N. S.
Wales. About eight new methods of extract-
ing the oil, tar and sulphate of ammonia are
being tried officially, though none of the new
inventions seems to have been used except in
laboratory work. The Americans appear to
envy the success of the Scottish shale-mines,
which from reefs two to six feet wide at
depths of 1800 ft. pay from 10 to 60 p.c
dividends. During 1916, the Scotch shale
cos. treated 3,500,000 tons, from which thev
produced 80,500,000 gallons of crude oil -
of marketable products (in gallons) : motor
S M iri o’o 9,9.9’9,9,2 ’ naphtha, 4,480,000 ; burning
oil, 22 000,000; gas or fuel oil, 13,100,000 •
and lubricating oils, 11,000,000. There
were also. the paraffin wax and sulphate
of ammonia. The industry is a fine field
a j ® new . Board of Trade to tackle.
And it isn’t likely to neglect the opportunity,
lhe vice-president, who no doubt will have
to do most of the bullocking, allows him-
seft to be an enthusiast on this question of
seeking for a of oil in Australia.
Mount Cuthbert (Q.) did better in May
and produced 222 tons of blister copper, com-
pand with 149 tons in April and 195 tons
m March. The smelter is capable of 10
tons of blister per day. Last month 2984
tons ore were treated ;so about 13* tons of
ore were required to produce a ton of blister,
lhe Hampden average is about 12* tons.
Blair -i&JjSL (Qfr is paying a six-
penny (£2o00). The co. sold £17,363
worth of coal in six months to March 31. but
lie output was hindered by the disastrous
floods of a few months ago. Brisbane is
paying 18s. for the co.’s fully-paid 10s.
shares, apparently in anticipation that the
railway now being constructed from Long-
reach t_o Winton will open up new markets
for Blair Athol coal.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION WITH-
OUT ROUGE OR POWDER.
Many women—both young and old —suffer
greatly from sallow complexions. Some-
times this is the result of climatical condi-
tions, sometimes the cause is constitutional;
hut whatever it be, it is decidedly unnatural
and unpleasant. Bathing in milk occasion-
ally is said to be most helpful and soothing
to the skin, hut just try the following recipe,
which any chemist will make up for you :
Two oz. of rosewater, two oz. flowers of
oxzoin and one djtam tincture of benzoin.
Apply this night and morning with a sponge
after washing, and* let it dry on, then dust
the skin lightly with a soft cloth. This home-
made lotion is one of the best and supersedes
rouge or powder, leaving the skin beautifully
soft, clear and smooth. — Advt.
