A WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, June 17, 1918.
My Dear Arini, —
Lieutenant-Governor J. 11. P. Murray, of
Papua, and his secretary, Leonard Murray,
enjoyed a few days this week in Sydney,
en route to Melbourne. His Ex. is an adver-
tisement of how to live in the tropics and
thrive on the climate. Papua has its hill-
tops, and the tall man (he’s well over 6ft.
high) talks cheerfully of a recent trip to
a peak 9000 ft. above sea level. His son
Pat was with him on the trip. The
youngest son, 18 years of age, left Riverview
College not long ago, and has father’s con-
sent to get into khaki. His big brother was
with an English regiment in 1914, was
wounded twice in France, won the Military
Cross and is stjll exchanging lead with
Fritz. Palestine is his latest address.
Stallholders anxious to supplement their
blue blossom supply raided city florists on
Violet Day, to the well-bred surprise of her
ladyship behind the counter. “Oh, dear, no.
Everything you see, Madam, is ordered.”
Entreaties to Melbourne to send supplies
were invariably answered, “None to be had.”
S O.S. signals were flashed to Moss Vale,
but the baskets of blooms that could be col-
lected from there were emptied in a couple
of hours when the man on the street com-
menced to unload his sixpences. “You can
smell them, if you like,” said one purple-
lmtted girl, “for a consideration; but if
you want to buy the bunch it’ll cost much
more than that.” The Soldiers’ Club girls,
with £4O worth of orders for the blooms, had
to carry on with daphne and sweet-pea.
The hon. treasurer expects that over
£BOOO will be the windfall. Last time the
violet bloomed for the Soldiers’ Club about
£l7OO was gathered in. That was considered
a big thing then, but the Club’s obligations
have grown. Good management holds this
city home for A.I.F. men on a sound basis,
and, keep this to yourself, the Club has no
debts.
The Millions Club, frivolously nicknamed
the “Munch Club” from its inveterate habit
of passing the oyster-patties, fed the depart-
ing French Consul Chayet the other day. Bel-
gium, U.S.A., Italy and Japan were officially
represented at the feast to the indefatigable
Frenchman, who has rarely been absent
from any patriotic or national gathering
since fateful August, 1914. He admits to a
tired feeling. The comparative seclusion of
South America, where the chief excitement
is a revolution before breakfast, will be a
restful interlude. He may even get an occa-
sional evening to himself when he settles
down in Guatemala. Anyway he carries away
the lasting good wishes of Sydney.
By the closing of Australia Day Fund,
the balance, £117,000, goes to the N. S. Wales
branch of the Red Cross. When in 1915
the public emptied its pockets of just over
£BOO,OOO to help our sick and wounded sol-
diers,. even to ourselves our generosity was a
surprise. Since then the fund has earned
about £32,000 interest, in spite of its grad-
ual disbursement. (Quite a lot of people
considered the disbursement to have been too
gradual.) Russell French took the chair at
the wind-up.
After long misery as a prisoner of war
in Germany, Lieut. Riley, a son of the Arch -
bishop of Perth, who sometimes visits us,
is in Holland. When the first guns boomed
lie was a Rhodes scholar at Oxford, and at
once joined up with the British “Contempt-
ibles.” The young man is now doing work
for the British News, a paper recently
born in Amsterdam.
The learned Macmillan Brown, of Maori-
land. is a visitor. He unpacked his kit last
week.
Archdeacon Gunther, good Australian,
who christened, bound in wedlock and buried
half the Anglicans in Parramatta district for
over 80 years, died a few days ago, aged
80. In recent years he was a dignified
figure at semi-ecclesiastic functions in
Sydney. lie was one of the kindest of men
and the easy prey of the burr which fastens
on to the coat of a soft-hearted cleric. He
was the son of Archdeacon Gunther, of the
Mudgee district.
Sister Eileen King, an Australian who
was severely wounded in France a few
months ago, is a daughter of a former
Deputy-Commissioner of Railways in
Queensland. The last mail brought word
that she’s out of hospital and regaining her
strength.
John Lemmone’s cheerful smile greeted
Melba when she arrived on Sunday.
Since she left here nine months ago
she has had a strenuous time, for,
besides heavy operatic and concert work,
she has been busy for the Red Cross
and other patriotic shows. She announces
that she is full of schemes which she will
put into operation with all possible speed
for the good of Billjim. On the boat
she organised a concert and an auction-sale
which raked in £4OO for the Red Cross. Alex.
Sass, who also returned to Australia, pre-
sented the songbird with a souvenir pro-
gramme gaily painted, which she sold for
many pounds, along with some new pictures
of her in operatic roles.
The prima donna rushed off to Melbourne
before Sydney had more than a glimpse of
her. She is back in Australia sooner than
was expected. Of her triumphal tour in
U.S.A. and of a chapter of accidents, includ-
ing the explosion of a German bomb in a
Chicago theatre which failed to do much
damage, she has engaging recollections. New
York hadn’t heard her in opera for years.
For her first-night performance in the Lex-
ington Theatre, “Faust,” the crowd began to
gather outside early in the afternoon. It was
a bitter winter day, and those in the long
queue clung to their bit of kerb throughout
a snowstorm. The tenor was the brilliant
Muratore, the French opera star.
Boston’s famous Symphony orchestra con-
ductor, Mueke, an elegant Hun, has been
interned, and the noted English musician, Sir
Henry Wood, engaged in his place. In Bos-
ton, by the way, the Australian skylark
sang at a concert to help the Halifax Dis-
aster Fund. At Santa Barbara (Cal.) the
Lilydale lady rented a mansion and kept it
filled with guests. In the house-party was
Lady Colebrook, a godchild of the late King
Edward and Queen Alexandra. She was
a famous beauty and notable as one of the
first Englishwomen to make munitions under
exactly the same conditions as a mechanic.
To contradict unreliable rumor the War
Chest shop-workers in George-street state
that they carefully avoid underselling the
trade or cutting into the price list of any
business. “In fact, the cost of the ingredi-
ents which we use in our cakes,” says Mrs.
A. Morris, in charge of the venture, “would
alone prohibit our undermining the ordinary
prices. We must make a margin of profit,
and even the donations in kind wouldn’t
warrant us ‘almost giving things away.’ ”
Every shilling made, barring for the rent,
goes to provide soldiers’ comforts.
The American-Australian League of Help
(Mrs. Brittain, president) has packed up
its gear and flitted into a new depot, 10-Car-
rington-street, not very far from its old
haunt in Wynyard-street. Sweets and home-
made candy, will be the League’s specialties
on Italy’s Day. June 28.

applause. She has dramatic fire, Mrs.
Ferrari Passmore’s night of opera, at the
Royal on June 21, is for the fund of Italy’s
Day. The photo of Emma is Judith
Fletcher’s work. The second act of Tosca
is also on the programme.
The C. of E. Boys’ Grammar had their
garden .fete officially “kicked off,” as one
young rip put it, by Mrs. Wright, wife of the
Archgaiters. The boys with the blue and
white hatbands are raising funds for a
Memorial Sports Ground. The sward is
near Nortlibridge, and will be a lasting and
fitting memorial to old boys who have fallen
in the defence of all that is good in man. Ex-
banker Tom Dibbs, who delights in these car-
nivals of youth, and the Badham triplet—the
venerable mother and two daughters—were
in the crush. It was a crush. An extra
hairpin would have stopped the traffic.
A big crowd poured into the Town Hall on
Saturday to see the film production of Am-
bassador Gerard’s book. “Four Years in
Germany.” In the official seats were Consul-
General and Mrs. Brittain, who left Hunland
in June, 1914. She recognised the accuracy
both of the portraits and the country in
which the most dramatic scenes were cast.
Consul Watteauw was also in the audience,
a silent watcher of some of the agony ot
Belgium. Mayor Joynton Smith spoke an
introductory piece. Consul Shimizu and his
dainty little missus, Consul Chayet, Deputy
Town Clerk W. G. Layton and a pretty
daughter, and Lieut.-Governor Murray ot
Papua (half a head above the average citi-
zen) and son Pat were also visible.
Isabel Ramsay, of the Inky Way, who
leaves shortly for ’Frisco, to attend a news-
paper folks’ conference, is going through a
course of Pekoe farewells. She hopes to
have a peep at England before returning to
Australia.
Peter Dawson has packed up his songs,
and will presently kiss his Annette (the
buxom lady who is Mrs. Peter) good-bye.
And then for the wars! He had a rousing
reception at his concerts during the week in
the Town Hall.
This is a recent
photo of artist
Charles Bryant,
lately added to the
list of “official”
painters who hope to
reproduce scenes in
which the glory of
France will live on
canvas. The Sydney
man has been away
for many years. Ten
of them were spent
in hard study with
John I-lassall and
the famous marine-
painter Olssen.
Bryant’s work is
familiar in the
London Royal x\cad-
emy and the Paris
Salon. The artist was rejected for active
service in the earlier days of the war.
Col. J. L. Shellshear, one of the most
enthusiastic artillery-officers Australia has
sent oversea, is now in charge of an English
hospital while someone else handles his be-
loved guns. There is a serious shortage of
medical men on the other side, and as Shell-
shear happened to be a doctor when at home
in Albury, he had to reluctantly leave his
roaring 18-pounders and take up the stetho-
scope again.
Winifred Burston's pianoforte recital at
the Conservatorium this week was a triumph
for the Australian girl. She played bril-
liantly, from Brahms (on a Handel theme),
without the score. The Liszt Sonata in B
minor and a Chopin finale was also played
from memory. A profusion of flowers was
handed up to the musician.
Among June birthday presents, counting
the King’s Birthday gifts in, the best came
to a woman in North Sydney. She is Mrs.
Donaldson, wife of the captain of the
Matunga—the B.P. boat that the Wolf
seized on the New Guinea coast and subse-
quently sank. After months and months of
silence and suspense word came that the
ship hadn’t foundered with all hands; then,
after some more months, a cable from him
that he was a prisoner in Germany. And
now. on his birthday, Mrs. Donaldson has
had a postcard, the first word from his own
hands, dated March 24. He was at Heidel-
berg then, in “a large, good camp.” “There
are three tennis courts,” he says, “and I
have just joined the club for the summer.”
“Jim” Allison, widower, is once more in-
stalled at the Union Club. He came back
to Sydney this week after pleading in vain
with Uncle Sam for a passport to cross the
Atlantic. He had a good reason for wanting
to get across. His young son has just got
back to Holland after three years in the
hands of the Hun. but this did not move
the stony-hearted Yanks ; so dad came home
to wait. •
The Beith family are home again after
thrilling times in England and New York.
The decorative eldest daughter is doing
V.A.D. work in London.
About 150 Roman “soldiers” and half a
dozen chariots (Walter Bentley’s idea) will
parade the streets on Italy’s Day. Part of
the procession will (d.v.), include a full-
sized gondola, built of plaster. Maybe this is
by way of making a horrible example of the
Federal Government. Mrs. Plogstead’s
liandy-girls in Martin-place will trade spa-
ghetti, hot waffles and Canadian maple,
civility, smiles and coffee. If you want more
than that you’re an unreasonable glutton.
Lieutenant Sam McCaughey, nephew of
Sam, M.L.C., is home on six months
leave. He has been in the trenches for over
three years. During one of his scanty rests
he found time to carry Una MacKelar, sister
of Mrs. Dr. Dunhill. The bride is in Eng-
land.
The China Inland Mission was last week
entertained by a returned missioner, J. H.
Edgar, F.R.G.S., with strange tales of old
Tibet. The job of handing round tracts to
the pig-tailed community is accompanied
by many and varied adventures. How
the lady missionary gets on when the local
population is rushing around with an axe is
always a marvel to her sisters who find the
boarding- of a George-street tram a suffici-
ently dangerous feat.
H. I. Moffat, of the Inky Way that runs
by the shipping newspaper offices, is the
proud daddy of a young war veteran who
has three years’ active service and the Mili-
tary Cross to his credit. Lieut. L. I*.
Moffat belongs to the Ist Reserve Division
of King Edward’s Horse, and he’s been
fighting in Italy and France. A younger
son, Alan, is with the A.L.H. in Palestine.
At a bag and basket stall in Martin-place,
under the wing of the French-Australian
League, Cora Randal Carey will hang out
the sign of the tambourine on June 28. It’s
about the size of a shilling with the Red
Cross painted on its face. Suspended by
an inch of Italian colors it is one of the
gauds Sydney will dangle from its button-
hole when the Day dawns.
If Cappelli is going to link up with his
countrymen in Italy, as stated a few weeks
ago, he is not saying good-bye just yet. A
business announcement of his “special
engagement” with a local company inciden-
tally warns off any worshippers who were
ready to weep adieu.
Mr. and Mrs. James Smith, of Colebrook,
the house that sits on a shoulder of Bellevue
Hill, have handed over £2703 for another
Australian battleplane. It will fly as “The
Joan.”
Entered up to the account of the Kaiser
another Australian boy of great promise—
Muirton W. Forbes, killed in France before
he was 20. If Sydney Church of England
Grammar was asked what three boys of the
last few years it would like to be judged by,
it would certainly put Forbes in the list.
Besides passing his exams, with credit he
played cricket with the school’s first XI in
1914-5-6, and was captain in ’l6. He was
captain of the school’s XV the same year,
in the school Eights, and lieut. in its cadets.
Enlisting as soon as he could, he went off as
a private and won his commission. His
mother, a widow, had already mourned one
boy.
The Rafalewski duet—the lady hits the
stars with her top notes as soprano Lili
Sharp—are moving off this map. A 12
months’ contract to wander in coffee-colored
lands was the bait. With them goes a Mos-
man girl, Eva Lea-Coates.
Latest London advices about Winifred
Purnell are that the young pianist, who has
studied hard during her years abroad, gave
a successful concert in the air-raided city
to an audience of the proper sort. There
were no bombs to drown the applause.
- The friends of Australian bird-men at the
Front better pin this up. Christmas gifts
are being packed at 59a Wentworth Avenue,
and the packers want help. Grace Stafford’s
Strollers are booked to appear in St. James’s
Hall to benefit the fund on June 26.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

Look, Mother! See if the tongue is
coated; this denotes sluggish
liver and bowels.
Mother! You’re child isn’t naturally cross
and peevish. See if the tongue is coated;
this is a sure sign that its little stomach,
liver and bowels need attention at once.
When listless, pale, feverish, “stuffy” with
cold, throat sore; when the child has tainted
breath and doesn’t eat, sleep or act naturally
or has stomach-ache or diarrhoea, remember,
a gentle liver and bowel cleansing should
always be the first treatment given.
Nothing equals California Syrup of Figs
—“Califig”—for children’s ills; give a tea-
spoonful, and in a few hours all the waste
matter, sour bile and fermenting food clogged
in the bowels pass out of the system, and
you have a healthy and playful child again.
All children love this harmless, delicious
fruit laxative, and it never fails to effect a
good “inside cleansing.” Directions for
babies, children of all ages and grown-ups
are plainly on the bottle.
Keep it handy in your home. A little
given to-day saves a sick child to-morrow,
but get the genuine. Ask your chemist for
a bottle of “Califig”—California Syrup of
Figs—then look and see that it is made by
the California Fig Syrup Company. Refuse
substitutes. All chemists sell “Califig”—

Melbourne. June 17, 1918.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Melba arrived on Monday morning and
had a tremendous reception at the
station. Ordinary passengers had the great-
est difficulty in getting clear. The singer
looked younger than when we saw her
last. She was dressed in a cutely-
tailored greatcoat the color of burnt earth, a
neat fur collar, and a jaunty little closely-
fitting hat with wings. The faithful Lemmone
travelled over from Sydney with her and son
George, wrapped in a huge overcoat and a
beatific smile, held her elbow all the way to
the motor-car. Sister Mrs. Lempriere made
herself noticeable in the crowd by a black
velvet belltopper “crewelled” with gay
flowers. Stella Power, smart as a French-
New York-Australian, got a special hug from
Melba. The Conservatorium was in force,
and god-mother wanted to go and practise
scales at once. George Allan and Fritz Hart
carried her off to lunch. Then home to
Coombe.
Genthelp Conant’s movie has been doing
the rounds of the suburbs, where the plutoc-
racy makes vulgah mirth at the badly-made-
up and awkward sassiety amateurs. How-
ever, he’s pleased with himself, and he is
plotting about five more photo plays.
John Madden, the late Chief Justice, left
£29.082. The widow, who is a daughter of
Melbourne’s one-time City Solicitor E’rank
Stephen, will have the income of the estate
for life, and at her death the whole goes to
the children.
The Ladies’ Field, talking of our old
friend Eugenia Stone, nowadays Lady
Doughty, mentions that she has got some
of the way back to printers’ ink: “She has
published in England a charming little book
called ‘The Cheerful Way,’ and contributes
articles to the Grimsby Daily Telegraph,
of which she is a director and part-pro-
prietor.”
The death of Col. Windle St. Hill recalls
that in ’Bl, when he was secretary to Gover-
nor Weld, of Tasmania, Lady W. ran to
him one day with the news that while walk-
ing through one of the passages she had seen
a tall stranger standing on his head in a
room. St. Hill darted away to investigate,
and found that the inverted party was not an
escaped madman but an athlete doctor friend
of the family, who was performing by special
request of her Ex.’s small son.
The lovely Baxter-Bruce widow has
arrived in Melbourne from Sydney after an
exciting few years abroad. She was on her
way to Germany when the world-storm
burst, but she took the right turning at once
and has been war-working ever since. Her
daughter is still in London doing V.A.D.
jobs.
Rupert Clarke is building himself a perfect
bungalow overlooking Sydney Harbor.
Madame Rumor repeats that daughter Amy,
whose husband is at the war, is coming out
to live with dad, and, of course, Madame
Rumor knows all about it.
Victorian squattocracy will be flocking to
Sydney at the end of this month for the ram
sales, many of its female members taking
the opportunity to snatch a holiday away
from Red Cross work. Otway Falkiner, of
Widgiewa, came down to Melbourne and in-
stalled his wife in a flat at Mallock House
before rushing Sydneywards.
Philip Russell, of Carngham, who tore for
Britain as soon as the war started, has
reached his majority with a British regiment.
He’s a son of the late James Russell, of
Osborne House, who helped to make sheep-
historv in the Western District.
John Corlett Teare, of Bennie, Teare and
Co., has raised a family which deserves well
of its country. The eldest boy, Lieut. Athol
Muir, has just returned with a Military
Cross, won for doing his job, though badly
wounded, under a stream of fire. The second
son, Lieut. John Stewart, of the R.F.A.,
sleeps on a French battlefield, and the family
treasures his Cross. Captain Phil, the third,
went from Gallipoli to France with the gun-
ners, and a sister works as hard for the Red
Cross as any of them. The lads were Mel-
bourne Grammar boys.
Of the five children of the late James
Graham, the actuary and insurance man who
died lately, four are in touch with the enemy;
the only one who isn’t is the younger
daughter. The eldest son is a prisoner in
Germany; the second ’listed in September,
1914, and, though wounded thrice, is still
battling in Palestine; the third is an Artil-
lery two-star; and the elder daughter has
been a staff-nurse at Colchester Military Hos-
pital for three years. Father, too old to go
to the war, gave his last three years almost
wholly to war work. He was a typical
generous Scot —granite on one side and velvet
on the other —and reputedly one of the very
ablest actuaries in Australia.
Melbourne has discovered that it has
enough citizens who went to school with
Plaig and Birdwood to surround a medium-
sized dining-table, and has set about forming
an Old Cliftonians’ Association. Dr. W. H.
Lang is one of the “old boys” of Clifton Col-
lege now settled in Melbourne, and Wynd-
ham Heathcote, the Independent parson,
who before the war fell out with the Angli-
can Church, is another. The most venerable
“old boy” yet discovered is a gentleman with
the mellow English name of Bean-Byard.
They’re everywhere. The William Gibbses,
of the Commercial Bank at Shepparton, saw
their boy R.R. off with the first lot; but,
tired of the long wait in Egypt he grabbed
a chance to join the British, and was sent to
Nigeria. He now wears his stars with the
West African Frontier Forces at Kano,
where the great caravans used to come down
from the Sahara, and where you can still
pick up odd bits of Crusaders’ gear.
War is very brutal; but it makes the
Aussie, at any rate, good cobbers with the
lame dog that wants a lift. One late day in
Bourke-street I saw a hunch-backed fiddler,
squatted in the gutter upon a camp-stool,
playing lively tunes while the shoppers drifted
indifferently by. His cap, containing 3d.
and a copper, lay on the ground. Later,
passing the same spot, I saw a crowd of 100
people around the fiddler. He was still play-
ing his lively tunes, but now beside him
stood a returned soldier, holding the player’s
cap for the crowd’s contributions. Cash was
just pouring in, and the soldier looked as
pleased as though he had drawn a starter in
Tatt.’s.
An ex-Melbourne lad, Eric Mackennal,
turned up recently in the uniform of a
Sammie at big brother Bertram’s studio at
St. John’s Wood, London, en route to
France. Melbourne pals had lost trace of
Eric for some time. It seems that he has
been holding down an important railway job
in California.
Keith Dawson, the handsome young cap-
tain, lately of Birdwood’s staff, and A.D.C.
to General Sir Newton Moore, is now Lieu-
tenant Dawson, of the Indian Army. (All
officers recede a step in rank on joining that
army, I hear.) He is at present stationed
among the heights of Beluchistan and is
busily learning five native dialects at once
(five different tribes are represented in his
regiment). His uncle is Private Peter
Dawson, who has just sung his final fare-
wells prior to leaving for the Front.
General Williams, who was our chief
military medico when war began, is back in
Melbourne.
One of the silk flags given by her State
Ex. as a stimulus to Red Cross cash-raising
has been waved to the tune of 1000 guineas.
The sight of this particular flag, displayed
among the bank-accounts of Colac and its
surrounding pasturages, has been like a red
rag to a Yarra-banker. Mrs. Angus Urqu-
hart, of Moondah, representing her neighbor-
hood’s Red Cross branch, was responsible for
the final flap, with a contribution of £lOO.
Lieut.-Colonel Murdoch, Australian Red
Cross Commissioner in London, who is get-
ting six months’ leave, is coming home for a
glimpse of Australia. Lieut.-Colonel Hay-
ward will transfer from France to England
to fill the gap.
To support its June appeal the Austra-
lian Comforts E’und will run a shop from
June 21 at 159 Collins-street. The Irvine
dame will play the overture on the cash-
register on the opening day.
The Adelaide despatch :
The Trench Comforts Shop, launched something
over a year ago, is surprising even the optimistic
committee responsible for it. For one week the
takings were £l5O. The Port Adelaide Comforts
Shop is averaging about £ 25 a week, and has had
to move to bigger premises.
We’ve had an orgy of patriotic concerts —one for
the “Thousand Voices” choir, one for the Y.M.C.A.
Army Department, one arranged by the Stock Ex-
change for the same fund, another with its takings
for the Artillery Trench Comforts Fund and the
Y.M.C.A., one at Walkerville for the Returned Sol-
diers’ Orchestra, and one for the Mission to Seamen
in the Queen’s Hall on Friday night. Even Sunday
wasn’t immune, and that day was fixed for the sixth
of Mr. Grigg’s annual orchestral concerts for the
Minda Home.
The latest private house to succumb to the demand
for flats is the Josiah Symon home at Fitzroy.
Lieut. Walter Price, M.C., son of our first Labor
Premier, has been appointed receiver of revenue in
the Produce Department.
A Military Cross has come to Captain Colin Stew-
art, eldest son of the Engineer-in-Chief, who left
Sydney with* the First Division in October, 1914.
Another S. Australian who has won distinction is
Major (now Colonel) Eric Conran, son of 11. L. Con-
ran, once of Glenelg and now of England. Soon
after war broke out he was flying in Belgium,
where he was severely wounded. Subsequently he
was sent to German East Africa and Egypt, and he
is now to take command of a wing at Huntingdon,
where he was aviation squadron commander before
he last went to France.
Miss d’Erlanger has gone to Melbourne, where she
is to stay for a week or so with the Munro
Fergusons.
Died at Malvern on Saturday W. W. Wilby, Office
Clerk in the House of Assembly for some years. He
was born in India (where his father was at one
time Commander-in-Chief of the troops in Ceylon),
educated in England and came to South Australia in
’ B °. . ,
The U-boats have apparently done for two out of
three cases of pictures that M ill Ashton was expect-
ing from England, including sketches of his own, and
canvases by Streeton, Julius Olsson, Lambert and
others, bought for J. McGregor, of Sydney.
Adelaide put out both hands to welcome Matron
Margaret Graham, who, when war began, had had
20 years’ experience on the city’s hospital staff, and
was head-nurse of the S.A. Military District. She
left with the first contingent, gained a mention in
Egypt, and had the Royal Red Cross pinned to liei
last September.
Three musketeers whose record is worth while are
sons of Mrs. W. Schenscher, of Saddleworth. Her-
bert has just come back with two wounds and a
Military Medal. Ernest has been twice wounded.
Fred has campaigned in Egypt, Gallipoli and France,
and has the D.C.M., gained for rescuing wounded
under fire.
One of the Sproule family, Captain James,
M.C., of the Royal Field Artillery, lately in-
vested in a hoopful of diamonds for the
adornment of Gladys Muriel Chatto, daugh-
ter of Thomas Chatto, Hillside, Estree,
Herts, England. This extravagance was in
celebration of Gladys Muriel’s promise to say
“Yes” at the altar.
Lieut. Walter Turner, a Victorian on ser-
vice with the A.1.F., induced Delphine
Dubuis, a chic Parisian girl, to walk down
the aisle of an old Chelsea Church with him
one day last April. The ’groom, who in
1917 published a book of verses, was sur-
rounded by budding literati at the cake-
cutting.
The English marriage market has absorbed
another Australian worth having—Major Eric
Arundel Wilton, machine-gun officer of the
16th Battalion, who has became .engaged to
Kitty Hanrahan, of Limerick. Eric, who is a
cheerful warrior of 26, was reported killed
in Gallipoli. But it wasn’t so bad as that,
and after convalescing in Italy he crossed to
ETance and won the D.S.O. last year. He
is the eldest son of the Warrawong Wiltons,
of Mount Macedon.
Our old friend F. R. Spofforth, the demon
bowler, protruded himself into the spotlight
in England the other day, at the marriage
of his pretty daughter Vera. Vera, who
is a topnotch tennis-player, said she would
to Major P. S. Youle, of the Royal Garrison
Artillery, at the village of Walton-on-
Thames.
Eily, youngest daughter of the late J. A.
Miller, of West’s Pictures, who was married
the other Wednesday, has been honeymoon-
ing in Sydney. For the ceremony at the
Carmelite Church, Middle Park, the bride
was arrayed in white, including the velvet
hat, whilst the one bridesmaid, sister Mollie,
appeared in saxe blue and pale pink. Inciden-
tally, the ’groom, who was present, was clothed
in pride and happiness and sundry fabrics.
He is Captain W. L. Smallhorn, one of the
Home Territory Department identities, and
has done his bit of service with the A.I.F.
An old knee trouble kept him from the
Front, but he is accused of having been a
valued officer in camp in England.
Pretty little Marie Hall, of Bendigo and
Melbourne, is now Mrs. Russell Sheppard,
tlubby is an air ’busman who was not long
ago sent from England to confer with Uncle
Sam’s aviation experts. lie is a brother of
Mabel Russell, who hit the Big Smoke hard
some months back in “Loudon Pride,” a piece
that failed to draw here.
The wonderful bassinet and trousseau pre-
pared for Nellie Mitchell’s grandchild will
not be wasted. They are being brought out
in readiness for a fresh claimant.
Violiniste Leila Doubleday is coming home.
During the last two years she has been
living quietly in French Switzerland, per-
fecting her art, and now she has decided to
risk the U-boats and show her own people
what 10 years of hard study have done for
her. In Vienna, before the war, she was
the star pupil of the famous Rose, who per-
sonally conducted her concerts in that city.
Amongst the musical critics there she
created a sensation by her amazing technique
and musicianship, and when she stormed
London under the management of Landon
Ronald she earned the most extravagant
praises.
An advertisement in last Saturday’s Argus
invited girls of musical talents to join a
ladies’ orchestra for a tour in Java. One
hears strange stories about what happens to
Joans in these unhandy lands, and a little
careful inquiry before packing the old kit-
bag doesn’t do a ewe lamb any harm. Which
doesn't mean that I have any suspicions in
this case, my dear.
Fred Collier is off to Sydney to arrange a
series of eight popular Saturday-concerts,
beginning on July 6.
Stella Power has grown into quite a hand-
some amazonian type. She belongs to a
numerous family whose hopes for the rising
star are greatly heightened since Melba
became fairy godmother to the girl.
The June Appeal has called up the Will-
o’-the-Wisps, another all-girl show which will
appear for patriotic purposes at The Play-
house on Saturday, June 29 On the roster
are Mrs. Probert, Misses Alexander, F.
Darke, H. Deane, S. Hull, F. Moore, M.
Muntz, E. Retalloek, D. Shaw and J.
Tuthill, and a special orchestra batoned by
Miss Mayston. Napoleon Boffard is to lend
his voice.
Miss Farmer, one of the most energetic
V.A.I). young workers at the Wirth Rest
House for invalid soldiers, arranged a con-
cert for the funds last week, and drew a
packed house to the Home’s big entertain-
ment hall. Miss Farmer possesses a pleas-
ing voice herself. The soldier boys know it
well, for she often warbles to them.
The other morning I saw Marie Tempest
shaming the Melbourne dames who refuse
to carry their own parcels. There came
Marie tripping along laden with a bandbox.
It was the nattiest thing in black and white
stripes, and the carrier wore the nattiest
of short-cut morning frocks. 1 wonder if
she would wrestle with an untidy parcel of
vegetables?
Mrs. Guy Post throwing flowers at her
husband was a feature of the opening night
of “The Nigger,” at the King’s. She was in
gorgeous raiment, and shared a stage-box
with our unimpressive Gaud Mayor and his
spouse. It was an impressive sight to
see at the close of, each act a bunch of
vegetation from the leading man’s missus
lobbing at the feet of her solemn hubby. He
had a strenuous time indulging in gymnastic
love-making with Thais Lawton, whose low-
cut evening-gown revealed a very beauteous
pair of arms. Marie Tempest, who on arrival
moved the gallery to welcoming noises, was
in the front of the d.c. with Mrs. Tallis.
Walter Kirby decorated the circle and Taits
of various sizes were visible in all parts of
the house.
Mrs. Bates Post (Adele Ritchie behind
the footlights) is a shapely madame. Her
feet, especially, are subjects for congratula-
tion, small, graceful and with delicately-
arched insteps. The lady is careful that
they are never seen in busted elastic-sides.
At the Bing Girls’ ball they were encased
in gold brocade slippers.
The Defence Department is finding the
V.A.D.’s very handy nowadays. Latterly a
systematic effort has been made to draw on
the reserves of the St. John’s Ambulance
concern, which has trained thousands of
young women in the first principles of mak-
ing the patient comfortable. A few dozen
girls were some time ago put to work at the
Caulfield and Base Hospitals and at Mont
Park; now the suburban and country cen-
tres have been asked to send along trainees.
This call for help is very complimentary to
the Y.A.D. toilers, who have already made
their mark. It certainly isn’t the money that
fetches them. The rate of pay, for a 12-hour
day occupied with work which is officially
described as “not light,” is 18s. —and not a
word has been said about the provision of
the girls’ white uniforms with the Red Cross
on their chests, nor about the blue, soap,
starch and labor necessary for laundering the
same.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

TRY MAGNESIA FOR YOUR STOMACH
TROUBLE.
IT NEUTRALISES ACIDITY AND
PREVENTS FERMENTATION.
Before giving up hope and deciding that
you are a chronic dyspeptic, just try the
effect of a little magnesia—not the ordinary
carbonates, oxides or citrates, but bisurated
magnesia, which you can obtain from prac-
tically any chemist, in either powder or tab-
let form. Take half a teaspoonful of the
powder or two compressed tablets with a
little water after your next meal. It will
neutralise the harmful acid in your stomach,
dispel the wind, soothe and heal your in-
flamed stomach, and quickly relieve your indi-
gestion. Many hundreds of sufferers have
gained relief from this simple remedy,
amongst them Mr. P. V. Anderson, of Cooray.
Queensland, who says: “I had suffered from
indigestion for about five years, and had
tried all sorts of treatment without success,
when a friend advised me to give bisurated
magnesia a trial. I am very glad I did so,
for I experienced immediate' relief, and am
now completely cured of my trouble.” Did
space permit many identical experiences
might be quoted, all tending to prove that, if
you keep your stomach free from harmful
acids by taking bisurated magnesia after
meals, you need never fear any serious form
of digestive or stomach trouble. But, of
course, it must be iisurated magnesia—other
forms might do more harm than good.— Advt.

“Silas Snell”: When we started to sink
th“ shaft of the Blue Peter, out near the State
Forest, Bungaree, we were told of Jennings and
Brett. Neither ever spoke, excepting under compul-
sion, and then with almost incredible brevity. The
two men had beta living within half a mile of each
other in thinly-populated bush country for 20 years
and the tradition was. that once Brett had met Jen-
nings at his slip panels and had said: “Hello ! How
yeh gettin on?” But the legend was covered with
doubt and was much disputed. However, I am in a
position to verify it. I had gone to Jennings over
a contract for slabs, and just then Brett came along,
faltered, as it were, in his walk, and said: “Hello,
Jennings, how’re yeh gettin’ along?” Jennings fol-
lowed Brett with his eye for over 100 yards. He
must have been moved deeply, for he spoke. “Inquisi-
tive 1” he said. “That’s twice he’s asked me.”
“F. Born”: Certain Queensland papers
have been debating of late whether the primitive
Bananaland Warrigal was a cannibal or not. Sup-
pose he was (as our ancestors undoubtedly were, if
you go back far enough), what does it matter?
May he not have been a decent fellow just the
same ? Staniforth Smith, when lost in Papua, fell
into the hands of cannibals, who treated the lost
party with the utmost kindness, fed them and sent
them on their way rejoicing. Smith remarked at
the time: “The great care we had exercised in
seeing that the natives had not been robbed by our
carriers stood us in good stead, and in place of
killing my small and exhausted party, they gave us
all the food they had, although we had nothing to
give in return.” Henry M. Stanley tells us that he
usually selected cannibals for carriers on account of
their superior intelligence and loyalty. Even the
cannibal, if treated decently, does not proceed to
cannibalise you unless your conduct convinces him
that your conversion into dead meat is really the
best use that can be made of you.
* * * *
“Bouragi”: Many of the native customs
mentioned by “Merrigal”, as Samoan or Melanesian
seem to be observed all over the Western Pacific.
The extreme deference to chiefs was practised by
the Maoris and Fijians particularly. In Papua the
Trobriand natives are the only tribe with any regard
for the claims of high descent. They never stand
iu a chief’s presence, much less walk past him. The
custom of placing a stone in a leaf on a path as a
“no thoroughfare” sign is common in Papua. A
favorite trick there is to knot a vine or long grass
across the track as a 'warning to go back. The North-
eastern Papuan welcomes a visitor by feeling over
and pinching the caller’s arms and trunk, remarking
at the same time what splendid condition he’s in.
Coming from a people who have been and are, when
they get a chance, unblushing cannibals, this is
rather disconcerting, however complimentary.

“Dale”: If “Dugout” (B. 30/5/’18) is the
man who suggested that I should put a pickle-bottle
on my boil, will you kindly forward his name and
address? In accordance with the recipe, the bottle
was filled, with boiling water, emptied and applied
to the tumor-. That started it. My neck began to
walk rapidly down the bottle, and the missus ran
to make sure that the insurance, policy was all right.
When she came back little Willie, the twins and
she concentrated on the glass limpet but without
avail, until a tap with a hammer shook it off. To
my surprise my neck did not go with the pieces.
The boil appeared to be the least upset member of
the household.

“Leo Lear”: The Abo. scribbler (B.
9/5/’18)- who took his oath that strychnine would
not slay pigs was correct. It won’t kill the native
rat either. Last year a colony of these little chaps
undermined my asparagus-bed and were fattening
on the roots. I put out strychnine baits, which were
all taken; but the rats remained. Repeated the
dose; same result. But arsenic did the trick.

“Tupla”: Here’s the dinkum oil for scrub-
itch. Take even quantities of sulphur and boric
acid, and mix them with fat or oil and apply. Have
cured Binghis with this when they’ve been a mass of

“Yanco”: “Peta” (B. 16/5/’IS) is right
about the prevalence of noxious weeds at Yanco
(N.S.W.). The irrigation area has alien and noxious
weeds in abundance; but stinkwort is scarcely
known. What “Peta” refers to as covering farms
and lining canal banks and roadways is not stinkwort
but bushy starwort, sometimes referred to locally as
Alfalfa sendalis and stick-weed. This, although an
undesirable plant and very unsightly on irrigable
lands, might perhaps justify its existence elsewhere
as a stock feed, as horses, cattle and sheep feed
freely on it when it is young. Yanco is not
at all singular in the deterioration of its land
through the growth of noxious alien plants, and the
stock-carrying capacity of the State is annually
decreasing through the general neglect 'of theft-
destruction. But droughts don’t destroy the curses
free of charge for lazy Governments and shiftless
councils. The seeds simply lie low and . await return-
ing moisture to show up with increased spread and
profusion.
* * * *
“H.V.E,”: Some little time ago an ama-
teur eelologist asserted that he found minute eels,
quite lively, in the floats of adult eels (female, of
course) taken from streams in the Armidale dis-
trict (N.S.W.). Scientists, however, hold that, as a
cast-iron fact, all fluviatile eels resort to the sea
to milt and spawn. But Sir John Hawkins, in his
notes to an edition of Izaak Walton’s “Compleat
Angler,” published well over a century ago, gleaned
the following from a “woman who had been wife to a
miller for near 50 years, and much employed in the
dressing of eels.” She said that she had never found
any spawn or eggs in the eels she opened, but had
sometimes found living eels in them, about the “big-
ness of a small needle.” On another occasion a
“gentleman of fortune” informed Hawkins that he
observed a “parcel of little creeping things about
the sides and belly of a fine large eel that was going
to be dressed.” They proved to be perfect little
eels, or elvers. Yet science holds that this cannot
be. Writer found a brown eel (Anguilla reinhardtii)
in a dying state in the mud of a dried-up water-
course, and beside it lay over a dozen small eels
from about sin. to 9in. in. length. The exact breed-
ing* arrangements of eels are still imperfectly known.
“Digger”: I had learned some of the new
language before I left home.; but I’m not sure
whether these had got into common use in Aussie:
F. 0., file off, get avvay; - gutser (common as dirt, and
quite respectable now), cropper ( e.g to revert to the
ranks) ; hellova, out of the ordinary; jacks, military
police; joe brown, understand; kiwi guards, artillery
or others with polished leggings; p.0.q., imshi
quickly; pin out, at once, right away; wash out, a
failure; whiz-bang, field post-card; woodbine, a cig-
arette (generally “ ’bine”) ; zero, an indefinite date
ie.g., “we’ll be home at zero”).
* * * *
“Umntu”: The Kaffir laborer in France,
surrounded by civilisation’s ■ horrors and marvels,
forgets them all when he first sees Jeanette milk
the cow. It is not the beauty of Jeanette that smites
him, it is her sacrilege! She is breaking what is
to him a time-honored if not a religious custom. The
woman can hoe and reap, and thatch and daub,
draw the water and hew the wood, and generally be
the beast of burden; but to plough the field, milk
the cows, and put up the framework of the hut are
male prerogatives that even the laziest red polygamist
would not forego.
“Aneb”: If “Kell B.” (B. 9/5/T8) knows
anything about breaking in horses he can’t write
about it. I’m no novice at the game, and let me
tell him that breaking in, and well at that, is far
better rewarded at 30s. per head than “educating”
a horse would be for £3. He evidently knows no
distinction in the three phases of a horse’s curricu-
lum. The animal must be handled before he’s
“broken,” and broken before he’s educated. The
first can be done well in three days with an average
beast, the second in from 10 days to a fortnight,
according to conditions; but the last takes any old
time over three months, and is rarely met with in
Australia outside the principal shows. If “Kell B.”
will educate an unbroken colt for £3 he’s cutting
down wages to a level unheard of hitherto. I’ve
never seen a hard-and-fast system with much to favor
it yet. Like schoolboys, all young horses must go
through the same routine ; some get their certificate
long before others, and it’s there that the school-
master or horsebreaker shows his brains. All large
horseowners are sick of showmen with gears and
“ideas.” Australia is indeed badly off for brainy
breakers, but, notwithstanding regular outbursts of
“Kell B.’s” and other systems, the old, old routine
is the one that survives.

AFTER ALL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For months before, the stranger came,
Up to the very dawn,
They talked him up the paths o’ fame
Till hours when churchyards yawn.
Mum vowed she’d call him Vernon, though;
Dad swore he’d call him Jim ;
Mum told him that his cake was dough;
Dad laughed at Mum’s flash whim 1
He’d be a hero, born an’ bred—-
A big ’Orsetralian bold ;
With eyes of brown, so ol’ Mum said,
An’ with a heart of gold.
Dad swore he’d make him milk the cows,
The porkers’ supper stir;
While ol’ Mum made some solemn vows
He’d be an officer!
Oh, my! The little clothes Mum made—
The jumpers an’ the trews,
An’ snow-white nappies wrapped an’ laid
For little Vernon’s use !. ...
The doctor an’ the nurse are here—
The fuss amounts to this :
The little rascal’s reached this sphere
An’ her front name is SIS!
VICTORIA.
RITA SUN-YA-SEE.

1. Scarcity. —The world’s supply of
metal has been curtailed by its use in

its continued high price after the war.

gated-iron used for roofing is 28-gauge,
and weighs about 851 b. per 100 sq. ft.
4. Rusts, Corrodes. —Iron roofing easily
rusts, a.nd will corrode and disintegrate
by atmospheric gases.
5. Heat. —Iron absorbs the
heat from the sun and transmits it to
the building underneath, and the heat
is retained in the building over night,
even though the temperature is cool
outside,
G. Hard to Handle. —Iron is heavy, and
as i* comes in large sheets it is bulky
to handle, thus requiring more time

"Pick": If you want to hoar a truly
entrancing wild-bird musical performance, try waiting
at dawn beside one of the unfrequented coastal lakes
of the X. S. Wales south coast until you have had the
rare luck to witness a gathering of the black swan
clans. W hat the birds meet for I do not know:
but occasionally, as though by pre-arrangement, they
assemble at daybreak on the shadowy surface of one
of the many lagoons. In regular Y-shaped forma-
tions, with a deep-voiced leader in the van. they
skim along the beaches and over the headlands, and
as each fresh contingent arrives at the rendezvous
a mighty chorus of welcome goes up from the
assembly. W hen thousands have blackened the water,
their massed clarionet band in the morning stillness
is a treat to be remembered for a lifetime. At sun-
rise the gatherings break up and the musicians depart
to their accustomed waters.
“Steve Hart": Stewart the squatter had
300 acres down on Possum Creek which he wanted
fo have ring-barked. So he put up a notice outside
the pub offering Is. 6d. an acre for the job. But
the fossickers on the creek weren't having any under
half a crown. Stewart rose threepence, but still the
miners sat tight. Then one day Bill the bullocky,
who brought the stores (mostly beer) once a fort-
night, said that Stewart had a returned soldier with
a “crook” leg on the job. “It’s that bloke over the
range at Rowdy Flat,” he explained. “The cove that
married Nell Thomas arid went; to the war shortly
after.” “Well, Stewart’s a miserable blank,” said
old Jim, “to take advantage of a man who fought
and bled for him and his 50,000 acres, not to men-
tion his sheep artd c-attle. I’ll bet lie’s only giving
the bloke Is. 0(1.,, or less.” Then, with a sudden
thought, lie asked: “Does the poor bloke go home
on Saturdays?” “Yes,” said Bill; “leaves at dinner
and goes to the Flat till Monday.” Turning to the

the population of Possum Creek paralysed. But. to
give him his due he paid the soldier the one and
nincpence. though the mob didn’t know that till the
bullocky came with more stores.

“A. Fisher”: Great fun being stung by
the small arid inoffensive looking bullrout. Three
to four inches is about his average length, but even
if he is only an inch long he can produce something
special in the way of agony. Shaped like a baby
codfish, he looks as though he has been browned and
burnt in a. frying-pan. A sting from one of his fins,
or an incision by one of the half-dozen almost invis-
ible lancets set. round the outside of the gills, is
calculated to send even strong men
stark rnad for an hour or two and in
some eases for a much longer time.
I got pronged in the second joint of
the first finger of the right hand,
which was rendered useless for a
couple of days. For from 15 to 20
hours the hand and forearm were
paralysed and I had three ligatures
below the elbow to stop the. poison
spreading. I overlooked “first aid’’
when I omitted to gash the finger
and let the blood run ; but then I was
nearly “dilly.” For the first minute
the pain was no worse than a half-
hearted bee-sting. At the end of the
next 60 seconds it was easy to imag-
ine that half a dozen wasps had been
working overtime on the spot. There-
after the agony intensified at a terrific
rate, driving a bead of perspiration
out of every pore in my skin. More
than once I took stock of myself to
see if I was all there. For two
hours I kept my block with the aid of
a mixture of scalding water and
Condy’s crystals, wherein- I held the
troubled hand. Deliverance from the
nerve-shattering ordeal came in the
shape of a snake-bite antidote. A few
nicks with a razor round the affected
spot, a drop of the dirty-looking fluid
in each cut, and the pain began to
vanish as if by magic. In less than
five minutes only a dull, sickly throb-
bing remained, and that continued
for something approaching 48 hours. It was
“Rooky” Vane’s antidote that did the trick. Am
credibly informed that Morrissey’s snake-bite cure is
just as effective, applied the same way.

“Wandi” : The brush-turkey (B. 23/5/T8)
arranges her-eggs in this fashion. First a cavity is
formed deep down in the mound of decomposing
vegetable matter, and into this the eggs are put in
position with the foot, small end downwards. Leaves
are scraped around and trodden down, and the hole
is filled up and the top well trampled. Eac-h time
an egg is laid a part of the nest is dismantled and
covered up again. The first four are placed one at
each corner of the crater, eight to 12 inches apart,
and covered with three or four inches of material.
Then the second tier is begun, each egg of which is
placed in a space midway between the positions of
the lower ones. Only two tiers are completed the
first year of laying; afterwards 12 to 16 eggs are
deposited. The mound is heaped up until the top
tier is burled to a depth of over two feet. Incuba-
tion lasts nearly six weeks, throughout Which period
the mound is regularly opened up and scratched
over. There may be an interval of two weeks between
the hatching- of the first and last eggs. When they
have worked their way out the chicks have to look
after themselves. They emerge full-fledged, how-
ever, and are naturally independent of parents from
their birth.

“C.8.H.” r If there is any part, of Aus-
tralia where snakes are more vicious than in the
bills between Foster and Boolarra,. South Gippsland
(Vie.), I'd be obliged for'the address so that I can
keep away from it. Within the last few years there
have been eight fatal eases of bite and in one family
alone three females have been bitten. Women and
children seem to be the principal sufferers, no doubt
on account of the snakes’ preference for the warmth,
shelter and food about the houses. Domestic workers
are obliged to put their hands into odd corners, and
thereby often land right on top of a crawler, while
the children stumble on them in Jhe long grass.

“Quidam”: Some people say there is a
sort of secret-society business among tramps, swag-
gMes, knights of the road, hoboes, or whatever you
like to call them whereby information is dissemi-
nated regarding soft snaps for tucker, tobacco, etc.,
and also spots where little but hard work is to be
obtained. Some assert that cabalistic signs convey-
ing valuable hints as to the hardness of the wood-
heap and the appetite of the family dog are en-
graved on gate posts and so on. Certainly I lately
had a run of unfortunate gentlemen who hadn’t had
a bite for 24 hours, but looked hale and hearty
men under the ordeal, and each one, besides enough
tucker to carry him along to the next mug, obtained
a certain quantity of tobacco. Incidentally none
offered to attack the domestic woodheap in return.
The pronounced reluctance of tramps to personally
cut firewood suggests that all of them are married
men with a wife somewhere or other. Probable
nothing has led to so much domestic strife and
divorces as the common or backyard variety of
v/ocdheap. Yet with all the opportunities so chival-
rously allowed her for practice, woman as a rule
uses an axe in a painfully awkward manner, agitat-
ing it straight up and down in a stiff ungainly way
that clearly shews she wants about 1000 years’" more
practice before she develops the easy, graceful style
which man so willingly exhibits before matrimony.
Fortunately she has every possibility of getting the
time allowance. The last tramp who asked for
tobacco was given a variety which, in a moment of
rashness, I had purchased for about 3s. 6d per lb.
Outwardly its appearance was very fair, but other-
wise it was not charming. Since that act pf dubious
genero.at. no tramp lias disturbed my peace, and the
question that now arises is whether this is merely a
coincidence, or do the matters have a distinct and
definite bearing the one on the other'? If the latter,
and the continued absence of tramp deepens this
.conviction, what a magnificent opening there would
be for someone to put on the market an unusually
obnoxious and altogether vile brand of tobacco for
the special use of people who live adjacent to main
roads! Even that deserving but struggling institu-
tion the Tobacco Trust might amass enough wealth
by such means to pull it out of its present pitiable
condition.

of arithmetic puts many things that seemed
plain in a figurative light. “Nine” is “spoil”
or *‘booty,” which explains why nine of the
10 lepers didn’t come back, whereas we
thought they simply walked on. A hundred
isn’t really a number; it means “rescued,”

Every Government, it appears, puts the
people in bondage. “The correct way is to
reduce the number of laws to the fewest pos-
sible,” which is a hard saying for a modern
people, but easy for those who had no pave-
ments to be expectorated on, and no traffic to
keep on the proper side of the street, also
no street; and who didn’t forge another
man’s name on a cheque and send it to the
bank and get the money, because there was
no bank arid no cheque and no money (bar
oxen), and not much other man, and nobody
could write.
As already mentioned, the book is worth
reading, for the things that are in it and
for the things that would be in it if there
was more of it. It is a blessed departure
from sordid daily affairs. The author —who
blushes anonymous in front of the publica-
tion, but signs himself “A. R. Ward” at the
rear —knows his subject, whatever it may be,
from A to Z. And after finishing the tome
it is like falling all the way from Dan to
Beersheba to ask a bank for two sovereigns
in exchange for a small cheque, and be told
that gold is only obtainable in Melbourne.
The Inky Way
Melbourne daily pressmen, occupying
places wherein they are demanded of con-
science to speak the truth, do in their spare
time turn for relief to fiction. Percy Rodda,
of the Age, who has placed a book with the
N.S.W. Bookstall Co., is the latest addition
to southern newspaper-novelists, who include
Roy Bridges (dramatic critic of the Age) ;
a predecessor in the job, Alf Buchanan;
Ambrose Pratt (until recently Age leader-
writer and now editor of the Mining
Standard) ; ‘George Cockerill, who thun-
ders for the Symes in Pratt’s place;
Donald MacDonald, “Observer” of the
Argits ; Oliver Growden, leader-writer on
the Herald ; and Charlie Barrett, the
Herald’s gentle nature-study expert, now
on active service.
“J.F.H.”: Here is a record in the Inky
Way that is not likely to be lowered. When
editor of the Pall Mall Gazette, John
Morley had just started writing a leading
article, when he received an urgent sum-
mons from Gladstone. Before the interview
was over Gladstone, as Prime Minister, had
made Morley Chief Secretary for Ireland
and a Cabinet Minister. Then Morley re-
turned and finished the article. Another
journalistic anecdote told by John Morley.
Parnell asked him for a look at the draft of
Gladstone’s first Home Rule Bill in order to
have a consultation on its provisions with
half a dozen of his colleagues. Morley de-
murred, fearing premature disclosure and
consequential unknown complications. But
he eventually agreed and no leakage what-
ever occurred. Afterwards Parnell told
Morley that three of the members to whom
he had shown that draft were newspaper
men, who could certainly have sold any
article they chose to write about the bill
for a thousand pounds.
Books Received
Ceorge Matson, who died in 1915 and enjoyed
during his latter days a literary pension from the
Commonwealth, was the long-distance verse-writer of
Australia: he had been doing it for 59 years. The
result is a collected volume of 134 pieces entitled
Australian Poetry (V. K. Jones, Ltd., Perth). The
preface, by Mary A. Juleff, indicates the appreciation
in which Matson was held by people as widely
parted as Ada Crossley and Flag-Commander K. Mori-
yana of a Japanese warship ; but the chief interest
of the work lies in the running commentacy it pre-
sents on the public events of the writer’s long years.
There is no great poetry here and the skill is slight;
but the verses are harmless and kindly and show
occasional insight. Here are three quatrains as good
as any in the book:—
The cold philosopher will say
That this thy last cold bed
Is good ; but can he preach away
The fact that thou art dead?
He sees not with thy mother’s eyes,
Nor can the sophist know
The meaning of the melodies
That filled me, thrilled me so.
For when you held my heart, and played
Upon . its willing strings,
My spirit through the future strayed,
And pictured joyous things.
Memory’s Voices, a booklet of war Versus by May
Kidson (Gordon and Gotch, Perth), is dedicated to
the author’s son, who was killed on service. It rings
throughout with patriotic fervor. Always behind
the verses you see the brave mother smiling through
her tears; and that is probably why they have been
of such value to recruiting in the West. Little war
verse will stand judgment by severe literary stand-
ards ; but at least this is sincere and sometimes
poetical. Now and again the authoress soars high
in sounding phrases, as when she asks
“Shall the locked music of a warring world
Break into woven sounds of melody?”
Still, the chief value of the little collection lies in
the fact that it is a mother’s war book.
Maslii and Other Stories.- —This collection of
Indian tales is by Rabindranath Tagore. They are
very quiet, almost devoid of violence or fierce exer-
tion such as croquet. In “The Supreme Night” a
disconsolate lover and his lady, then another man’s
wife, are marooned in flood-time on an island of
three square yards. They don’t speak nor do they
touch each other, and in the morning they go their
separate ways. “The Postmaster” is a slim young
colored person who does his business in a small
shed in a small village. The small orphan girl who
odd-jobs for him loves him. In his small way he
loves the small girl. When he resigns owing to
small salary or something he goes away and she
doesn’t. Some of the tales are more fierce even than
these. (Macmillan and Co., London).
Business, Robbery, etc.
“Murkah”: Regarding wireless, “Arthur
R.” (B. 23/5/T8) asks some questions. The
difference in signal-strength between day and
night transmission is always equally
marked; it does not vary with the seasons.
When Marconi first suggested the possibility
of trans-Atlantic wifeless, attention was
immediately directed to the “mountain” of
water which rises to a height of 150 miles
between the nearest points of Ireland and
Canada. “That’ll stop him,” said the ex-
perts. But it didn’t, and on December 12,
1901, a wild day, the first signals were shot
across the ocean. The fact that the signal-
waves didn’t shoot off into space was con-
sidered by a man named Heaviside to be due
to the presence of a reflecting layer in the
upper atmosphere, which kept deflecting the
waves down to earth. This theory has
since been generally accepted and the name
“Heaviside layer” given to the deflecting
stratum. The effect of sunlight on this layer
is to considerably reduce its reflecting pow-
ers. Therefore more of the wave-energy
“gets through” during daylight, and that is
the best-supported theory for the reduction
of range during the hours of sunlight. Per-
sonally I believe that this feature is more
marked in the Northern than in our own
Southern Hemisphere.
“Orlop”: Shipping terms and arrange-
ments tell of queer survivals from old days.
A “charter party” is the latter-day equiva-
lent of the Latin carta partita, under which
the Mediterranean shipowner of 2000 years
ago prepared his agreement in duplicate, cut
it across the middle through some particular
word or figure, and swapped halves with the
charterer of his fast and favorite trireme.
Long before that, again, Noah’s famous
voyage was undertaken on a verbal charter
party, faithfully carried out. “General aver-
age,” the common law of the sea, under
which individual loss incurred for the safety
of the ship and cargo—as, for instance, in
jettisoning deck-cargo in a storm—is paid
for by a contribution from the other inter-
ested parties, was well understood and acted
upon by the Phoenicians. The bridge of a
modern liner is the direct descendant of the
plank which used to be thrown across from
one paddle-box to the other in the earliest
steamers for the convenience of the captain
when bringing his ship into port. The wheel
was then on the quarter-deck, sailing-ship
fashion. The stern galleries of a warship
were originally the latrines for the after-
guard, and the heavy scroll work from the
figurehead to the luff of the bow served a
similar purpose for the crew. The Navy
Regulations still refer to the sanitary accom-
modation for the crew as the “Ship’s Head.”
“The Oldest” : An ancient building has dis-
appeared from the south-east corner of
Market and Pitt streets, Sydney. In the first
’forties it was (of course) a pub kept by
William Toogood. In 1850 it was The Cali-
fornia, kept by one Douglass. Five years
later Thomas Spencer named it The Exhibi-
tion. In the ’sixties it was kept by McGlone,
the detective who arrested the bushranger
Frank Gardner: he got the reward and used
it to buy in. When Gardner was released
after 10 years he made his way to San Fran-
cisco, and there McGlone, who had done no
good, became his barman. Since that time
the old pub has been a bank and a softgoods
store, and upstairs Tom Slattery and Louis
Heydon chased Costs, and often caught them.

“S.D.”: “E.S.S” (B. 2/5/’18) did not
mention that the Queensland Pine Co. didn’t
get busy over the manufacture of. paper till
the Lands Department got on its tracks.
Hunter, the Minister, saw that, in the lib-
eral agreement which the co. had made with
the late Denham Government, provision
was made for the utilisation of treetops for
pulp manufacture. But the co. had not
made a move, so he told it to pulp or pay
an increased royalty. The co. woke up
suddenly, and a sample of pulp (for brown
wxapping-paper) which has been manufac-
tured is declared by the co.’s imported
American expert to be equal to the best
U.S.A. product.
* * * *
Melbourne reporters laughed over an in-
cident at the Inter-State Commission’s in-
quiry into the working of the cash-order
system. A drapery man was under exami-
nation, and lawyer Joe Wolff, appearing
for the co. that runs the system, asked
him whether the firm had received a cer-
tain letter. He referred the question to one
of his colleagues in court, who said “No,
no.” Whereupon the lawyer, having got this
authority to repeat his “No” whilst out of
the witness-box, put him into the box and
repeated the question. Then the terrified
draper, with the penalty of perjury before
his eyes, meekly changed his “No” to “Yes,”
and was told to get away and feel ashamed
of himself. The joke came after the close of
the proceedings, when the crushed witness
complained of being badly treated.
* * * *
“Croson”: It was natural for the mill-
owners’ deputation that lately spoke with
Vic. Minister of Agriculture Oman to object
to the presence of the press, but it was un-
fortunate the journalists learned enough to
interest the public before they departed.
Now it is known that the harassed flour-
millers vented their griefs at Oman because,
the 1915-16 wheat “having absorbed all the
moisture it is capable of absorbing, they are
unable to add further moisture, and conse-
quently their profits are affected.” “Profits”
is rather a good word in this connection, and
reminds one of the milkman who poured rain
into his cowjuice—until gaol deterred him
somewhat. The public has long been aware
that wheat absorbs moisture from the air,
more rapidly near the sea—formerly to the
great advantage of the wheat-merchants—
but it had hitherto no idea that the flour-
miller speeded-up Nature by himself adding
as much water ds the grain would hold —and
selling it as flour, of course. One Melbourne
miller casually told Oman that in 1916 his
firm could add 2000 oz. of water per hour to
the wheat then being gristed, whereas now
it could add nothing. So the poor firm
was obviously If tons short on each day’s
output. And the water cost only lfd.! No
wonder they wept.
*.* * *
“Metacentre”: Some of the coastal steam-
ers can —or could before the War Devil
broke loose —be used to illustrate the pro-
gress of modern ship-development rather in-
terestingly. The Coolgardie, built about
1884, is the original flush-decked pattern,
with only a forecastle, small bridge, fiddley,
and short-house aft for officers. The Roto-
mahana, of a later year, has extended her
bridge well aft to cover the fiddley, and has
moved the short-house further forward. Then
along comes the Aramac, unites the poop and
the original bridge and fiddley into one long
promenade deck, and puts the working bridge
a storey higher. The Wodonga improves on
that by putting her boats on overhead skids,
built up from the bridge deck. Then the
Wyandra replaces the boat-skids by another
deck running forward to the break of the
bridge. The Wyreema goes one better again
by covering in the forward well-deck of the
Wyandra, which brings her back to the
original Coolgardie, but one storey higher.
The Kanowna puts cabins on the top deck
of the Wyreema type and adds another deck
above to carry the boats and the officers’
quarters. Finally the Karoola continues the
promenade deck of the Kanowna aft, and
builds up the forecastle head above the for-
ward flush deck, giving practically two
storeys on the original Coolgardie model.
For all these additions careful allowance is
made by giving additional beam to main-
tain stability, and the built-up ship, top-
heavy though she may look to the untrained
eye, is actually stiffer and safer than the old
low flush-decker.
“The middleman is necessary just as long
as the manufacturer and retailer will stand
him,” says the managing director of Pelaco,
Ltd. Which is to say that, as regards Aus-
tralian products, the middleman lives on the
lack of mutual organisation between the
manufacturer and the retailer. As a stocker
of imported goods, however, he is practically
a necessity to small buyers, and must. con-
tinue to exist in a diminishing form until the
range and output of local manufactures
covers all public requirements. The first
aspiration of a people should be to push him
out of business. The importer is the enemy
of patriotism and the waster of labor which
might be productively employed. Yet to see
him presiding over a gathering of merchants
you’d think him indispensable and a benefit
to the community!
“R.8.J.”: It would be interesting to know
why N. S. Wales Education Department still
runs its School Savings Bank. Two other
banks and a war-savings scheme not only
encourage thrift, but pay interest, which the
rchool bank doesn’t. It doesn’t cover localities
neglected by the other banks, because in the
hamlet so microscopic as to contain
neither bank agency nor post office the school
is below the grade that runs to the dignity
of a bank. If it were a source of revenue —
that is to say if, by allowing the kid no in-
terest on his money, while re-banking the
same coin and collaring the resulting interest
—a surplus could be shown, there might
be some excuse for not slaying the old mon-
strosity. Just to prevent any nasty, sus-
picious person getting the idea that the
whole concern is for the sole purpose of
keeping several well-paid and perfectly use-
less jobs in existence, some figures should be
published.
* * * *
“S.” : Our occasional rabbit-o leant against
the doorpost and unbosomed himself. He was
wreathed in blood-stained bunnies, and a
cigarette-butt drooped sadly from the corner
of his mouth. “This ’ere game isn’t wot it
useter be. Before they fixed the prices on
me, I was makin’ a steady two-poun’-ten a
day an’ wot I could get for th’ skins, l
can’t do it now. Goo’day, Missus.” My
husband is a civil servant who “keeps up
appearances” on about £7 a week—after
years and years of study and work. . My son
is growing up and the University is before
us, but I’m wondering whether it wouldn't
be better to buy him a barrow.
A BALLADE OF VAIN STRIVINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What shall the scholar gain, though oil he burns,
Night after night, until the morning grey
Peers through his frost-dimmed lattice and discerns
Him poring' o’er some tome? Can learning stay
Time’s march, or stop the passions’ wild affray?
Mad sage, what hopest thou to gain as fee?
Upon thy precious parchments worms will prey—
Thou shalt a tale, a ghost, and ashes bel
What shall the miser profit, though he turns
From simple joys that gild the beggar’s day?
Not all the golden guineas that he earns
His waste of golden sunlight can repay;
The hours when love and laughter meet at play.
Base miser, though thou hoardest greedily
Thy gold, thou canst not thus transmute thy clay—•
Thou shalt a tale, a ghost, and ashes be!
What gains the lonely anchorite, who spurns
The pleasures keen that thrill the sinner gay?
The flesh that he denies still aches and yearns,
And virtues shall not save it from decay;
While women smile contempt, ’twill go the way
Of that of lechers. Madman, canst not see,
Who each God-given ecstasy dost slay,
Thou shalt a tale, a ghost, and ashes be!
Envoy.
Comrades, while shines the gracious sun, make hay!
Joy mocks at mad ambition. Struggle free
From all that keeps life’s rare delights at bay—
Thou shalt a tale, a ghost, and ashes be!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON OWEN.

Heart of Empire Serie»—No. 9.
The Houses of Parliament,
on the site of the old Palace of
Westminster, Were opened in 1847.
Designed by Sir Charles Barry,
R.A., in the Gothic Perpendicular
Style and noted throughout the
world for their symmetry and
beauty. The Royal or Victoria
Tower, 340 feet high, is the royal
entra ce to the House of Lords,
a magnificently decorated chamber.
The House of Commons, 70 feet
long, is also very handsome. Big
Ben was set in place in 1860.
Westminster Hall was built by
William Rufus and has been skil-
fully incorporated in the design.

Three Ways to Know Paramount and Artcraft
1. By seeing these trade-marks or
names in the advertisement of
your local theatres.
2. By seeing these trade-marks or
names on the front of the theatre
or in the lobby.
3. By seeing these trade-marks or names

Jm
POLITICAL
POINTS
Mount Elliott, the big Queensland collec-
tion of mines, which ought to be producing
copper regularly for the Allies, has had
another strike. But even when it hasn’t
got a strike what the I.WAV. calls “the flea”
is at work. General-manager Corbould
describes the pnpcess.
You _ can detect nothing wrong except that there
are minor breaks and the plant is hung up for a
while. Then a few days’ rest from that—nothing
wrong now except the production. Everybody work-
ing, nothing doing. They endeavor by all means
possible to so worry the manager that he closes the
works, which is a “lock-out.” Or you have a fine
body of men who are satisfied and contented. The
fleas are set to work, and you soon get a request for
some concession. You refer them to the Arbitration
award. If you make it clear that you are keeping
to the award the matter ends so far as the Com-
mittee of the A.W.U. is concerned. But the fleas
get to work to out the committee, and they often
succeed, because disgusted men say, “Oh, damn the
meetings!” and won’t attend to protect their own
interests. So the fleas win.
Ir all reminds Corbould of the boys who put
up ,a joke on another by throwing the half-
cooked dinner out into the yard. It seemed
a very good joke, too, till dinner-time.
A MORAL VICTORY.
, “The people of the Upper Hunter (N.S.W.) were

The Hun : “That’s right, my little man,
let’s bury the hatchet, and then yon need
never go cap in hand again to anyone
except me.”
The Commonwealth Government an-
nounces gaily that last year it made a profit
of about £250.000 out of rabbits and rabbit-
skins. Had it made the same percentage of
profit out of the turnover of sheep and sheep-
skins and wool the squatters of Australia
would have been about £5,000,000 poorer and
the Commonwealth revenue about that much
richer. It is sometimes an advantage to be a
squatter and not a rabbiter. A good deal
depends upon who is Prime Minister.
The Tasmanian Government, after much
shuffling over shipbuilding during the past
12 months, looks to be holding a bad hand.
When the Federal Government ordered two
5500-ton steel vessels the other day the
Devonport slip was selected on which to
build them. A day or two later Captain H.
Wood, of that town, secured an order to
build 20 wooden vessels for a private firm,
and where the Government’s hard luck
appears to be is that most of these boats
will be constructed in the Wilmot elector-
ate. An influx of a couple of thousand
laborers will probably cause the bare Liberal
majority to evaporate at the next general
election. Premier Lee and Minister Hays,
both Wilmot representatives, may not even
get into the new Parliament.
Following on the scandalous extravagance
which resulted in Queensland’s railways
last year producing a deficit of three-
quarters of a million, the Government now

There was once a man who got into the
habit of drinking two bottles of whisky a
day. Presently he found that he couldn’t
walk upstairs to bed. So they brought his
bed down to the ground floor. Then every-
thing was all right. The Great Reform had

Wherefore unduly high wages must also in-
