“303”: Sergeant Thomas Ryan, of the
Victorian Assembly, said recently in Martin-
place, Sydney : “Of the 709 members of Parlia-
ments in Australia only five are to be seen
and heard on the recruiting platform.” Yet
more than half the elected members were
returned as enthusiastic Win-the-War
patriots. Now, going back to 1915, before
the draft was applied, this was how the
British House of Commons showed up. Of
Unionists 140 out of 288 members were on
active service—say 49 per cent. Of Libs, and
Labs., including followers of Asquith, Haldane
and co., and all the talking lawyers and
traders, 40 were on service out of 298, say
14 per cent.; of Nationalists (Irish) two
members out of 84, say 3 per cent. Aus-
tralia’s record even of recruiters is a frac-
tion of one M.P. per cent. After getting
elected the vast majority don’t even trouble
to kill the Kaiser with their mouths.
One possible reason for the haste to bring
back wounded Australian soldiers without
even waiting for them to get their wooden
legs before leaving is that it is advisable to
get them to a place where they can be paid in
paper money. The Government has just
issued another £3,000,000 of it.
N. S. Wales Labor’s new policy is to
make a mess in order to have the chance of
cleaning it up. Isn’t there enough mess on
the premises already to grapple with?
From the Gallery :
The greatest hindrance to the swift despatch of
business in the Reps, is the presence of females in
the Speaker’s Gallery. Most of the ladies are mem-
bers’ friends, and as many have dropped in to hear
their hero spout, he is bound to do it. You will see
members, immoderately silent on a measure where
their opinion would be valuable, open their faces on
some piffling little matter before a petticoated audi-
ence. The amount of patter mostly varies according
to the age and complexion of the congregation; two
or three reasonably pretty girls will be the means of
giving the Hansard reporter Pitman’s elbow. But
there- are other men who are bound to provide plati-
tudes simply to maintain their domestic ascendancy.
The idea that no man is a hero to his wife is dis-
proved a thousand times by the air of conscious pride
with which a particular lady watches the gyrations
of her hubby in the House.
Four big countries, the U.S., Canada,
Japan and Australia, have been in the war
for some time —three of them from the be-
ginning—without being invaded, blockaded
or even bombed. Three of them are doing
well out of the war. The U.S. has increased
the value of its exports 300 per cent, since
the row started, and it reckons that it will
have 25,000,000 tons of shipping afloat at
the finish of the scrap instead of the pre-
vious 5,000,000 tons, and will have got back
the job of the world’s boss sea-carrier and
marine parcels-delivery. Japan has increased
its exports 500 per cent, and its mills and
shipyards are working all round the clock.
One of its shipping companies recently broke
the monotony of life with a dividend of over
TOO per cent. Canada’s exports have gone
up 400 per cent., In 1917 they reached
£317,000,000 and after paying for imports
the country had over 111,000,000 sovereigns
to collect. Australia’s exports (£98,000,0001
have gone up merely about 25 per cent, and it
only drove the first nail of the Government’s
shipbuilding programme the other day.
Nothing at all has happened to its manufac-
turing industries. In an industrial and com-
mercial sense it has become a place sugges-
tive of the sleepy old parish with thatched
cottages and moss on the church roof.
In this apologetic country the Hun agent
or Hun sympathiser whirleth about continu-
ally, and no one interferes with him. At
Wolfram (Q.) the miners wanted an award
and Judge Macnaughten gave it. The miners
refused to obey it and looked at the law
as they might at a dead rabbit. So Judge
McCauley gave a better award. The miners
refused that and demanded more, and the
employers conceded their demands. And the
strike went on just the same. Apparently
the secret is that the metals produced at
Wolfram are used for munition-making, and
the I.W.W. or pro-Hun agencies have de-
cided that metals of that sort must not be
produced. Nobody has been tried for treason
up to date.
Senator Gardiner is reported as stating in
Parliament that the alleged Australian Labor
ideal —peace without indemnities or annexa-
tions —would simply “leave every invaded
nation in a pre-war condition.” So it would
—barring the women on whom rape has been
committed; the women, children and non-
combatant men who have been murdered,
tortured, enslaved, starved or mutilated;
barring the vast indemnities that have
already been exacted from the occupied dis-
tricts, in money, food, machinery, coal, iron,
live-stock, oil and anything else that was
valuable and portable; also the towns and
villages and farms that have been obliter-
ated, the years of industry that have been
lost, the loss of years of education to multi-
tudes of children, the mental wreckage caused
by living amid horrors that never ceased, and
the thousands of millions of debt incurred in
putting up a defence against the possibility
of even worse happenings. All these things
have been suffered, not by the Hun, who
hasn’t been invaded yet, so that his pro :
perty and his unarmed population have
escaped, but by the peoples—mostly the little
peoples—on whom the Plun has made inva-
sion. It suggests an affable ghoul dancing
beside a deathbed to talk of any pacifist
programme putting the Hun victims “in a
pre-war condition.” God couldn’t do it, let
alone home smug, well-paid, well-fed poli-
ticians, unless He put back the clock of the
universe and gave us yesterday.
“J.G.”: The reference to D. Percy Rus-
sell (B. 6/6/T8) recalls that he made his
Federal elections debut in 1910 when he
contested Ballarat in the Labor interest
against Deakin. The contest was about
the most exciting in the Commonwealth, as
only a handful of votes separated the com-
paratively unknown miner and the Prime
Minister. At one stage of the count Deakin
had the horrifying feeling that Russell was
going to out him. Mostly by the aid of the
“postal” votes Deakin scraped in with a few
to spare. That was Deakin’s last contest, as
he retired from politics before the next elec-
tion.

gers in Petrograd to £6OO a year. Now the

THE NEW LUMINARY.
DER KAISER: “Ach! Dot look like mein evil
star.”
Since the days of Batman or thereabouts
Victorian Governments have promised a
decent Scaffolding Act. Now the unions
have grown weary and propose to make the
law themselves —and administer it them-
selves. The Building Trades Federation has
drawn up regulations covering scaffolding of
all sorts, bracing, ladders, ropes, splicing,
hoists, lifts, shearlegs and the rest of the
paraphernalia; and if they are not observed
on any job then the workers will be with-
drawn. So far Parliament has taken no
notice; but if it continues blind and deaf it
may ,be sorry afterwards. If a community,
or sections thereof, find they can make their
own laws and carry them out to suit them-
selves they will presently want to know
what’s the use of Parliament at all.
N. S. Wales Attorney-General Hall, who
has just returned from China:—
There are no factory laws there; no education
laws; no workmen’s compensation; no arbitration
courts. I saw children under ten years of age work-
ing in the cotton factories 12 hours a day. The
wheels never stop from one year’s end to 'another,
except on the Chinese New Year’s Day. Thousands
of children start at six in the morning and work till
six at night. They grab a little lunch at their
work. At six o’clock at night they are replaced by
thousands of other children—and so the thing goes
on, seven days 1 a week, and 52 weeks in the year.
Yet the good Australian unionist will pro-
bably keep on cheerfully “sinking the fiscal
issue,”' thus permitting the output of the
sweated Chinese to be imported into Aus-
tralia. . How long does the fiscal-sinking
Australian unionist think his protective
Labor laws are going to stand up against
that sort of thing?
According to N. S. Wales Labor-leader
Storey (Parliamentary report, 14/(>/T8)
I believe that the workers can not do wrong.
In that case the Almighty may consider Him-
self superannuated. If the Creature becomes
infallible simply because he is a qualified
blacksmith or office-cleaner or musician, the
Creator seems superfluous.
“N, S. Waler”: What makes me feel more
tired than all other things put together is
the spectacle of two Houses, comprising 142
great hulking members, passing a bill that
weighs an ounce and a half.
BALLADE OF THE CLEAN SCUTCHEON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We do not pray that war may go
And leave Australia scatheless all;
In this grim time of death and woe
Such hope, in sooth, would idle fall.
But when this hellish strife is o’er,
And others tell of what hath been,
Pray Heav’n they boast on this our shore:
“Our fathers kept our scutcheon clean!”
Pray Heav’n they boast: “Our forebears brave
They fought and bled for this dear land ;
They did not ape the cunning knave
Who skulks and draws no gallant brand ;
They did not say, ‘Let England's blood
And France’s blade in flashing sheen
Redeem Australia’s Nationhood!’
Thank God, they kept our scutcheon clean !
“They did not bide in peace at home
The while they knew that ‘aliens’ bled
To save Australia’s cot and dome
And hold in freedom each dear head ;
It was not theirs to lounge at ease
And keep a brow sedate, serene,
While others ‘sailed through bloody seas’—
Thank God, they kept our scutcheon clean !”
QUEENSLAND.
P. AIREY.

WORMS,
ONE NINE INCHES LONG AND ANOTHER FOUR
INCHES PASSED BY CHILD AFTER TAKING
COMSTOCK “DEAD SHOT” WORM PELLETS.
Mrs. A. E. Bade, of 203 McKillop Street.
East Geelong, Vic., writes:—“l have great
pleasure in writing to you to let you know
the satisfactory results I had in using your
‘Dead Shot’ Worm Pellets for my young son,
aged 14 years. He had been ailing for some
time and complained of pain in the spine and
a very sore stomach, and often in the night
would start vomiting without any warning
whatever. He also had several fainting
turns. I became alarmed and took him to
the doctor, but the treatment did him no
good. I then thought I would try your Worm
Tablets, as to me he had every appearance of
worms. I procured a bottle of them, together
with a bottle of Indian Root Pills, and com-
menced the treatment. The second day after
taking them he passed a stomach worm nine
inches long, and the next day one four
inches long. Since then he has picked up won-
derfully and is twice the child he was. If he
shows any signs again I will not hestitate to
try them, as I was so pleased with the results.
lam most grateful to the proprietors of these
tablets, as my husband is away on Active
Service, and the worry of our boy being so ill
nearly drove me mad. Thanking you again.”
STOMACH WORMS.
A MENACE TO CHILDREN.
Stomach worms are undoubtedly the most
invidious kind of all. They inhabit the
stomach and inflict great havoc upon their
victim. They deprive the victim of half the
nutriment he consumes, and when the stomach
is empty gnaw the sides of the stomach,
causing such terrible sensations as to often
cause faintness. Aperient medicines have no
effect upon them, for when any purgative is
taken they fasten themselves to the walls of
the stomach by a groove running down their
side. The best method of dislodging stomach
worms is to starve the system and take Com-
stock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets. Tie
worms are weakened by the want of food,
and are induced to take whatever is taken into
the stomach. Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm
Pellets contain the properties which sicken
and disable the worms, and a dose of opening
medicine will clear them out of the system.
Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets
are a purely vegetable medicinal preparation
for the eradication of worms in children and
adults. For sale by all chemists and store-
keepers, price 3s. 6d. per packet, or will be
sent post paid at the same price by The
W. H. Comstock Co., Ltd., 23 Lang Street,
Church Hill, Sydney; or Parish Street,
Wellington, N.Z.
FLATTERING
Imitations in Exactly Similar Bottles.
It has been stated that imitation is the sincerest form of flattery, and
if this statement is true the public who have been using Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills for years should feel highly flattered in having its
judgment regarding the merits of this remedy confirmed by a number
of imitations which are at present on the market, some of which
are in exactly similar bottles. Although imitators may copy the type
of bottle and label, and even the advertising accompanying the
packet, they can only imitate Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills—they
cannot produce anything like them. See to it that only
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are secured, and politely resent imitations
or worthless substitutes.

PROPERLY
Weights for the Grand National Hurdle
Race seem to flatter the chances of, say, Pistolier,
Bundong, Bardol, Will Comfort, Fastolf, Nick-a-Jack
and any of the new recruits among the light weights
that can jump a bii. and are not yet ancient. Bun-
dong, with only 71b. more than he carried into
second place last year, is undoubtedly well treated,
for he is now only a comparatively young fellow as
jumpers go. Pistolier, too, is very well in it at
10st. 41b. if he has improved in his method of
getting over the sticks. Clever jumping, even
where hurdles only bar the way, counts for much
in a fast three-mile go. The doubt about Bardol is
as to how he jumps, but he is backed as though he
were sufficiently expert, and he looks as well worth
picking in a “double” with say, Samander or Caibye
as any of them.
Last year there was nothing in the same class
with Caibye and Old Mungindi over the big jumps.
They have gone up a lot in the weights, but are
nevertheless preferable on paper to all the proved
chasers, bar Samander, a recent winner over the
Flemington course. This six-year-old, with 9st. 81b.
for his load, strikes one as being the pick of the
handicap for the G. N. Steeplechase. Culluleraine,
on the same marks for age and weight as Samander,
is probably faster, but has not yet been proved over
the stiff Flemington course. On present appearances
it seems probable that there will be warm favorites
before the day for both Hurdle and Steeple, and that
the books will sigh in vain for a rank outsider to
turn up.
* * * *
The Hurdle Race at Moonee Valley last
Saturday suggested that imported horses are going
to have a say in the three-miles event on July 6.
Calais, one of Tye’s lot from England, won a two-
mile race from end to end with Will Comfort chasing
him ~ and St. Anthony (also imported) in respectful
attendance. In the Steeplechase old Booligal came
to the front again. For the first time since long ago
Booligal was handicapped below 12 stone at the
Valley'. He won easily, after allowing another nag
to make most of the running. Samander, much
fancied by many people for the G. N. Steeple, had a
fall in this race and lost a lot of his popularity by so
doing.
* * * *
Weighed out for the Final Handicap at
Melbourne, Joe Carter, the oldest jockey in Aus-
tralia. Joe’s first important winning ride was in
the Victorian Derby on 11. N. Simpson’s Flying Doe.
That was away back in ’56. Afterwards he piloted
winners of big races over the line in Tasmania,
Victoria, N.S.W. and M.L. He weighed in at 85
years, 60 of them spent in the Commonwealth.
* * * *
The Kaiser has killed Melbourne Winner,
the sporting adjunct of the Herald. The bright
little paper had the misfortune to be born just as
the European pot boiled over, and the price of infant
food is now unbearable.
* * * *
“Trot”: It seems a pity the A.d.C. does
not put on a real steeplechase menu at Randwick
for the winter meeting. Cross-country horses are
poorly catered for in N. S. Wales, but there can be
little doubt that a bit more encouragement would
bring more animals into the game, as in Vic. The
placing of a hurdle race regularly on suburban pro-
grammes has brought an increased number of
jumpers along, and though some of them are not
champions, lepping has improved a lot during the
past season. If the A.J.C. tabled two hurdle races
and two steeples on each day of the winter meeting
and staged a distance race on the flat under welter
weights, such a meeting would be just as popular
at Randwick as it is in some parts of Maoriland.
Races for “novices” and two-year-olds and sprinters
could well be cut down. They are at it every week
all the year round.
* * * *
“Whip” : The “humble petition,” lately pre-
sented to the Premier of S. Aus., asking him to look
into an alleged gross injustice perpetrated by the
Onkaparinga Racing Club in 1915, seems to have
fizzled out. The Club offered to give the profits at its
first meeting after the war started to the Belgian
Relief Fund, and it handed over £315. The petition
says that the gross profits were £2OOO, and that
the Club has always refused to render a proper
account to the Fund. The fact is that the Club has
. one big meeting every' Easter Monday, and that is the
only one in the year; so the takings on the day
have to cover the expenses of management and up-
keep for 12 months. Premier Peake has gone into
the matter afresh. The Belgian Fund committee
says that it has never made any remarks beyond
acknowledging receipt. of the £315. The police
assert that they found that the Club’s auditors, well-
known and responsible men, had certified that the
balance-sheet was a proper and accurate account.
The suggestion is that the movement may be a per-
sonal one aimed at Von Doussa, the secretary, though
he has spent the 70 years of his life almost entirely
in the service of the public, is still M.L.C., and has
practically no knowledge of nor interest in the coun-
try his name was originally hatched in.
* * * #
“Barbwire”: At the Charleville (Q.)Show
some years ago the Hunter’s Plate was just over and
some of the hurdles had been knocked down. The
man whose duty it was to see that the hurdles were
put in order after each “jump” was absent from the
ring—evidently gone for a pint. The next event
was “Best Lady Rider Over Hurdles,” and one of
the officials was very troubled at the hurdles not
being ready for the event, and decided to drive in
the pegs himself. So he bawled: “Where's the hax?
Bring in the hax.” The bellringer started off to
announce an event which was almost the last one
on the programme. “All hacks this way, please. All
hacks this way!” and immediately the ring was
crowded with ladies’ hacks. The official was para-
lysed. “You fool!” he groaned. “X said the
hax —not the hacks!”
* * * *
“Gleniris”: The Age has devoted a Satur-
day “special” to a general criticism of the up-to-date
horsewoman, in top-boots and riding-pants, who
straddles her nag instead of draping both feet (etc.)
on the near side of the animal. Reference was also
made to “hippy” women, who ought to be at home
looking after Little Willie, but insist on making them-
selves conspicuous on horseback. The tendency of
the times is to shove womenfolk out into the open
air as much as possible for exercise and exhilaration.
Now, which does the most in this direction—the
smart, safe pair of pants and simple seat, or the old-
fashioned skirt and pronged saddle, which gives the
rider the plaintive appearance of some wild thing
clinging to a cliff? Nowadays, plenty of “hippy” women,
instead of increasing their girth 'by rotting around
in the old arm-chair, are reducing their “hippiness”
by getting out on hubby’s hack while he is at work.
The greenery-yallery miss of the afternoon tea-table
is rapidly changing into a broad-chested, breezy
damsel, full of healthy energy and a sound outlook
on life; and the healthiest and broadest-chested ones
are certainly those who give the' loud laugh to
gran’ma’s saddle, hanging cobwebbed in the toolshed,
while they go forth from the horse-paddock in useful
corduroys, holding themselves aboard with both
thighs as Nature built them to do.
* * * *
Sid Godfrey persists in winning the war
at Sydney Hippodrome several times a week. Heavy
reinforcements have been provided by local feather-
weights, but grave fears are entertained of the
reserves becoming exhausted. A 1 King was the last
reinforcement. He took Godfrey’s offensive on the
chin in the third session, and Dr. Dave Smith ordered
removal of the outed to the nearest casualty station.
* * * *
“Silas Snell”: Boxing in Melbourne was
almost down and out; but hope revived a little
when Jimsie Wills, heavyweight champion of Tas-
mania, put in an appearance. An opponent was
found for the huge ex-miner from Zeehan in Dick
Turnbull, Pride of the Port, a hefty lumper who
had eaten up three bucolic champions, including
“Spud” Just, the Warrnambool Wonder. The Tas-
manian champion was liberally advertised. He had
a string of performances a yard long, and patrons
looked for a sanguinary battle. Among the hangers-
on at this date was one Teddy the Hen, a lank, pale-
faced, lantern-jawed, long-nosed street urchin, who
was employed about the hall by the promoters.
Teddy the Hen occasionally boxed a preliminary, or
served as pasting post for a pro. in training, and as
general receiver was a wonder. The Wills-Turnbull
fight was a Victoria Hall fixture, and it drew a full
house at three, two and one. The champion of Tas-
mania was easily first favorite: it was generally
understood that he was going to bring Australian
boxing into its own again. A featherweight prelim-
inary was being fought, when a sudden diversion
arose as a result of the attempt of a large man with
a face like a knotted box-block to drift from
the 2s. to the 3s. seats. Teddy the Hen, lanker and
paler than ever, was holding the pass. The immense,
plain person insisted. Teddy resisted. The enor-
mous one aimed a blow at Teddy, like Hercules
heaving a butcher’s-block. Teddy slipped it easily,
planted his left, swung his right,/ and the heavy
fellow with the dreadful face fell in the gangway
and lay outspread, dead to the world. There was
silence in the house for 10 seconds. Then arose an
ominous murmur which swelled to a cry of anguish
as the truth soaked home. There would be no super-
fight that night. The giant on the floor, asleep, to
all appearances, for a week, was Jimsie Wills, cham-
pion of Tasmania, and standing over him, eager to
resume hostilities, was Teddy the Hen!
“Hugo Knapp”: This goes as the greatest
scrap in Cobar (N.S.W.). A while back a number
of I.W.W. men came over from Broken Hill to teach
the Cobar miners how to run their industrial affairs.
They were not overwhelmed with welcome, and this
seemed to surprise and vex them. Anyhow, bad feel-
ing grew up and peace folded its wings and retired
pending the inevitable clash. This came one night
recently, at an I.W.W. meeting in the main street.
A disciple of sabotage had just mounted his box
when, at a prearranged signal, Cobar delivered its
punch. Up and down the street the battle raged
and the best the police could do was to act as un-
official masters of ceremony, steering contestants away
from plate-glass windows and picking up the
wounded. You can buy I.W.W. stocks in Cobar
to-day at seven for sixpence.
* * # *
League football in Sydney sparkled up on
Saturday when A. McPherson casually booted seven
goals for Western Suburbs against Annandale. The
same sure boot has piled up 19 goals during the
season.
* * * ■*
Herb Gilbert, the bulky skipper of the
Western Suburbs outfit which is making a splash in
Sydney Rugby League games this season, is a much-
travelled enthusiast. Many years ago he represented
N.S.W. against Maoriland; then, after touring Eng-
land with an Australian team, he pitched his tent in
the Cold Country and played the Northern Union
game for a few winters. He returned to Australia
three years since and pulled on a jersey for Eastern
Suburbs. Last season he incorporated himself with
the Wests and topped the club’s efforts with nine
tries. On Saturday he crossed Annandale’s line
three times.
Home again at the end of a three years’
sprint after Abdul and Fritz, Major J. H. P. Eller,
formerly a well-known Essendon cross-country runner.
He breasted the tape in a fierce stunt in France, where
he won the D.S.O. for bombing a German machine-
gun out of action single-handled, capturing a pillbox
and consolidating his objective. He is not 40 yet.
* * * #
“Wart”: Major A. A. (“Lex”) Evans,
who has just been handed the D. 5.0., was one of
the best all-round athletes the Speck has turned out.
If he did not reach inter-State championship form it
was because he never specialised, but had a go at
every sport that came along, on land or water, and
won trophies at them all. He fought in S’Africa. and
was a lieutenant in the local battery of artillery
which volunteered in a bunch on the outbreak of
war. On Gallipoli he gained a captaincy and a
Military Cross, and in France he was given a
howitzer battery and the major’s crown.
W. J. Stack, who recently won the D. 5.0.,
swung a bat for Sydney ’Varsity and N.S.W. Xl’s in
pre-war days. Cricket runs in the family. Old man
G. B. Stack was in Ma’s XI of ’66.
* * * *
“Q.”: The hills of the N. S. Wales central
tablelands resound these days with the fox-hunter’s
gun. The sport has become popular and it is profit-
able, too, for skins are worth 7s. 6d. or 10s. apiece.
I recently unearthed a single-barrel b.l. weapon to
take a hand, and it seems a remarkable implement.
At 70yds. it deposited 33 No. 1 shot in a kerosene
tin, it slew a fox at 90yds., and executed a hare at
104yds. I got the weapon, then a dirty, unused
thing, for 10s. at a cocky’s clearing-out sale. No
sport could get it now for a fiver.
“J.C.”: According to “Tarwin” (B.
16/5/’18) the rabbit-shooting stunt of Burdy (S.A.)
would be very quick work, as to slay 778 rabbits in
10 hours means a shot every 46sec. Nothing very
expeditious about that if the meat transpires. But
tyiere have been really quick things done with a gun,
and O’Shannessy, a Gippsland trooper, did one of
them. When stationed on the Lower Tarwin he
was a man of exceptional skill with the trigger. lie
made a match to shoot against time under these
conditions: The gun to be in pieces locked in its
case on a table. At the word “go” the case to be
unlocked, gun taken out and fitted together, 10
glasses thrown left and right at 30yds. to be
smashed, the weapon dismantled, put back in the
case and locked up. All this to be . done inside one
minute. The performance was attached to the menu
at a local sports meeting. Tom Lees, then a well-
known racing man, was timekeeper. Whalley and
Roily Woodville, then of Inverloch, were referees.
O’Shannessy finished his contract in 42sec. He was
a marvellous shot with gun or service revolver, and
always performed with both eyes open.

hare going crossways fully extended, at 35yds., using
heavy cartridges, what allowance should be made
for time before the shot reaches its mark?

Complaints have been received that men have

Billjim always seems to be in the thick of it when

* * * *
“Rarawai”: “Old Dad,” the cook of a
D.A.C., is an ardent fisherman and whenever we
camp near a canal the old man can always be found
soaking bait. Recently the horse lines were being
shelled, and all hands were ordered to take cover.
When the strafe was at its thickest we noticed Dad
pottering about in the open. “Get under cover, you
1” shouted the s.m. “What are you doing
out there?” “Looking for a bit of lead to put oh
me fishin’ line,” chirped the placid fossicker.
* * # *
Oswald Caust, dead of cancer in Adelaide
at 42, was a fine natural cricketer who never quite
got to the high places where he seemed to belong.
He had three stages: As a punishing batsman in a
country district, the “lower North” of S.A.; at
Broken Hill, where he captained the local team
against an English XI; and as wicket-keeper (and a
heavy scorer) for the Norwood Club in Adelaide at
a time when about seven of its teams were already
in the best XI of S.A., and it was hardly possible
to put any more of them in. Like another and
greater wicket-keeper of a past generation, Alfred
Jarvis, Caust had been a blacksmith. It is a great
profession for hardening the hands.
* * * *
Worth noting is the regularity of A. C.
Kitchen in the handicaps of Sydney Walking and Field
Games Club this season. In three starts he has
won twice, and on Saturday he just failed to con-
cede J. W. Batger 40sec. in a mile. This speed in
the pursuit of sideboard trimmings has brought
Kitchen almost to scratch with top-notcher E N
Smith.
* * * #
“Yass” : So N. S. Wales fisheries officials
are capturing cod in the over-flow lagoons and water-
holes of the Murrumbidgee, and liberating fry and
mature fish in Burrinjuck dam. Likewise, according
to Chief Sec. George Fuller, fishing thereabouts is
to be prohibited for three years “to admit of the
speedy development” of the place as a fishery. Fuller
might as well get coal for Newcastle. He speaks of
21,000 fry having been turned adrift. Well, I have
fished at the dam many times and will swear there
are more than 21 thousand billion fry there already.
With a mate and a dozen hand-lines the catch has
run up to about lcwt. a night, the take ranging from
two to ten pounds each. Cod are fearful cannibals, and
consume fish up to a fourth their own weight. More
fish are scoffed in a night at the dam by other fish
than the few individuals who go there with hooks and
bait could catch in a year. As matters stand
Fuller could send a trawler to the dam and supply
half Sydney with fresh food daily. All this piffling
round with fry and closin £ the dam is foolishness.
The shutters are up during the spawning season and
that is enough.
* * * *
“J.R.K.”: Cure for “Morwong’s” catfish
sting (B. 9/5/’18). After trying many remedies
1 ye come back to bathing in hot water or poulticing
with hot bread. Tie up the affected part and keep
warm. If the sting is on the sole of the foot, add
a little permanganate of potash to the hot water and
bathe. This latter also applies to the bite of the
stinging-tree, common in North Queensland.
* * * *
“The Wanderer”: Talking about the 181 b.
(dressed weight) caught recently in the Manning
(N.S.W.), the fisherman, Ben Ford, was the same
who discovered the two oldest inhabitants of the eel
family at Kimbriki, on the river. The pair were
arrested in a trap. One weighed 441 b. and the other
371 b. (undressed). The head of the big fellow was
preserved, and can be seen by any doubter
* * * *
“Snapper”: If my friend Smethers, a
professional fisherman, is not called to give evidence
before the latest Victorian Royal Commission on
fish he ought to be, for he has the root of the matter.
Smethers points out th&t if we had copied our black
brothers in the matter of the supply of butcher’s
meat we would have found it even harder than it
is now to get the family joint—kangaroo or ’possum
—and pay for it. It is even worse in the case of
fish, for, while you can go hunting or snaring on
land in any weather, a storm may at any time stop
fishing for days. What Smethers wants to do is to
encourage the breeding of tame fish, which can be
kept in paddocks and used for slaughter when it is
too rough to go hunting wild ones. If porpoises
could be trained to act as sheep-dogs and so save
the expense of fencing, all the better.
* * * *
“A.M.1.E.E.” : Re “F.N.T.’s” (B. 16/5/18)
paragraph on the steam-driven motor-car. He
is correct in saying that Australia’s first car was
so propelled. This same car was the first of the
family to do the overland trip from Sydney to Mel-
bourne. The main source of trouble was the kero-
sene burner. This would go on strike as often and
readily as Broken Hill workers; when it did work
the smoke from the fuel filled the boiler tubes and
prevented steam generation. Inventor Herbert Thom-
son, of Armadale (Vic.), devoted himself to the per-
fecting of this part. He eventually succeeded, and
I recently heard that he had sold the patent rights
in his burner to the White Co., of Cleveland
(U.S.A.).
* * * *
Despite streaks of grey under his hat and
wrinkles on his brow, Horace Rice, the veteran Syd-
ney tennis champion, still whips a hefty volley
across the net. After conceding long starts he last
week landed the Red Cross tournament handicap, and
on Saturday he and partner Kidston won four sets
(6-1, 6-1, 6-2, 6-2) in A-grade club matches. Horace
gathered his first championship 24 years ago.

9 Things tine Motorist
should Remember
1. One of your smallest expenses—lubrication—is your
chief safeguard against your heaviest expense —depre-
ciation.
2. Friction is relentless.
The effects of the incorrect oil will not be apparent
during the first piston stroke or the second. But
when piston strokes run up into millions —and that
does not take long—friction begins to get its due.
You need not look for the effect of wear then. You
can hear it.
3. We have solved the problem of scientific lubrication.
4. If given intelligent care and scientific lubrication,
your car should run better the 2000th mile than theS
first. !
5. No matter how freely a low quality or wrong-bodied
oil is used, it can never do the work of the correct,
lubricant.
6. No one oil will suit all cars. This is absolute.
7. You selected a car that suits you. Now select an oil
that suits your car.
8. Though you may want correct lubrication, you will j
seldom stumble on it by chance.
9. The Lubrication Chart, printed in part on the right, ■
was prepared only after an engineering analysis of
every car. It represents our professional advice, and
specifies the correct oil for your car.

The Country Producers' Selling Co.,
Ltd. (Sydney), a more or less co-operative
concern dealing in farm and station pro-
duce :—•
Bonus
to Con- Written
March, Loss. Dividend. signors. Off. Deficit.
1911.. £660 —— — — £660
Profit. Reserves.
1912.. 3590 5 p.c. = £984 £869 — 1077
1913.. 6975 74 „ ., 1731 2872 — 3449
1914.. 9058 8 ,, „ 2000 4237 £1000 5270
1915.. 6081 7 „ 1750 3498 500 5603
1916.. 9479 7 „ „ 1750 5295 1000 70S?
1917.. 9031 7 ,. 1750 4826 1000 8542
1918.. 8626 7 ,. ., 1750 5383 500 9535
Somehow, though it had a strikingly good
start, this co. isn’t making the progress that
it ought to, notwithstanding that it allows
(1) consignors of wool 20 p.c. off, reducing
the selling charge to 2 p.c.; (2) consignors
of fat stock 10 p.c. off, making the selling-
charge 21 p.c.; and (3) something unspeci-
fied off the selling charge on general produce,
from which concession consignors got £515
last year. The wool business keeps steady :
last year’s consignments were apparently
40,284 bales as against 41,961 bales in the
year to March, 1915. In the Ilomebush sec-
tion. however, whereas 385.918 sheep and
24.123 cattle were handled in 1914-5, only
179.692 sheep and 9265 cattle were put
through last year; and the general decline in
the yardings hardly accounts for all this
shrinkage. The country producers of N. S.
Wales still have a lot to learn regarding the
benefits of co-operation.
The co., nevertheless, continues to improve
its financial position, thanks mainly, of
course, to the war price which the xAllied
Governments have been giving for Aus-
tralian wool. In 19.14-5 the co.’s gross earn-
ings were £29,721; for the past 12 months
£39,623. But the methods of the Wool Com-
mittee are so complicated that the selling
agent finds his expenses per bale a great deal
higher than they used to be. Also, owing to
delay in settlements, this co. had liabilities
totalling (apart from the mortgage) £155,783
on March 31 last, as against £18,011 in 1915.
Still this isn’t a matter of any consequence,
as £148,999 was owing by the Common-
wealth Government as agent for the British
Government. Besides accumulating in seven
years reserves of £9535. equal to 38 p.c. of
the paid capital, the co. has written £4000 off
premises. This and some of the visible re-
serves have been employed to reduce the
mortgage on the freehold stores; the plaster
has been shrunk from £21.000 in 1912 to
£16,000 at present. Practically, therefore,
assuming that the stores are worth what
they cost, the co. has, after wiping out pre-
liminary exes, and paying its dividends and
bonuses, accumulated an amount equal
to rather more than half its capital in seven
years.
* . * * *
Here’s an Australian now at Long Lake,
Alaska:—
I'm at the Kennecott-Bonanza copper-mine, situ-
ated at the end of a 195-mile railway, which runs in
from the coast at Cordova, on Prince William Sound.
Mine and rail are owned and operated by a syndi-
cate of New York capitalists, headed by J. P. Mor-
gan and the Guggenheim brothers. The ore is
shipped over the railway to Cordova, thence by
steamer to the smelter at Tacoma (Wash.), a
distance of approximately 1800 miles. It sounds a
long way, yet the cost of producing refined Copper is
less than 5 cents per lb.—say, £23 per ton—which
I think is the world’s record at present. The
reason is the high grade of the ore, chiefly copper
glance, covelite and bornite, worth at times from
60 p.c. to 78 p.c. copper. The freight rate on the
railway, which was completed in 1911, at a cost of
26,000,000 dollars, is 40 dollars a ton, and the
passenger rate is 124 cents per mile. The co. pro-
duced during 1917 an average of 6,000,0001b. copper
per month. The temperature drops to 60 degrees
below zero at times during the winter months, but
in spite of this and the heavy snowfalls, the co. has
been able to operate the line practically all the year
round. This is a very promising country for the pro-
spector ; but owing to high freight rates and cost
of living it is practically inaccessible to any but
people with capital. ■ Also the ground is covered
with snow about eight months in the year, which
helps to make prospecting expensive.
* *■ * *
“Rigger” : The development of the Papuan
oil-fields has become an evergreen subject in
the Federal Parliament. It appears that
work has been practically stopped for a year
because plant bought in U.S.A. was sent via
Sydney to Valaila without being examined
by a Government engineer. The plant was
evidently incomplete, because certain parts
were made in Sydney and not fitted to the
American portion until both lots reached the
oilfield. It is said that important pipes
could not be screwed together because the
American thread differed from the Austra-
lian. It is no use looking for somebody to
blame, because the history of petroleum
mining in Papua is a tale of muddling from
first to last. The oil is there all right, and
of a light-gravity type suitable in the first
instance for use as fuel oil. Yet after four
years Australia has not got an oil-field.
Glynn, the Minister for Papua, says “the
Government could not allow exploitation
such as some people desired.” Surely the
Minister is capable of protecting this coun-
try’s interests fully and carefully! Private
Enterprise wants to go in and get the oil
known to be there and needed very badly
by the public. Why not limit the period
of exploitation to 20 years, insist on buy-
ing all oil produced at market price, and
charge a royalty of 10 p.c. to the people
who ra ; se the petroleum? It seems too
absurd to talk of the difficulty of “bringing
in” a flowing oil-well because of the trouble
of setting and casing in volcanic mud. There
is scarcely an oil-well in Mexico, the greatest
oil country on earth to-day. that is not bored
through volcanic mud, and the Yankee oil
engineers laugh at the idea that mere shift-
ing mud cannot be overcome. Summarised,
the position is that Papua is as good an oil
territory as Borneo, that the Government
has made a shocking mess of this as of
everv other business, and that in order to
create a domestic oil-supply it ought to per-
mit Private Enterprise to search for oil
under proper safeguards.

Frank Coffee writes to explain. In the
letter which he wrote to the Sydney Tele-
graph some months ago, and which The
Bulletin lately quoted as evidence that an
inquiry into the cost of the proposed wheat
elevators was necessary, there was an error.
He misread the report he received from the
engineer. The 3|-million-bushel elevators
he spoke of cost a million dollars; the one
at Vancouver with a capacity of 11 million
bushels cost $750,000.
Truly the Kaiser has an eye to the future.
In 1905 he presented an alleged gold cup,
said to be worth £lOOO, as a U.S. yachting
prize. It has now been melted down, and
proved to be mostly pewter and worth £7 in
all. So William bouvht £lOOO worth of
popularity from Uncle Sam, and saved £993,
with nine years’ compound interest, to help
pay for sinking Uncle Sam’s ships. The
Hohenzollerns started as money-lenders, by
the way. Brandenburg, the foundation of
their empire, came to them as a sort of for-
feited pledge.
The Mcßride case, S. Aus., has ended in
the High Court. Mcßride is a fine
old squatter, very generous to charities
and in general to his own family, and when
he went to law with his widowed daughter
over a piece of land, Judge Buchanan hinted
delicately that age (the old man is 87)
was all that was the matter with him.
Mcßride argued that he gave the daughter
merely, the use of the land; she said he had
given it her outright. The Supreme Court
found in her favor, but the persistent
father appealed, and the High Court has
decided that he has the right end of
the stick. What the Court thought of the
moral aspect can be guessed from the fact
that it refused him the costs, which must by
now be enormous, that would ordinarily go
with the judgment.

industry everywhere is becoming
keenly conscious of the economy of
the conveyor method of transporting
material.
The man-pushed wheelbarrow and
truck- and even horse haulage —are
proving too costly for short-distance
transport.
Goodyear Conveyor Belts show a far
lower transport cost per ton.
They are balanced belts.
The cover is tough and thick enough to with-
stand the abrasion of sharp and heavy materials.
The carcass is of especially woven duck, giving
maximum strength per unit of weight.
The belts are constructed so that unusual wear is
built into both carcass and cover with due regard
to the conditions under which the belt will be
used and the materials which it is to convey.
In few cases does the cover outlast the carcass or
vice versa.
If either were the case, the belt could not be an
economical one for you, because after one part
of a belt is worn out the other cannot continue
in service.
But in balanced belts such as the Goodyear the
two are accurately proportioned.
The construction experience that has made these
balanced Goodyear Conveyor Belts possible was
gained in building Goodyear Tyres.
The tread of a tyre corresponds to the conveyor cover,
its carcass to the body of the belt.
The same skill, superior materials and construction that
have made possible the mileage records of Goodyear
Tyres have made possible the low tonnage-cost records
of Goodyear Conveyors.

Eight Good Points about tbe
COLO TYRE.
(1) TEEAD.—Corrugated, presenting small surface to the road, thereby saving friction.
(2) TEEAD.—A great thickness of rubber, which is practically cut- and chip-proof.
(3 ) BREAK Kit STRIP, which, being made of loose! y-woven, heavy fabric, binds the
tread to the carcase of the tyre.
(4) PUEE EUBBEE. —Pure Eubber is placed under Breaker Strip.
(5) CAECASE.—Composed of five plies of heavy, long-fibre canvas, heavily impreg-
nated with rubber.
(6) FULL AIE SPACE.
(7) SIDE WALLS.—Eealising the severe work tyres are called upon to to do in the
country, we have placed a great thickness of rubber on the side walls, preventing
water from entering, as often happens in other makes of tyres after a few months'
service. Other makes have very little thickness of rubber at this point.
(8) BEADS are heavily reinforced and skin-coated with rubber, to prevent rust.

“Glengallan”: Australia’s old friend Hou-
dini, the handcuff expert, crops up in U.S.A.,
where he has been invited to teach the
Sammies some of his tricks. At the direction
of General Evan Johnson, Lieut. Hills, aide-
de-camp at Camp Upton, has asked the
magician for details of his offer to instruct
American soldiers and sailors how to escape
from prison manacles and torpedoed trans-
ports. Hoad ini has promised to reveal his
secrets to a class of 12, who can then put
others wise, until as escapers the U.S. army
and navy will have no living equals.
“Harrison O.”: Recently I thought I had
put forward an original idea in suggesting in
a Poverty Point par. that Alexander Watson
should recite the sixth chapter of the Book
of Job, but there is nothing new under the
limelight. American papers singe to hand
THE SHARPSHOOTERS.
“Awful, this war, isn’t it?”
“Horrible! A man can’t go to the pictures
note but he takes a risk of havin’ a
recruitin’ lecture fired at him.”
contain accounts of the production by Stuart
Walker, at the Booth Theatre, N’Yark, of a
dramatic version of the Biblical yarn. When
(he curtain rose two Narrators were re-
vealed in niches on either side of the stage,
which was kept dark until the verses dealing
with Job and Satan’s efforts to get bold of
him were ended. Then the hero took the
centre, supported by Eliphaz, Bildad and
Zophar, against a background depicting the
landscape of Uz. Job (George Gaul) sat
during most of his long speeches, but stood
up to the violent passages, and is said to
have caused a sensation by his passionate
delivery of the announcement: “I know that
my Redeemer liveth.” After the whirlwind
and storm a brilliant light was poured upon
the body of the prostrate hero, whilst the
Voice (which belonged to Australia’s esteemed
friend David Bispham) spoke its piece. The
Narrators recited the closing verses, dealing
with Job’s return to prosperity. Music was
used in moderation and incense burnt
throughout. There is no immediate prospect
o* the Firm or Bert Bailey going after the
Australian rights.
From a stage charmer of 25 years ago:—
In my young- play-acting days we wore tights and
were proclaimed “hussies” ; but oiir upper garments
were decent enough, and no girl would appear with-
out sleeves, though in a decolletage the sleeve was
merely a crescent to hide the armpit. Without being
a wowser I scarcely enjoyed the spectacle of bare
knees and thighs in the recent Williamson panto.
The unfortunate "girls were unable to “make up”
their legs with the sticky mixture known as “wet
white,” as they had a hurried change into tights
for another item, and the silk would not pull on
over the whitewash. The footlights are cruel, and
showed some knees red or else rough. A few brunettes
wanted depilating, and most of the legs looked
ridiculous nude, but excellent when cased in silken
hose. The bodices began late and ended early. This
necessitated the hideous shaven armpit-—a fashion
even copied by society dames. I predict a prickly
time for these young shavers later.
“0.K.” : The present generation knows not
“Called Back,” the novel of long ago which
produced a fortune for “Hugh Conway,” the
author, before it proved a little gold-mine
in its dramatised form. Australians of
older growth remember “Called Back” as
the drama wherein Kate Bishop (mother of
Marie Lohr) played the heroine, and George
Rignold had his great part of Macari, the
Italian spy. One is reminded of it by a
mention of the fact that Hugh Conway’s
three sons are prominent figures in the war.
The eldest is a general, the second son is
something short of an admiral, and the third
is one of the fleet-chaplains. The family
name is Fargus.-
“Fax”: Alexander Watson’s most intelli-
gent admirers are his warmest sympathisers.
Compare the life of Watson on tour with
that of the star actor who plays one charac-
ter for weeks at a stretch. He
changes liis programme every
night, and every day his brain is
at rehearsal. His waking hours
are mostly spent in recalling dif-
ferent lots of lines, each lot long
enough to last over two solid
hours of monologue. He has no
cues to prompt his memory, no
scenic items to remind him of his
words. An actor might say that
Watson has no stage business to
bear in mind, no deeds that he
must time to the tick. But a
mummer’s actions are no extra
strain on his memory, for he
mechanically identifies them with
certain words, or with certain
bursts of silence when they are
dumb show. The long run of a
piece is the star actor’s holiday.
No holidays for Watson, save on
a long sea voyage. Probably the
Shakspearean scenes are most
deeply bitten into his brain, be-
cause they required the deepest
study when he was learning them.
The late Alfred Dampier used to
interrupt his productions of lurid
melodrama with “Shakspearean
Fridays,” changing the play every
week. Walking to the theatre
on these poetic drama nights he
would quietly spout his lines for
Hamlet. Jaques or what not, and
say “Ah, one never forgets
Shakspeare!” When I men-
tioned this to another old actor as
evidence that Dampier had a “soul” he merely
remarked: “You don’t forget Shakspeare
because he is so blanky hard to memorise.”
This actor certainly had'no soul.
The Sydney soprano. Madeline Collins, has
won a prima donna’s billet with Thomas
Beecham in London, and is to voice a big
heartbreak in “Romeo and Juliet.”
Two old theatrical friends have a lot of
life in them still. One is Bert Gilbert, here
long ago with Ada Reeve on her first, or
“French Maid,” visit to Australia. He
lately played the lead at the Islington Em-
pire, London, in a musical farce produced by
himself, and of Avhicli he is also author and
in part composer. The other relic is “La
Poupee.” It still runs on tour in England,
and the “rights” are valuable enough to be
the subject of a lawsuit, the holder of them
being an enemy subject and not now on hand
to be dealt with.
“THE THREE TWINS,” AT SYDNEY
MAJESTY’S.
Dyspeptic General Field Fisher expresses
his opinion of Ned Mayland (Fred
Maguire) as a suitor for his ward.
“Cobber” : Electric corridor box-plans are
the latest innovation at the picture shows.in
Hobart. Each chair is represented by a
miniature electric-light which automatically
switches on when the seat is occupied. In-
tending patrons can thus see prior to enter-
ing the theatre exactly what places are
available.
The yarn of- an old Pointer:—
1 never see anyone else on the stage. I know they
are there by a sort of instinct. I do not even hear
them as you would hear a conversation in your
drawing-room. I know what they are saying, but
I'm hanged if I know how I know. It is certainly
not by hearing them in the ordinary sense. And I
see nobody. Even the enraptured heroine I am
holding in rny arms is not consciously visible to me.
I kiss her, and rarely miss the spot, but by what
process I score the hit I cannot understand, let
alone explain. I remember hearing George Rignold
say something to the same effect. “When I am on
the stage, in point of fact, I do not actually see
anybody else on the stage. But, then,” added
George, “neither does the audience i”
Wallace Brotvnlow is again uplifting in
public what remains of the voice in which he
poured out his passion for Nellie Stewart in
“Ma Mie Rosette.” He has been engaged by
the Fullers, and made a start at Brisbane,
winning the approval of Billo and Doreen
for the Pagliacci prologue and other ditties.
' “0.K.”: Robert Courtneidge years ago
remarked on the versatility and adroitness
of Australian stage-hands as compared with
the British sort, and now it seems that in
wheeling the folding sets of “The Masquer-
into position they have licked the best
American endeavor. This energy behind the
scenes is exciting to the audience that sits
in darkness whilst the work is going on.
The people can’t see the performance of
the miracle changes, but they can hear ’em.
There’s plenty of sound of scenery in motion
whilst the London Fog is giving place to
Chilcote’s drawing-room, or the drawing-
room is being wheeled aside to make space
for Loder’s lodgings, or vice versa. These
happenings in the darkness, averaging less
than 40 seconds each, are features of the
entertainment. Also they are strenuous
times for Post. He is one man in one cos-
tume when darkness falls on the stage, and
the other fellow in another costume when
the light is turned up again. With the aid
of a darkie dresser he accomplishes what
would be called “feats of protean art” in a
vaudeville show. In fact. Post shares in
the glory of a record-breaking interval be-
tween _ lights dd\vn and lights up. seeing that
the slightest hitch in the adjustment of his
garments means a delay in lighting up. He
is jumping out of one suit of clothes and
into another whilst the floor is moving under
him. Melbourne audiences were the more
appreciative of these lightning-change effects
because they had been chafing under a series
of dreary, time-wasting intervals in drama
played elsewhere.
“0.”: Turner, the old d.c. doorkeeper at
Her Maj.’s (Melb.), is a sick man nowadays,
paralysed on one side. Thirty years “on the
doors” at the Princess’s and Her Majesty’s
has made him a theatrical identity, just as
long service in a similar capacity at Fleming-
ton and Caulfield has familiarised him to
racegoers. The old boy is not only a store-
house of good stories, but a model of tact
and discretion.

ing and orating. In six days she persuaded
20 eligibles into khaki.

HIS OBJECT ALL SUBLIME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Maltravers is an actor grand,
Quite satisfied in his own mind
You could not in this southern land
With long and careful searching find
An artist his superior
At anything he reaches for.
When not engaged in .Melbourne he’s
Conducting touring companies.
He told us yesterday that he
Had been presented with a child.
The tenth in his fine family.
He seemed entirely reconciled.
“Ten!” Thespis said. “Your secret’s known —-
You’d raise a company of your own!”
“A company?” cried Rouge. “Have sense!
He means to breed an audience!”
VICTOIUA.

London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the E glish Press.
Simple and Effective .Methods.
By Olga Amsdell, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
Why Have Grey Hair?
Few people know that grey hair is not a
necessary feature of age—that it can be
avoided without resorting to hair dyes. A
very old, home-made remedy will turn the
hair back to a natural color in a few days.
It is only necessary to get from the chemist
two ounces of concentrate of tammalite and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply
this simple lotion to the hair for a few nights
with a small sponge and you will soon have
the pleasure of seeing the greyness disappear.
This recipe is perfectly harmless, is neither
sticky nor greasy and has given perfect satis-
faction for many generations to those in
possession of the secret.
Blackheads,. Oily Pores, etc.
The new sparkling face-bath treatment rids
the skin of blackheads, oiliness and enlarged
pores almost instantly. It is perfectly harm-
less, pleasant and immediately effective. All
you have to do is to drop a sty mol tablet,
obtained from the chemist, in a glass of hot
water, and after the resulting effervescence
lias subsided dab the affected portions of the
face freely with the liquid. When you dry
the face you will find that the blackheads
come right off on the towel, the large pores
contract and efface themselves and the greasi-
ness is all gone, leaving the skin smooth, soft
and cool. This treatment should be repeated
a few times at intervals of several davs in
order to make sure that the result slnill be
permanent.
The Magnetism of Beautiful Hair.
Beautiful hair adds immensely to the per-
sonal magnetism of both men and women.
Actresses and smart women are ever on the
look out for any harmless thing that will in-
crease the natural beauty of their hair. The
latest method is to use pure stallax as a sham-
poo on account of the peculiar glossy, fluffy
and wavy effect which it leaves. As stallax
has never been used much for this purpose it
comes to the chemist only in ilb. sealed origi-
nal packages, enough for 25 or 30 shampoos.
A teaspoonful of the fragrant stallax gran-
ules. dissolved in a cup of hot water, is more
than sufficient for each shampoo. It is very
beneficial and stimulating to the hair apart
from its beautifying effect.
How to Remove a Bad Complexion.
Cosmetics can never really help a poor com-
plexion; often they are positively harmful,
the sensible, rational way is to actually re-
move the thin veil of stifling, half-dead scarf-
skin from the face, and give the fresh, vigor-
ous and beautiful young skin underneath a
chance to show itself and to breathe.
This is best done in a very simple way, by
merely applying mercolised wax at night, like
cold cream, and washing it off in the morning.
Cood npercolized wax can be obtained from
any well-stocked chemist. It absorbs the dis-
figuring cuticle gradually and harmlessly,
leaving a brilliant natural complexion. Of
course, this also takes with it all such facial
blemises as red blotches, tan, moth patches
sallowness, liver spots, pimples, etc. As a
freckle remover and general complexion beau-
tifier this old-fashioned remedv is unequalled.
The Powder Puff Passes.
Every normal woman is always interested
111 any plan by which she can quickly enhance
her beauty, provided the plan is perfectly safe
and simple. Ever since the discovery that
ordinary cleminite, such as any chemist can
supply, is an instantaneous and harmless
beautifler, there has been- a growing demand
for it from smart women everywhere. They
usually get about an ounce and add just
enough water to dissolve it. A little of this
perfectly harmless lotion gives a beautiful
complexion to any face instantly. It renders
face powder quite unnecessary. Moreover, its
use cannot be detected. The skin is instantly
beautified but appears perfectly natural
under the closest scrutiny. [3

From Petrograd:—
The food situation in Russia has some interesting
features. While Petrograd is reduced to a daily
half-pound of alleged bread per mouth, and milk in
some quarters is unprocurable, the interior has an
exportable surplus which could relieve the awful
shortage in the big cities. Two factors, however,
operate against the cities getting a square meal. The
first is the railway disorganisation caused by the
promiscuous Bolsheviction of competent officers of
cadet or bourgeois sympathies, and deepened by the
new popular habit of annexing a train, as one
might a wheelbarrow. The second reason—and it is
vastly more important—is that the moujiks will not
sell their stocks of corn. They are partly animated
by a desire to injure the proletariat who produced
the present chaos, but their main fear is that they
will accumulate useless coin. It must be remem-;
bered that the peasantry became enriched during the
Czar’s reign by the allotments received from their
sons in the trenches; and as the few simple things
they desire are practically unobtainable, they see no
use in storing further riches on earth. One cannot
suggest to the moujik that the cash might be useful
for the future, because he would consider that reason-
ing silly. Not one in a thousand has imagination:
despite the fine work of the Zemstvos, or local coun-
cils, the bulk of the moujiks live only for the
moment—especially if there’s vodka in it.
“Burrundie : Two Territory identities went
west lately, and the rank-and-file turned out
to see them home. Arrived at the hospital,
which is also the town mortuary of Darwin,
it was found that two coffins couldn’t be
accommodated in the old cart which does
duty as hearse, so there was a procession to
the cemetery, a burial and another return,
half the mourners waiting at the graveside
while the other half returned to the hospital.
It being the wet season, the graves were
nearly full of w r ater despite desperate bailing
with one bucket. As each coffin was lowered
a battle began between the water and the
gravediggers. Eventually more holes were
bored in the coffins, and after much turning-
turtle and . poling, they sank to rest. Next
day a Government official was buried; but
this time a neat hand-pump was provided to
unwater the grave.
“Timber” has a cut:—
The troubles of the timber-millers in N. S. Wales
are trifling compared with those in Bananaland.
Royalties by the Crown are as high as 6s. 6d. per
100 feet upset, a slight increase on the Is. 6d. de-
manded by N.S.W'. Such high prices are ruling
because of bad administration on the part of the
Forests Department and manosuvring on the part of
a strong syndicate. The log pine trade is cornered
and the syndicate referred to is forcing up prices
beyond reason. It holds freehold timber country
that will not be cut out for the next 15 years, and
the Forests Department is playing into its hands.
The mills are hungering for supplies, yet are com-
pelled to work short time. The Administration,
meanwhile, allows the log trade to be cornered for
the benefit of one concern, which is making thousands
by exporting, whilst there is an embargo on Crown
timbers being sent out of the State. It would be
much better for the public if lower royalties were
fixed and shipment of logs from the State prevented,
whether from freehold or Crown lands, thus giving
more work and providing cheaper houses.
“Private Room”: The examination of vol-
unteers at the depots is too perfunctory. Two
good mates decided to enlist together, so
they went along to a metropolitan depdt and
w r ere both O.K.’d by the doctor there. Happy
in the knowledge that they would be able to
stick together, they went out to Broad-
meadows at the appointed time. At the
examination before inoculation, one was
passed and the other turned down because
of a weak heart. Not content with this, the
reject asked to be sent before the Board.
Verdict: “Won’t have you at any price!”
Still persisting hopefully, he was sent to the
highest authority. Final verdict: “You’d
crack up in a fortnight.” Now, why couldn’t
the depot doctor have told him that before
he threw up his job and divided his civi.
effects? This sort of thing certainly inflates
the recruiting figures in one way; but it
also adds to the number of men with just
grievances, and that had more to do with
killing Conscription than anything else, and
is quite as bad a stumbling-block in the way
of voluntary enlistment.
A Victorian boy tells how the Emir of
Kano took delivery of his C.M.G.: —
For a week before ■ the event great preparations
were made on the racecourse, and the Emirs of the
nine other divisions were asked to attend. On the
evening before the Day they had all arrived, with
their many thousands of followers. On the morning
the whole place was astir with horsemen, and the
old town, which is inside the walls, was soon
shrouded in dust.
The sight from the racecourse stand, was unfor-
gettable. Any 20,000 horsemen congregated in a
small place make a fine sight; but when they are in
gorgeously-colored robes and carry shining spears and
shields, the effect is wonderful. They presented
themselves as a formidable ally, these Hausa chiefs,
with their thousands of well-mounted followers.
Governor-General Lugard must have felt a proud
man as he surveyed the host that stood ready to
march past and salute him; for it was he who first
fought through and opened up this country not 16
years ago; and the Emir upon whom he was con-
ferring honor that day was one of his old foes.
During the march weird music was produced by
instruments carried by the horsemen. Such a row I
had never before heard. It appeared that the im-
pression of the followers was the more noise they
could make, the higher would their particular seriki
(king) be held in esteem by the seriki white man.
Some few of the warriors wore the chain armor of
the Crusades, brought to Kano centuries ago by the
caravans of the great desert.
The Emir of Kano possesses a jester, and it was
this man’s job to ride up and down in front of the
stand and announce the different serikis as they
passed. He was dressed in rags and had as many
tricks and grimaces as Chaplin.
The next part of the programme was the Hausa
salute, which consists of a 400-yard charge at full
gallop straight at the saluting base. When the
thousand horsemen are almost upon you the seriki
puts up his hand, and the troop stops dead. That
seriki having saluted, he and his followers turn to
the side to dismount and watch the other Emirs do
their bit. The sudden halt is done with a most
severe and cruel bit, which would stop an elephant
at the gallop ; the part in the bit comes to a point
and is about three inches long. In consequence of
this sudden pulling up, most of the horses in this
country are cow-hocked.

What is Neurasthenia?
Neurasthenia is the medical term applied
to the condition of nervous exhaustion that
is very common in modern ife. The patient
does not appear to have any definite disease,
but becomes incapable of sustained exertion.
Sometimes the sufferer is subject to hysteria.
If the patient can get away to the country
or seaside and forget all cares and worry it
will help greatly towards a cure. It will
also be necessary to take a reliable tonic—
such a one as Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
instance. These pills stimulate the appetite
and assist in the nutrition of the nerve cells.
As the nerves get their nourishment from
the blood the treatment must be directed
towards building up the blood. Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills act directly on the blood, and with
proper regulation of the diet have proved of
the greatest benefit, in many cases of neuras-
thenia. A tendency to anaemia, or biood-
lessness, shown by most neurasthenic
patients, is also corrected by these tonic
pills. Your own dealer sells Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills. Price 2s. 9d. per box, six boxes
15s. 3d.

ment of “Diseases of the Nervous System”
is contained in a useful booklet which will
be sent free to any applicant by the Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co., Box 594 G.P.0.,

London, Liverpool. Manchester, Glasgow.
Walter C. Brown, 37, late of the Northern
Territory, literally humped his swag 400
miles to get into khaki. He hoisted himself
and his burden on the excrescence of a camel
and travelled to Oodnadatta, thence by rail
to Melbourne, where he was sworn in a
week or two ago.
Jude, of the Vic. Miuing Employees’ Asso-
ciation, has been appointed secretary-rep. of
the new miners’ division of the A.W.U. It
means, of course, that he has got up, turned
round and sat down again. But it also
means that there will be more power behind
his punch—for good or otherwise.
Major L. F. Giblin, the only member of
the Speck’s Parliament to get into khaki so
far, has just scored a D.S.O. The Bul-
letin, knowing something of the very un-
usual Giblin, doesn’t doubt that lie won it
a time or two before he got it.
Sydney Water Police Court, which, as a
measure of economy, is to close at the end
of June, was established in 1850. It sat first
on the western side of Circular Quay, on
the corner of Argyle-street, but after a few
years shifted into a stone, building facing
Albert-street, and now used as the Charge
Court. The first presiding magistrate was
Henry 11. Browne.
Two .chaplains recently paragraphed in
The Bulletin —W. F. Dexter, D. 5.0., “the
Pinching Parson,” and F. W. Holland, “the
Cocoa King”—are down for Military
•Crosses. Dexter is an Anglican of 45, and
Holland a Presbyterian of 38. They are not
a bit like the padre who returned a while
ago on a crowded trooper and was counted
out by the boys because he had neglected his
job among them on the voyage.
“Melb”: Recurring to G.-G. Ferguson’s
bandaged head, through one of Von Mueller’s
pine trees falling upon him, after chopping
a la Gladstone. About half a century ago,
before Government House was thought of,
Von Mueller planted 19,000 little pines on
the hill and showed the reporters of the
Melbourne newspapers around saying: “This
will be a glorious forest in the next genera-
tion. The lovers will come here and thank
me.” Alas! the hill only grows imported
cocked hats—one at a time.
Sunday picture-shows are unknown in
Adelaide, but Port Pirie is kicking against
the recent closing of them there by the Chief
Sec. The Council of Churches got the job
done, and the Town Council says it should
have been consulted first. The chairman of
the local Liberty League told an indignation
meeting that the population had to roam the
streets, or gamble, if the movie is denied
them. “I have daughters, and while they
are at the pictures I know they are tolerably
safe; but, if there were no pictures, God
only knows what might become of them.”
Sunday pictures to buttress the Sanctity of
the Home —this is a new idea. And probably
a reasonable one. If Melbourne and Sydney
had as few social advantages and comforts
as Pt. Pirie, they would want the pubs kept
open, too.
“Seancer’s” memories of a prophetess:—
I never knew that Mrs. Rising, the late Queen of
the Melbourne clairvoyants, was a Scotchwoman until
the fact was mentioned in The Bulletin. It explains
how hard-headed David Syme came to take his fellow-
countrywoman seriously, after Alfred Deakin had
introduced him to the mystic circle. An English-
woman wouldn’t have got a hearing from David under
the same pretensions. Unbelievers who had tested
Mrs. Rising’s clairvoyanev in her younger days, and
had found her wanting, were amazed to hear of the
old man being one of hdr patrons when she was past
her prime. The grim lord of the Ace had his doots
about Mrs. Rising, of course; nevertheless he must
have paid her a lot of money for spiritual diagnosis
and advice. The late Windsor, his long-time editor,
used to say that the boss would give half his for-
tune to be convinced of the genuineness of medium-
istic claims. He wanted badly to be a sincere
believer, but his* hard connnonsense kept him back.
He certainly could have obtained convincing evidence
of Mrs. Rising’s frauduleney from many witnesses.
When traps were laid for her she fell in, maunder-
ing of dead husbands to women who had never been
married, and giving' messages from dead children to
childless Wives. When her disgusted' clients called
her an impostor she broke out into abuse and raved
about her influential supporters. Threatened with
exposure she would talk of the members of Parliament
and lights' of sassiety who would /ally to her call.
During her long life at the game she acquired a
■great mass of information about all sorts and condi-
tions of people, so she was full of tips concerning her
new customers before she took them in hand.
“Chink”: It will probably soothe “Gal-
lery Hand” (B. 23/5/’18) to know that
we in Bananaland have duly marked his
observations aneut the “provincial” nature
of Brisbane newspaper methods ; but it has
to be said that our sincerest feeling in the
matter is one of grief to find that- anyone
claiming kinship with the Inky Way could
exhibit such amiable stupidity. If our
brother of the Gallery had sufficient jour-
nalistic savvy to rise above notetaking, he
might learn that a report of a meeting can
be faithful and bright at the same time, and
that brightness does not necessarily mean
“smart” writing. The sneer at the proposal
that Premier Ryan should roll up 1000 men
for the Front, which he weeps over as
“guff,” was a fair report of an important
meeting. The announcement of-the project
was forecasted by Recruiting-Minister
Orchard as something that would “send a
thrill throughout Australia,” and any press-
man who neglected to w T rite up how that
announcement was received by several
thousand people would be well rewarded by
the order of the bag. In any case, the brick
of “provincialism” is a dangerous weapon for
Melbourne or Sydney pressmen to handle.
The little-Australian views of the news-
papers of these cities are notorious. Bris-
bane papers do not sacrifice matters of
national interest to local events, but they
manage, with a vastly greater area to cover
than their southern affinities, to pay decent
attention to the interests of the State of
Queensland. Contrariwise, I wot of several
instances in Victoria where country local-
bodies have had to appeal to the Argus and
Age for better treatment. And if “Gallery
Hand” or any other scribbler of these pride-
stuffed cities wants further enlightenment
on the point, I swear that The Bulletin
could tell ’em something interesting, particu-
larly in regard to the once-proposed Austra-
lian capital at Dalgety.


Edison BlueAmberol
Records for June
m
The June list contains an unusually comprehensive
variety of vocal and instrumental music, and it is specially
notable for its richness in fine numbers rendered by
famous Edison artists.
The Concert Records are a special treat. The “Serenade”
violin solo, by Mary Zentay (accompanied by Milan
Smolen), is exquisitely sympathetic, and “Home, Sweet
Home,” by Anna Case, is a revelation of tenderness.
All in the regular list are well up to Edison standard.
The dance music—including the three records of the
Lancers(Garde Republicaine [French] Band) and several
others —should be most popular at this season. “It’s a
Long, Long Time Since I’ve Been Home,” by Billy
Murray ; “Why I Love Him,” by Robert Clark; and “It’s
Time for Every Boy to be a Soldier,” sung by Gladys
Rice and chorus, lead the vocal items.-

A Few Bars of Australia’s Greatest
National Song', Written and Signed
by tbe Composer.
Mr. Marsh Little’s famous song, “Boys of the Dardanelles,” is known to everyone. On Anzac
Day it was played by every band in the historic procession, and the soldiers marched into the
Lord Mayor’s Luncheon at the Town Hall, Sydney, to its stirring tune. Nearly 100,000 copies of
“Boys of the Dardanelles” have been sold, easily an Australian song record.
In addition to Mr. Little’s wonderful gift as a Composer, he is regarded as one of Australia’s
most popular singers. He was born at Mud gee, N.S.W., and, wishing to benefit the many Australians
who appreciate his work, he writes to us the following valued letter.—
“It may interest yon to hear that I regard your New Discovery as being a great
asset to singers and elocutionists. When appearing at the Opera House, Auckland,
N.Z., on the Fuller Vaudeville Circuit, I was so indisposed through a severe relaxed
sore throat that I thought I should have to step out of the hill. I could only deliver
my songs (in a song scena) in whispers. . The stage-manager advised me try New
Discovery. I did, and the result was magical—my voice was quickly restored, and
I’ve been singing the praises of Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery ever since.
