Trentham—Morning.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OH, Trentham morns are misty morns;
but where the vapor thins
The hills stride in like cavaliers
with ruffle at their chins;
Throwing aside their sombre cloaks within
Night’s dolorous halls
They bend to list the tones of day—the
sweet thin bugle-calls ;
The tunes among the gleaming reeds, the
harps among the trees,
And all the musicale of mirth that peals
around their knees.
Pale scax-ves of tulle and gossamer which
twine the crouching huts,
Bwung by the kirtle of the breeze across the
rifle-butts;
Dim echoes mazed amid the mist, and little
rosy cries
Which flutter through the. atmosphere like
- scarlet butterflies ;
And rounded sounds which march across
and up the valley-zones.
Grey as the grey roofs of the huts and cool,
grey cobble-stones;
Yet shining through the folded mist I sense
the nobler part,
And hear the heart-beat of the world and
match it with my heart.
Front-faced toward the broader life and kin
to stalwart things,
The full strong torrent of the Soul bears
rich imaginings,
Flowing across the plains of Life in one
brave cataract
Washing the dross of Dream and Sham from
off the gold of Fact;
The garment of Hypocrisy, the customs man
has framed,
Fall from the naked Being, and leave it
unashamed:
For I have seen the light of Truth which
passes all surmise,
Strong on the faces of strong men and shin-
ing from strong eyes.
The paths upon the Trentham heights are
very thin and brown ;
Behind the trees, before the trees, they
loiter up and down;
Yet ever to the upmost place beyond the
dreaming wood,
They seek the reeling skyline as a strong
man seeks the Good ;
Yet bi’owner than the Trentham paths that
gird the mountain rocks,
The nut-brown fingers hotly grip the dull-
brown rifle-stocks;
And brighter than the morning star which
droops beyond the glade,
The steel-white sky is echoed from a bur-
nished bayonet blade.
Chiming upon the charmed sight a blue
mantilla slips
Softly between the clouds that lie like
wind-tossed ostrich-tips;
And Trentham Morning puts a bronzed
bugle to her mouth,
And from the east the shrill, high light goes
echoing north and south ;
The tall trees shout, the cascades sing, the
taut reeds wail and hum,
And galloping among the dew, the rowdy
day-winds, come;
While swiftly on the topmost heights the
flags of fog are furled—
The sun leaps forth like gunfire and roars
across the world!
TBENTHAM CAMP, M.L.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
When This Wonderful War
Is Over.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN it is over, and people have
time to consider the old things
that it resurrected with improve-
ments, and the new things that it produced,
and the newer things that it hasn’t produced
yet but may before the finish, they will vote
it an amazing war. This is quite apart
from the butchery, and the hypocrisy, and
the shifting of boundaries, and the Hun
habit of being a wild beast running amok,
and the making of a new hell upon earth,
and the making of a new god whose name is
William, and the creation of new ideas as
to the possibilities of running into debt, and
things like these.
The new and incredible gun which shoots
a distance of 75 miles is an item. It may
not be much in itself, but it is the beginning
of something. One reason why it shoots so
far is that the shot flies high, and gets - into
a rarefied atmosphere where friction is
greatly diminished. If it flew higher, fric-
tion would presumably be further diminished,
also the drag of the earth’s attraction would
be reduced. Then it would naturally go
farther. - Supposing it went so high that it
did part of its journey where there was no
appreciable air at all and the pull of gravi-
tation was cut down another 25 or 50 or 90
per cent., it might perhaps stay up for a
quarter of a day, and see the earth slipping
away beneath its feet, and fall at last on
Chicago or Tobolsk. Of course if it stayed
up a whole day it would end by falling, in an
exhausted condition, after a journey round
the world, on the people who discharged it,
which is a thing to be avoided. And if it
flew much higher than that it would prob-
ably become a planet and not strike any-
thing. If the owners of the biggest possible
gun didn’t realise for a month or so that
they were only making planets they would
naturally be wild.
So far as it has gone the new gun sug-
gests that the existing ideas about the
security of islands as against purely land
Powers may have to be revised.
The return of the bow, or rather the cross-
bow, begins to look possible. There is a
demand for a weapon of almost unlimited
range which will be cheaper than the almost
unlimited gun, and quieter, and more port-
able. The bow went out because it was the
last effort of the human muscle as a propel-
ler of missiles, and the original seed mus-
ket and cannon (the “crakys of war,” as the
baby cannon bound with hoops was called at
the time when it burst and blew up a Scot-
tish king) came in because they were the
beginning of pure science. The older weapon,
which had superseded the half-brick and the,
sling and the javelin, might have kept its
place if science had been applied to it. _ A
crossbow of any size you like to mention,
manipulated by enough wind or water power,
or by a sufficiently big forest-devil —the
machine which hauls down trees —with
plenty of slaves or horses at the handle
would carry a bolt of any dimensions any
distance, and .if the bolt was a tank full of
the strongest explosives it would make a
noise wiien it arrived even if it departed
silently.
The Greek fire—a flaming legend shot
through a tube —has come into its own
again. It hasn’t made so much progress as
some of the old inventions which have come
back, but then it has been lying on the shelf
for many years while other devices were
being steadily improved. It is a resnectable
institution which soothed the bedside of a
dying empire hundreds of years ago and kept
tottering Greece on its feet long after it
should have been dead by all the rules of the
game. Over and over again the Greek, when
all seemed lost, breathed this valuable trade
secret in the eye of the enemy’s fleet, and
the enemy fled, blazing and discouraged.
When the flying fire acquires a longer range
it will be a mighty business. And the Chi-
nese stinkpot has awakened again on a scale
that the most earnest Chinese stinker never
imagined. The old pot possibly had its
beginnings in the habit of throwing dead
bodies into the enemy’s well. Then the cata-
pult (which should have had a place in the
bow and arrow and early-cannon paragraph)
was used to fling decomposed horses and
such-like over the wall into the hostile town
in the hope of creating disease, and the hos-
tile town naturally flung the dreadful thing
back. Sometimes the animal spent quite a
lot of its time in the air. Whether the
radiance of phosphorescent horse flying at
night was in any way a forerunner of the
searchlight and the star-shell is at present
unknown.
The habit of throwing the late lamented
animal back and forward was a branch of
the great military art of recrimination. This
was practised in very early times, when a
champion would stalk in front of his army,
telling the opposite champion that his mother
wasn’t properly married. It is a long road
from the barbarian who told the other bar-
barian that his name was Mud to the Hun
gas apparatus, but the road is plain.
Probably, however, the modern airship is
the greatest development of the war. It was
only yesterday when the first air-Columbus
flew the Straits of Dover and the first air-
Hannibal did the bird act over the Alps only
to break his neck in Lombardy. Now Lon-
don is bombarded by sky vessels three times
a week. For the last four years the invent-
ors of clippers of the clouds haven’t had to
hunt for patrons and capitalists. The patron
and the capitalist have been waiting for them
with money and encouragement and decora-
tions. The clipper of the clouds has grown
out of all knowledge in speed, size, cheap-
ness. and weight-carrying capacity. Above
all. it has increased out of all knowledge in
safety. It only falls when an enemy shoots
it. It is safer than old-horse Dobbin on a
quiet highway, and it beats the mule for
sure-footedness. Experts hold, in all seri-
ousness, that when the war is over, and
there is time to build airships for mercantile
purposes and there are civilised places where
they can fly without having guns fired at
them, they will take up the job of common
carriers—yachts and liners and cargo drud-
ges of the places between earth and heaven.
Then the train will presently get the sack,
and the steamer will get the run, and other
vehicles, except for short distances or back-
lane work, will get the grand bounce. Also
Australia’s great ravenous, foolish, corrupt,
permanent and unproductive debt will be
called Slush.
For the airship requires no hideously ex-
pensive permanent way, with its bridges,
viaducts, tunnels, cuttings and embank-
ments. Its road requires no repairs. If
things aren’t paying for axle-grease in one
direction it gathers up its line under its arm
and goes elsewhere. It can lay its new
transcontinental route or tear up its old one
at a minute’s notice. It is a steamer to which
deeps and shallows and sand-bars are all
one, and the sea it travels requires no dredg-
ing. Being both things in one it doesn’t
tranship its goods when it changes from the
land to the ocean, or when it strikes a bor-
der where some fool or freak has ‘arranged
a break of gauge. It is the embodiment of
economy, velocity, philosophy and blank in-
difference to circumstances, and it is the
short cut incarnate. It is coining, and the
old machines are going, like superannuated
gods blown before a high wind.
And when the air-liner and the air-tramp
are really here Prime Minister Hughes will
assuredly appoint a Royal Commission of
many expensive persons to inquire (1) if we
have air in Australia; (2) if it is the same
kind of air as in other places; (3) if dis-
tances are the same here as similar distances
in other places; (4) if the ground is hard to
fall upon should the machine drop from a
height of say a mile; (5) how high the
average passenger would bounce after a drop
of that size;, and (6) the general moral,
spiritual, political, botanical, mineral, pas-
toral and horticultural aspects of the enter-
prise and its probable climatic or other in-
fluences.
The submarine has increased and ex-
panded and improved in every way, but it
can’t be said to have shed much new light
on itself except as a sneaking implement of
destruction and a wet subterfuge. It may
have a great future as a fisherman, a hunter
for lost treasure, or even as a digger for
gold, coal, silver, copper, gems, etc., at the
sea-bottom, but these matters are uncertain
Still the possibilities of the submarine as
the sinker of deep mines make a fascinating
vision. All the mineral fields on earth
wouldn’t equal in size and potential riches
the claim it could peg out, especially with a
mechanical octopus to assist it—or a num-
ber of the creature. And in addition to its
usefulness the petrol-driven octopus would
bo company, down among the dead whales’
tombs. It is a poor squid that never re-
joices, especially when the reef is going Iooz
to the ton.

The tank is, of course, the old chariot of
Assyria and Egypt with modern weapons,
modern walking fortifications and modern
driving power. If Elisha, the prophet, were
dying in his bed to-day the wail of Joash,
the king, would only be slightly varied. It
would run: “O my father, my father; the
tank of Israel and the air-planes thereof!”
The implement, is notable, not merely on its
own account, but because it is the inevitable
father of the great super-tank—the Flat-
tener of Cities—which is coming, though it
basil t yet arrived. A fight between the
super-tank and the perfected Greek fire
squirt would be worth seeing.
Curiously enough, the burning-glass has
taken no prominent place in the Great War.
This is remarkable, as the affair seems to be
really perfect—has been so for a very Ion"
time —requiring only to be made larger and
more of it. It is cheap, and it is so simple
Hi at ill-conditioned persons make a crude
lorm of it with a broken bottle, and use it
to set fire to the squatter’s grass. Properly
applied, it should serve to wither cities and
crops, and to thaw out frozen harbors, and
lo boil water, and fry chops, and ringbark
armies, and drive snakes out of holes, and
chase herds of wild animals out of forests,
and dry up rivers, and explode magazines,
and make or unmake droughts, and create
summer where no summer is due. Above all
things, the burning glass, if it was big
enough and numerous enough, could molt the
ice at the. Poles, and expose the land under-
neath which no one has yet seen. On the
ground underneath it might lie possible to
stow wheat-crops during the short fervid
summer, and the virgin soil, which has never
been married to the plough since man was
the great original baby near Babylon, might
prove rich in minerals, wild cats, promoters’
shares and debentures. Certainly the sudden
melting of the ice would cause the five
feeans to slosh round suddenly and con-
siderably. There would be a rising of the
waters which might inundate the Ninety
Mile Beach, the borders of the Gulf of Car-
pentaria, Eucla, Five Dock, Melbourne.
Texas, the Netherlands. Denmark and other
fiat places. But it is impossible to change
the traditions of old Time and ancient
Space without breaking an egg or two.
Speaking of Time, the Great War will
probably be the driving force required to
start man on a new branch of inquiry. He
has learned to travel upward, but it was
only vesterday that he learned to travel
upward very high. He has learned to travel
downward, but it is very, very recently that
he learned how to travel downward to any
great distance, and the art is still in its
infancy. The boring of the Complete Hole,
so that any human being can fall right
through the earth by gravitation and cross
the globe per medium of his own weight,
is still to come, like the upward movement
that will send him to other worlds. And the
first primitive rudimentary machine for
carrying passengers backwards and forwards
along the roads of Time hasn’t been com-
menced yet. It exists in Fiction, hut even
the keel hasn’t been laid in Fact. Yet it
will come because it is wanted ; how badly it
is wanted this war has shown. Never
before, in historical times, were there so
many refugees and so few vacant places to
refuge in. When the requisite implement is
devised, hordes of refugees will take passage
by it, and instead of going across the borders
of land or sea to another country they will
speed across tire borders of the centuries to
another millennium. The discontented and
the dispossessed and the disgruntled and the
overcrowded will get into the up-to-date
travelling apparatus, each with a gun and
an axe and a sandwich and a huge bag of
cartridges, and try their luck. So Julius
Caesar may find a horde of Australian
strangers clearing the British forests for
settlement, and receive the surprise of his
life. Or William the Norman may get his
head blown off and his whole army gassed,
thereby obliterating in advance the Plan-
tagenets, and the Tudors, and the Stuarts,
and the Hanoverians, and wiping up George
Y.’s claim to the throne, and that king
himself and all his relations. And other
refugees will drive forward into the Future,
and come back with strange, undreamt-of
weapons, which will' blow the innnards out
of the Present. And To-morrow coming back
will meet Yesterday coming forward, and
they will fight like fiends on top of To-day,
and To-day will have a devil of a time, and
will fly for its life either into- extreme
Futurity or into the oldest kind of Antiq-
uity. Then no one will write history, for
when history ceases to be fixed, and becomes
a fluid thing, washing to and fro and here
and there, and Darius may cease to be
because he never was, through Billjim get-
ting there ahead of him, and Alexander the
Great may be supplanted before he was born
by somebody who wasn’t born till 2500 years
after he was dead, all matters will be too
unsettled and unfixed for print. It will then
be a poor country which hasn't 157 authen-
tic beginnings, and 2ft distinct civilisations
and 100 dynasties, republics, anarchies and
revolutions reigning at once all the time.
There are also possibilities about a lever
which will upset cities: and about an auto-
matic king, so that William Hohenzollern,
when lie is dethroned and sent back into the
glacial period, and left there, may be
succeeded by a Hydraulic Hypocrite, or an
Electric Pecksniff, or a Steam Brute. But
these proposals are too wild to call for con-
sideration.
JAMES EDMOND.
Makin' a Home.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ALTHOUGH the day was sunless, a
hot, parching wind from the North-
West seemed to sap the life and
energy from every living thing; a haze from
some far-off bushfire changed distant objects
into ghostly unrealities ; the usual voices of
the bush were hushed and all nature
appeared to be sunk in listless apathy.
A day on which each movement bathed the
body in perspiration and every breath seemed
to stifle, work became a burden; yet out in
the farthest paddock Lizzie Morris was help-
ing with the stumping. Her skirt was
bunched up around her waist, showing heavy
hobnailed boots and a coarse petticoat made
from flourbags; on her head was a maws
peaked cap, from under which her hair
escaped in wisps that clung damply to her
perspiring face.
Her father, a lanky, dour-looking man, was
yoking up a couple of bullocks, preparatory
to hauling out the stump that he and Sam
had been nearly all the morning excavating
and chopping clear of its long radiating roots.
“All clear there?” called Morris.
“Right-o,” replied Sam, scrambling out of
the hole and leaning on the axe, while allow-
ing his eyes to wander to where Lizzie was
dragging loose roots out of the broken soil
and piling them in heaps ready for firing
“Don’t go ter sleep there! Git that pole
and put one end under the stump an’ be
ready t’ lever ’er up when I say the word.”
But Sam was still gazing towards the
distant Lizzie.
“Y’ know, boss, I reckon that there work’s
too stiff for a girl; it’s terrible hard on the
back.”
Morris snorted. “Hard on the back, eh!
What was her back made for, I like ter know.
An’ what the devil has it got t’ do with you,
anyway ?”
“Oh, nothin’. On’y I wus just thinkin’,
that’s all.”
“Well, think about what y' doin’, an’ per-
haps yer’ll do it right. That ain’t the way
ter use a lever; git: a log an’ put under it
just on the edge of the hole.”
“Course, it's got nothin’ ter do with me,”
Sam continued, “but I reckon a girl oughten
ter graft like a man ; they ain’t built that
way. An’ it don't give ’em a chance ter be
any good at things they ougbter know, like
cookin’ or sewin’.”
“Perhaps yer think I oughter buy ’er a
pi a liner and ’ave ’er taught paintin’! Now,
shut yer mouth an’ git busy. Are yer ready
there?”
“Wait a bit; now !”
“Hey, Darky ! Hey, Poley ! Git there,
blarst yer hides!”
The bullocks, after the manner of their
kind, were slow to move, and there was much
swishing of tails and shaking of heads before
the trace-chains tightened : then in their pon-
derous way they swung first to one side and
then to the other, while Morris shouted
profanity.
As though loath to leave the spot where it
had reigned so long, the remains of the great
tree taxed the strength of the bullocks and
Morris’s vocabulary to their utmost before it
consented to move. Then with some of its
still intact roots like giant feelers, the squat
stump resembled some monstrous spider as it
climbed slowly out of the hole.
“Phew ! that’s that cow settled. Gawd, it's
hot.” Morris produced a large and glaring
bandana handkerchief, which he proceeded to
use much as one would use a bath-towel.
After a glance at the grey sky as though in
search of the sun, he continued : “I dunno,
but something seems to tell me it’s time fer
tucker.”
lie cupped his hands in front of his mouth
and shouted, “Hey, Lizzie.”
There was no response, so he picked up a
piece of bark that was lying near, rolled it
up roughly in the shape of a funnel and tried
again.
“Hey, Liz-zie-e-e.”
The distant girl rose from her task and
straightened her back.
“It's time to boil the billy.”
Lizzie turned away with a wave of the
hand to indicate that she understood, while
the men finished their job of casting loose
the chains from the conquered stump.
The sultry, oppressive atmosphere dulled
desire for food, so the meal of the three con-
sisted mostly of steaming pannikins of tea.
In the intervals of sipping her tea Lizzie
was absorbed in extracting a splinter from
her palm with the point of a pin. Every
now and then she would draw in her breath
sharply with a siffling sound that caused
Sam’s lips to set each time in sympathy.
“Thank goodness, that's out!” exclaimed
Lizzie at last. “The darn thing has been
worrying me all the morn in'. See yer got
the big feller out all right. I hope yer didn’t
leave many of them stragglin’ roots fer me ter
grub out; I’m sick of luggin’ at the rotten
things.”
“Y’ make a devil of a fuss over a bit of
work,” remarked her father, as he walked
off. “Come on, you.”
But Sam lingered uncertainly, watching
Lizzie’s every movement with a round-eyed
stare, until, with his great fiorid face redder
than usual, he blurted:
“Y’ know. Liz, I think a lot of you, an’ T
don’t like t’ see you toilin’ like a. road-
mender. I wus wonderin’ —that is—yer see
—er—if we wus ter git married. I been
savin’ up all the time I've worked fer yer
old man, an’—er—what about it?”
Lizzie examined carefully the place on her
hand where the splinter had Deem
“Oh, I dunno. 1 s’pose we might ’s
well.”
The brief break in the monotony of their
lives occasioned by the wedding was over,
and Sam and Lizzie were settled.
Sam was busy digging post-holes, and from
the kitchen came the clatter of - dishes.
Presently Sam made his way to the kitchen
door.
“Ain’t yer finished washiu’ up yet? Shake
it up an’ come an’ gimme a hand.”
“But. Sam, I got t’ bake to-day.”
“Well, yer can do that to-night, carn’t
yer? I want yer to take the adze an’ shape
up some more posts fer the bail.”

“Nerves”
Our nerves are similar to an intri-
cate network of telegraph wires.
Controlled and nourished by a
portion of the brain —known as
the nerve centres —the delicate
threadlike nerves radiate in all
directions throughout the body.
So long as the nerve centres are
capable of continually supplying
nourishment to the nerves, the
nerves will remain strong and
healthy. But directly the nerve
centres become weakened by
overwork, worry or anxiety, they
are unable to transmit the
necessary nourishment, and the
nerves become worn out and
“on edge.” Then it is that a sud-
den sound makes you “ jump ”

from neuralgia—you are rest-
less and depressed. In this con-
dition there is nothing to equal
